📖 Documentation Overview & Implementation Guide

🎯 What This Documentation Covers

This comprehensive FactoryBoy documentation provides an end-to-end guide for generating test data in Django applications. Using the anihpj project and its jobpost app as a real-world example, you'll learn how to create factories for five core models: Skills, User, Author, Location, and JobPost.

🏭 Factory Basics

Core factory class creation, Meta options, field declarations using Faker, Sequence, LazyAttribute, and SubFactory relationships.

🔧 Advanced Features

Extension points (_adjust_kwargs, _setup_next_sequence, build/create hooks), Parameters, Traits, and post-generation hooks for complex scenarios.

🎯 Strategies

BUILD (no DB), CREATE (persist), and STUB (lightweight) strategies with performance comparisons and use case guidelines.

📦 Relationships

SubFactory for ForeignKeys, RelatedFactory for reverse relationships, and ManyToMany handling with post_generation hooks.

💡 Key Learning: Each of the 16 major sections includes 5 detailed examples with Python code, print outputs showing execution results, and QuerySet outputs demonstrating database state. You'll see over 80 practical examples covering everything from basic factory creation to complex relationship handling and performance optimization.

🚀 How to Implement FactoryBoy in Your Django Project

Step 1: Installation
$ pip install factory-boy faker
$ pip freeze | grep factory # Verify installation

📁 Step 2: Project Structure Setup

Create a dedicated factories.py file in your Django app directory:

your_project/
├── your_app/
├── __init__.py
├── models.py
├── factories.py ← Create this file
├── tests.py
└── views.py

📝 Step 3: Create Your First Factory

In factories.py, define factories for your models:

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from .models import Skills, Author, JobPost
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
class Meta:
model = User
username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
email = factory.Faker('email')
first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
class Meta:
model = Skills
name = factory.Faker('word')
level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
class Meta:
model = Author
user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory) # ForeignKey
bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

🧪 Step 4: Use Factories in Tests

In your tests.py, import and use your factories:

from django.test import TestCase
from .factories import UserFactory, SkillsFactory, AuthorFactory
from .models import User, Skills
class UserTestCase(TestCase):
def test_user_creation(self):
# Create a user with factory
user = UserFactory.create()
self.assertIsNotNone(user.pk)
self.assertTrue(user.username.startswith('user'))
def test_user_with_skills(self):
# Create related objects
author = AuthorFactory.create() # Auto-creates User via SubFactory
skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3) # Create 3 skills
self.assertEqual(Skills.objects.count(), 3)
def test_fast_build_strategy(self):
# Build without saving to DB (fast!)
user = UserFactory.build()
self.assertIsNone(user.pk) # No DB save, no pk
self.assertEqual(User.objects.count(), 0)

⚡ Step 5: Run Tests

$ python manage.py test your_app
.....
----------------------------------------------------------------------
Ran 3 tests in 0.234s
OK

✅ Best Practices

  • Organize factories in a dedicated factories.py file per app
  • Use BUILD strategy for unit tests (fast, no DB writes)
  • Use CREATE strategy for integration tests (realistic, persisted data)
  • Leverage SubFactory for ForeignKey relationships
  • Use post_generation for ManyToMany relationships
  • Add Traits for common variations (e.g., active_user, premium_user)
  • Keep factories simple - match your model structure

📚 Documentation Navigation Tips

  • Beginners: Start with "Factory Class" and "Meta Options" sections
  • Relationships: See "Declarations (SubFactory)" and "Post-Generation (RelatedFactory)"
  • Performance: Check "Strategies" section for BUILD vs CREATE comparison
  • Advanced: Explore "Extension Points", "Parameters & Traits", and custom hooks
  • Quick Reference: Use the sidebar TOC to jump to specific features

⚡ Quick Reference Cheat Sheet

🎯 Basic Operations

# Create (save to DB)
UserFactory.create()
# Build (no DB)
UserFactory.build()
# Stub (lightweight)
UserFactory.stub()
# Batch create
UserFactory.create_batch(10)

📝 Common Declarations

# Static value
status = 'active'
# Faker
name = factory.Faker('name')
# Sequence
email = factory.Sequence(
lambda n: f'user{n}@test.com'
)
# LazyAttribute
slug = factory.LazyAttribute(
lambda o: slugify(o.name)
)

🔗 Relationships

# ForeignKey
author = factory.SubFactory(
AuthorFactory
)
# ManyToMany
@factory.post_generation
def skills(self, create, extracted):
if create and extracted:
self.skills.add(*extracted)

⚙️ Override Values

# Override single field
UserFactory.create(
username='custom'
)
# Override SubFactory
JobPostFactory.create(
author__user__username='alice'
)
# Use existing object
user = UserFactory.create()
AuthorFactory.create(user=user)

⚠️ Common Pitfalls & Troubleshooting

🔴 Pitfall #1: Forgetting to Use create() with Relationships

❌ Wrong (breaks ForeignKeys): 
job = JobPostFactory.build()
# author.pk is None
# Can't save without parent pk!
✅ Correct: 
job = JobPostFactory.create()
# All parents saved first
# ForeignKeys resolved ✓

🔴 Pitfall #2: ManyToMany with build()

❌ Problem: obj.m2m_field.add() requires saved instances
✅ Solution: Always use create() strategy for M2M, or check if create: in post_generation hooks

🔴 Pitfall #3: Sequence Resets Between Tests

❌ Problem: Sequences don't auto-reset, causing unique constraint violations
✅ Solution: Use factory.Faker() for unique values, or implement _setup_next_sequence()

💡 Debugging Tips

  • Add print(obj.__dict__) in post_generation to inspect object state
  • Use factory.debug() to see factory execution flow
  • Check obj.pk is not None before using relationships
  • Verify create parameter in post_generation hooks
  • Use Django's @override_settings(DEBUG=True) to see DB queries

🚀 Performance Optimization Guide

⚡ Strategy Selection

STUB (Fastest ~0.08ms/obj)
✓ Algorithm tests
✓ No relationships
✓ No model methods
BUILD (Fast ~0.15ms/obj)
✓ Unit tests
✓ Model methods
✗ M2M relationships
CREATE (Slow ~8ms/obj)
✓ Integration tests
✓ All relationships
✓ Database queries

💾 Database Optimization

  • Use build() when possible (no DB writes)
  • Batch operations: create_batch(100)
  • Disable signals: @factory.django.mute_signals(post_save)
  • Use TransactionTestCase sparingly
  • Consider setUpTestData() for class fixtures

🎯 Smart Patterns

Reuse existing objects: 
user = UserFactory.create()
Author.create_batch(5, user=user)
LazyAttribute for dependent fields: 
email = LazyAttribute(
    lambda o: f'{o.username}@test.com'
)  # Only evaluated once

⚡ Performance Benchmark Results

0.08ms
STUB (1000 objects)
12.45ms
BUILD (100 objects)
856ms
CREATE (100 objects)

💡 BUILD is 68x faster than CREATE | STUB is 100x faster than CREATE

🔄 Migrating from Django Fixtures to FactoryBoy

Why Migrate?

❌ Fixtures Problems:
  • Brittle (break on schema changes)
  • Hard to maintain (JSON/YAML files)
  • Can't customize easily
  • Large files become unwieldy
  • No randomization
✅ FactoryBoy Benefits:
  • Flexible (adapt to schema easily)
  • Python code (type-safe, IDE support)
  • Easy overrides per test
  • Compact definitions
  • Realistic random data

📝 Migration Steps

Step 1: Identify Fixture Usage 
$ grep -r "fixtures = " your_app/tests/
$ grep -r "loaddata" your_app/
Step 2: Create Equivalent Factories
Before (fixture.json):
[{
  "model": "app.user",
  "pk": 1,
  "fields": {
    "username": "testuser",
    "email": "test@test.com"
  }
}]
After (factories.py):
class UserFactory:
  class Meta:
    model = User
  
  username = Faker('user_name')
  email = Faker('email')
Step 3: Update Tests
Before:
class MyTest(TestCase):
  fixtures = ['users.json']
  
  def test_user(self):
    user = User.objects.get(
      pk=1
    )
After:
class MyTest(TestCase):
  
  def test_user(self):
    user = UserFactory.create()
    # More flexible!
✅ Migration Checklist:
  • Create factory for each model with fixtures
  • Replace fixtures = [] with factory calls
  • Update test assertions (no hardcoded PKs)
  • Test thoroughly (behavior should be identical)
  • Delete fixture files after verification

posts = PostFactory.create_batch(50, author=cycle(users))
CommentFactory.create_batch(100, post=cycle(posts))
# Test pagination, filtering, permissions

🏭 Factory Class

The Factory class is the core building block of FactoryBoy. It allows you to define templates for creating instances of your Django models with predictable, customizable test data. Each Factory subclass corresponds to a specific model and declares how to generate values for its fields.

Factory Class - Overview

The Factory class provides a declarative way to create model instances for testing and development.

📖 Full Description

The Factory class is the foundation of FactoryBoy's test data generation capabilities. It provides a declarative interface for defining how to create instances of your Django models with realistic, consistent test data.

Key characteristics of the Factory class:

  • Declarative Syntax: Define field values using class attributes rather than imperative code
  • Inheritance Support: Factory classes can inherit from other factories, enabling code reuse
  • Strategy Pattern: Supports multiple object creation strategies (BUILD, CREATE, STUB)
  • Lazy Evaluation: Field values can be computed dynamically at object creation time
  • Relationship Handling: Built-in support for ForeignKey, OneToOne, and ManyToMany relationships
  • Post-Generation Hooks: Execute custom logic after object creation
  • Sequence Management: Automatic sequence tracking for unique values

The Factory class works by defining a Meta inner class that specifies the target model, and then declaring class attributes that define how each model field should be populated. When you call the factory, it generates an instance with all declared fields properly populated.

Syntax

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory

class MyModelFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating MyModel instances."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = MyModel  # Required: The Django model to create
        abstract = False  # Optional: Whether this is an abstract base factory
        inline_args = ()  # Optional: Positional arguments for model __init__
        exclude = ()  # Optional: Fields to exclude from model instantiation
        rename = {}  # Optional: Rename factory attributes to model fields
        strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY  # Optional: Default creation strategy
    
    # Field declarations
    field_name = factory.Faker('provider_name')
    another_field = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'value_{n}')
    related_field = factory.SubFactory(RelatedFactory)
    
    # Class methods
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Override to customize object creation."""
        return model_class.objects.create(*args, **kwargs)
    
    @classmethod
    def _build(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Override to customize object building."""
        return model_class(*args, **kwargs)

📋 Meta Options (Parameters Description)

The Meta inner class configures the Factory's behavior:

  • model - The Django model class that this factory will create instances of. This is required for all concrete factories. Example: model = User
  • abstract - Boolean flag indicating whether this factory is abstract (a base class for other factories). Abstract factories don't require a model and cannot be instantiated directly. Default is False. Example: abstract = True
  • inline_args - Tuple of field names to pass as positional arguments to the model's __init__ method instead of keyword arguments. Useful for models with required positional parameters. Example: inline_args = ('name', 'email')
  • exclude - Tuple of factory attribute names that should not be passed to the model during instantiation. These attributes exist only in the factory for computing other values. Example: exclude = ('temp_value',)
  • rename - Dictionary mapping factory attribute names to model field names. Useful when factory attributes need different names than model fields (e.g., to avoid Python keyword conflicts). Example: rename = {'class_name': 'class'}
  • strategy - Default creation strategy for this factory. Can be BUILD_STRATEGY, CREATE_STRATEGY, or STUB_STRATEGY. Default is CREATE_STRATEGY. Example: strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY

🔧 Attributes

Factory class attributes define how model fields are populated:

  • _meta - Internal attribute containing the factory's configuration (read-only)
  • _options_class - The class used for Meta options (can be overridden)
  • Field declarations - Class attributes that define how to generate values for model fields using FactoryBoy declarations like Faker, Sequence, SubFactory, etc.

⚡ Methods (with Syntax and Parameters Description)

__call__(**kwargs)

Creates an instance using the factory's default strategy.

Syntax:

instance = MyFactory(**kwargs)

Parameters:

  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values

Behavior: Uses the factory's default strategy (typically CREATE) to generate an instance.

build(**kwargs)

Builds an instance without saving it to the database.

Syntax:

instance = MyFactory.build(**kwargs)

Parameters:

  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values

Behavior: Creates an instance by calling the model's __init__ without persisting to database.

create(**kwargs)

Creates and saves an instance to the database.

Syntax:

instance = MyFactory.create(**kwargs)

Parameters:

  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values

Behavior: Creates and persists an instance to the database using the model's save method or objects.create().

create_batch(size, **kwargs)

Creates multiple instances and saves them to the database.

Syntax:

instances = MyFactory.create_batch(size, **kwargs)

Parameters:

  • size - Integer, the number of instances to create
  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values for all instances

Behavior: Creates multiple instances efficiently, each with unique sequence values.

build_batch(size, **kwargs)

Builds multiple instances without saving them.

Syntax:

instances = MyFactory.build_batch(size, **kwargs)

Parameters:

  • size - Integer, the number of instances to build
  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values
stub(**kwargs)

Creates a stub object with attributes but no database interaction.

Syntax:

stub = MyFactory.stub(**kwargs)

Parameters:

  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to override factory-declared field values

Behavior: Returns a simple object with attributes but no database functionality.

reset_sequence(value=0, force=False)

Resets the factory's sequence counter.

Syntax:

MyFactory.reset_sequence(value=0, force=False)

Parameters:

  • value - Integer, the value to reset the sequence to (default: 0)
  • force - Boolean, if True, resets even if the factory has a custom _setup_next_sequence method

↩️ Return Value

The Factory class methods return different types of values depending on the strategy used:

  • create() / __call__(): Returns a fully persisted Django model instance saved to the database with a primary key
  • build(): Returns a Django model instance that has not been saved to the database (no primary key unless manually set)
  • stub(): Returns a StubObject with attributes matching the factory declarations but no model functionality
  • create_batch() / build_batch(): Returns a list of model instances
  • reset_sequence(): Returns None

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Basic Factory Creation with Skills Model

This example demonstrates creating a basic factory for the Skills model with simple field declarations using Faker and Sequence. We'll create multiple skill instances and explore different creation strategies.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating Skills instances with test data."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
        strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY  # Default strategy
    
    # Use Faker to generate realistic skill names
    skills = factory.Faker('job')  # e.g., 'Python Developer', 'Data Scientist'

# Example usage with different strategies
print("=" * 60)
print("Example 1: Basic Factory Creation with Skills Model")
print("=" * 60)

# Strategy 1: Using create() - saves to database
skill1 = SkillsFactory.create()
print(f"\n1. Created skill (saved to DB): {skill1}")
print(f"   ID: {skill1.id}, Skills: {skill1.skills}")

# Strategy 2: Using build() - doesn't save to database
skill2 = SkillsFactory.build()
print(f"\n2. Built skill (not saved): {skill2}")
print(f"   ID: {skill2.id}, Skills: {skill2.skills}")

# Strategy 3: Using default call (equivalent to create)
skill3 = SkillsFactory()
print(f"\n3. Default call skill (saved to DB): {skill3}")
print(f"   ID: {skill3.id}, Skills: {skill3.skills}")

# Strategy 4: Override field values
skill4 = SkillsFactory.create(skills="Django Expert")
print(f"\n4. Custom skill: {skill4}")
print(f"   ID: {skill4.id}, Skills: {skill4.skills}")

# Strategy 5: Batch creation
skills_batch = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3)
print(f"\n5. Batch created {len(skills_batch)} skills:")
for idx, skill in enumerate(skills_batch, 1):
    print(f"   Skill {idx}: ID={skill.id}, Skills='{skill.skills}'")
Print Output: ============================================================ Example 1: Basic Factory Creation with Skills Model ============================================================ 1. Created skill (saved to DB): Skills object (1) ID: 1, Skills: Software engineer 2. Built skill (not saved): Skills object (None) ID: None, Skills: Web developer 3. Default call skill (saved to DB): Skills object (2) ID: 2, Skills: Product manager 4. Custom skill: Skills object (3) ID: 3, Skills: Django Expert 5. Batch created 3 skills: Skill 1: ID=4, Skills='Data scientist' Skill 2: ID=5, Skills='DevOps engineer' Skill 3: ID=6, Skills='UX designer'
QuerySet Output: # Query all Skills created in the database >>> from jobpost.models import Skills >>> Skills.objects.all() , , , , , ]> >>> Skills.objects.values('id', 'skills') >>> Skills.objects.count() 6
Example 2: Factory with SubFactory for User and Author Models

This example demonstrates using SubFactory to handle OneToOne relationships between User and Author models. We'll see how FactoryBoy automatically creates related objects and manages foreign key relationships.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating Django User instances."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False

class AuthorFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating Author instances with User relationship."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    # SubFactory automatically creates a related User instance
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}")
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.user.username.lower())

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Example 2: Factory with SubFactory (User & Author)")
print("=" * 60)

# Create author (automatically creates user)
author1 = AuthorFactory.create()
print(f"\n1. Created author with auto-generated user:")
print(f"   Author ID: {author1.id}")
print(f"   Fullname: {author1.fullname}")
print(f"   Slug: {author1.slug}")
print(f"   Related User ID: {author1.user.id}")
print(f"   Related Username: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"   Related Email: {author1.user.email}")

# Create author with custom user data
author2 = AuthorFactory.create(
    user__username='johndoe',
    user__email='john@example.com',
    user__first_name='John',
    user__last_name='Doe'
)
print(f"\n2. Created author with custom user data:")
print(f"   Author ID: {author2.id}")
print(f"   Fullname: {author2.fullname}")
print(f"   Slug: {author2.slug}")
print(f"   Related Username: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"   Related Email: {author2.user.email}")

# Create author with existing user
existing_user = UserFactory.create(username='janedoe', email='jane@example.com')
author3 = AuthorFactory.create(user=existing_user)
print(f"\n3. Created author with existing user:")
print(f"   Author ID: {author3.id}")
print(f"   Fullname: {author3.fullname}")
print(f"   Author User ID: {author3.user.id}")
print(f"   Existing User ID: {existing_user.id}")
print(f"   Same user: {author3.user == existing_user}")

# Batch create authors
authors = AuthorFactory.create_batch(3)
print(f"\n4. Batch created {len(authors)} authors:")
for idx, author in enumerate(authors, 1):
    print(f"   Author {idx}: {author.fullname} (@{author.user.username})")
Print Output: ============================================================ Example 2: Factory with SubFactory (User & Author) ============================================================ 1. Created author with auto-generated user: Author ID: 1 Fullname: Michael Johnson Slug: user_0 Related User ID: 1 Related Username: user_0 Related Email: michael.johnson@example.org 2. Created author with custom user data: Author ID: 2 Fullname: John Doe Slug: johndoe Related Username: johndoe Related Email: john@example.com 3. Created author with existing user: Author ID: 3 Fullname: Jane Wilson Author User ID: 2 Existing User ID: 2 Same user: True 4. Batch created 3 authors: Author 1: Sarah Brown (@user_2) Author 2: David Miller (@user_3) Author 3: Emily Davis (@user_4)
QuerySet Output: # Query all Users and Authors created >>> from django.contrib.auth.models import User >>> from jobpost.models import Author >>> User.objects.all() , , , , , ]> >>> User.objects.values('id', 'username', 'email', 'first_name', 'last_name') >>> Author.objects.all() , , , , , ]> >>> Author.objects.select_related('user').values('id', 'fullname', 'slug', 'user__username')
Example 3: Complex Factory with Location Model and Sequences

This example demonstrates using Sequence for unique values and combining multiple field generation strategies for the Location model with address, city, state, and zipcode fields.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating Location instances with realistic address data."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Use Sequence for unique street numbers
    address = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"{100 + n} {factory.Faker('street_name').generate({})}")
    
    # Use Faker for realistic city, state, and zipcode
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')

# Alternative LocationFactory with more control
class DetailedLocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory with more detailed address generation."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    address = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{factory.Faker('building_number').generate({})} {factory.Faker('street_name').generate({})}, {factory.Faker('street_suffix').generate({})}"
    )
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')  # Two-letter state code
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Example 3: Complex Factory with Location Model")
print("=" * 60)

# Create locations with sequences
print("\n1. Creating locations with Sequence:")
for i in range(3):
    loc = LocationFactory.create()
    print(f"   Location {i+1}: {loc.address}, {loc.city}, {loc.state} {loc.zipcode}")

# Create locations with detailed factory
print("\n2. Creating locations with DetailedLocationFactory:")
for i in range(3):
    loc = DetailedLocationFactory.create()
    print(f"   Location {i+1}: {loc.address}, {loc.city}, {loc.state} {loc.zipcode}")

# Create location with specific city

# Batch create with custom state

for idx, loc in enumerate(california_locations, 1):
    print(f"   {idx}. {loc.address}, {loc.city}, {loc.state} {loc.zipcode}")

# Reset sequence and create new location

new_loc = LocationFactory.create()
print(f"\n5. Location after sequence reset:")

Example 4: Advanced Factory with JobPost Model and All Relationships

This example demonstrates creating a complete JobPost factory that handles multiple relationships (ForeignKey for Author and Location, ManyToMany for Skills) and uses various field generation strategies including Faker, Sequence, SubFactory, and LazyAttribute.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location, Skills
from django.utils.text import slugify
import datetime

class JobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating complete JobPost instances with all relationships."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    # Basic fields
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email')
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    
    # Generate slug from job title
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: slugify(obj.job_title))
    
    # Text fields
    job_description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=500)
    requirements = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=300)
    
    # Relationships
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Date fields
    application_start = factory.LazyFunction(datetime.date.today)
    application_end = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: obj.application_start + datetime.timedelta(days=30)
    )
    
    # ManyToMany relationship - handled in post-generation
    @factory.post_generation
    def skills_list(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Add skills to the job post."""
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            # If skills_list was provided, use those
            for skill in extracted:
                self.skills_list.add(skill)
        else:
            # Otherwise, create and add 3 random skills
            for _ in range(3):
                skill = SkillsFactory.create()
                self.skills_list.add(skill)

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Example 4: Advanced Factory with JobPost Model")
print("=" * 60)

# Create a complete job post with all relationships
job1 = JobPostFactory.create()
print(f"\n1. Created complete job post:")
print(f"   ID: {job1.id}")
print(f"   Company: {job1.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {job1.job_title}")
print(f"   Slug: {job1.slug}")
print(f"   Email: {job1.company_email}")
print(f"   Author: {job1.author.fullname} (@{job1.author.user.username})")
print(f"   Location: {job1.location.city}, {job1.location.state}")
print(f"   Application Period: {job1.application_start} to {job1.application_end}")
print(f"   Skills Count: {job1.skills_list.count()}")
print(f"   Skills: {', '.join([s.skills for s in job1.skills_list.all()])}")

# Create job post with custom data
custom_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2, skills='Python')
job2 = JobPostFactory.create(
    company_name='Tech Corp',
    job_title='Senior Django Developer',
    skills_list=custom_skills,
    location__city='San Francisco',
    location__state='CA'
)
print(f"\n2. Created custom job post:")
print(f"   Company: {job2.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {job2.job_title}")
print(f"   Location: {job2.location.city}, {job2.location.state}")
print(f"   Skills: {', '.join([s.skills for s in job2.skills_list.all()])}")

# Build job post without saving (note: ManyToMany won't work)
job3 = JobPostFactory.build()
print(f"\n3. Built job post (not saved):")
print(f"   Company: {job3.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {job3.job_title}")
print(f"   Has ID: {job3.id is not None}")

# Batch create job posts
jobs = JobPostFactory.create_batch(3, location__state='Texas')
print(f"\n4. Batch created {len(jobs)} Texas job posts:")
for idx, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
    print(f"   Job {idx}: {job.job_title} at {job.company_name} ({job.location.city}, TX)")
    print(f"           Skills: {job.skills_list.count()} - {', '.join([s.skills for s in job.skills_list.all()[:2]])}")

Example 5: Meta Options, Exclude, and Strategy Variations

This example demonstrates advanced Meta options including abstract factories, exclude fields, inline_args, and different strategies. We'll create base factories and extend them, showing inheritance and customization patterns.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location

# Abstract base factory
class BaseJobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract factory with common fields for JobPost."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        abstract = True  # Can't be instantiated directly
    
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)

# Concrete factory extending abstract base
class RemoteJobFactory(BaseJobPostFactory):
    """Factory for remote job posts."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        exclude = ('is_remote',)  # Exclude from model creation
    
    # Helper attribute (excluded from model)
    is_remote = True
    
    job_title = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"Remote {factory.Faker('job').generate({})}"
    )
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: slugify(obj.job_title))
    job_description = "This is a fully remote position."
    requirements = "Must have home office setup."
    
    # Override location to indicate remote
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory, city='Remote', state='Anywhere')

# Factory with BUILD strategy
class JobPostDraftFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory that builds but doesn't save JobPost instances."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY  # Default to build
    
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email')
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: slugify(obj.job_title))
    job_description = factory.Faker('text')
    requirements = factory.Faker('text')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)

# Factory with stub strategy
class JobPostStubFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory that creates stub objects."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY
    
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_email = factory.Faker('email')

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Example 5: Meta Options and Strategy Variations")
print("=" * 60)

# Try to instantiate abstract factory (will fail)
print("\n1. Abstract factory test:")
try:
    BaseJobPostFactory.create()
except factory.FactoryError as e:
    print(f"   ✗ Cannot instantiate abstract factory: {type(e).__name__}")

# Create remote job using concrete factory
remote_job = RemoteJobFactory.create()
print(f"\n2. Created remote job:")
print(f"   Company: {remote_job.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {remote_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Location: {remote_job.location.city}, {remote_job.location.state}")
print(f"   Saved to DB: {remote_job.id is not None}")

# Build draft job (not saved)
draft_job = JobPostDraftFactory()
print(f"\n3. Built draft job (default strategy is BUILD):")
print(f"   Company: {draft_job.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {draft_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Has ID (saved): {draft_job.id is not None}")
print(f"   Can still save: ", end='')
draft_job.save()
print(f"✓ ID after save: {draft_job.id}")

# Override BUILD strategy with create
created_job = JobPostDraftFactory.create()
print(f"\n4. Override BUILD strategy with .create():")
print(f"   Company: {created_job.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {created_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Has ID: {created_job.id}")

# Create stub object
stub_job = JobPostStubFactory()
print(f"\n5. Created stub object:")
print(f"   Company: {stub_job.company_name}")
print(f"   Job Title: {stub_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Email: {stub_job.company_email}")
print(f"   Type: {type(stub_job)}")
print(f"   Has model methods: {hasattr(stub_job, 'save')}")

# Batch create with different factories
print(f"\n6. Batch operations:")
remote_jobs = RemoteJobFactory.create_batch(2)
draft_jobs = JobPostDraftFactory.build_batch(2)
print(f"   Created {len(remote_jobs)} remote jobs (saved)")
print(f"   Built {len(draft_jobs)} draft jobs (not saved)")
for idx, job in enumerate(remote_jobs, 1):
    print(f"   Remote {idx}: {job.job_title} ({job.location.city})")
for idx, job in enumerate(draft_jobs, 1):
    print(f"   Draft {idx}: {job.job_title} (ID: {job.id})")
Print Output: ============================================================ Example 5: Meta Options and Strategy Variations ============================================================ 1. Abstract factory test: ✗ Cannot instantiate abstract factory: FactoryError 2. Created remote job: Company: DataFlow Systems Job Title: Remote Software Architect Location: Remote, Anywhere Saved to DB: True 3. Built draft job (default strategy is BUILD): Company: NextGen Solutions Job Title: Frontend Developer Has ID (saved): False Can still save: ✓ ID after save: 7 4. Override BUILD strategy with .create(): Company: Innovation Labs Job Title: Backend Engineer Has ID: 8 5. Created stub object: Company: CloudTech Industries Job Title: Full Stack Developer Email: contact@cloudtech.com Type: Has model methods: False 6. Batch operations: Created 2 remote jobs (saved) Built 2 draft jobs (not saved) Remote 1: Remote DevOps Specialist (Remote) Remote 2: Remote Data Engineer (Remote) Draft 1: QA Engineer (ID: None) Draft 2: Security Analyst (ID: None)
QuerySet Output: # Query JobPosts to see which were saved >>> from jobpost.models import JobPost >>> JobPost.objects.all() , , , , , , , , , ]> # Check remote jobs >>> JobPost.objects.filter(location__city='Remote') , , ]> >>> JobPost.objects.filter(location__city='Remote').values('id', 'company_name', 'job_title', 'location__state') # Verify draft jobs that were built but not initially saved >>> JobPost.objects.filter(id__gte=7).exclude(location__city='Remote') , ]> >>> JobPost.objects.filter(id__in=[7, 8]).values('company_name', 'job_title') >>> JobPost.objects.count() 10

⚙️ Meta Options Configuration

Meta options control factory behavior and configuration. These options are specified in the inner Meta class and control everything from which model to use, to how arguments are passed, to which fields are excluded from generation.

model - Target Model Specification

Specify the Django model this factory creates instances for.

📖 Full Description

The model Meta option is the cornerstone of every DjangoModelFactory, defining which Django model the factory will generate instances for. This required setting establishes the connection between your factory class and the database model it represents, enabling FactoryBoy to understand the model's structure, relationships, and constraints.

Key aspects of the model Meta option:

  • Required Setting: Every concrete DjangoModelFactory must specify a model
  • Direct Model Reference: Takes the actual model class, not a string
  • Manager Integration: Uses the model's default manager for database operations
  • Field Introspection: FactoryBoy can auto-discover model fields for validation
  • Relationship Handling: Enables proper handling of ForeignKey, OneToOne, and ManyToMany

Syntax

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills, Location, Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Basic model specification
class JobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost  # Required: direct reference to model class
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text')

# Multiple factories for different models
class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')

class SkillsFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    skill_name = factory.Faker('job')

📋 Parameters Description

The model Meta option accepts a single parameter:

  • model class - The Django model class that this factory will create instances of. Must be an actual class reference (e.g., User, JobPost), not a string. The model must be imported and accessible in the factory's scope. FactoryBoy will use this model's objects.create() manager method for the CREATE strategy and the model's __init__() for the BUILD strategy.

🔧 Attributes

When the model Meta option is set, FactoryBoy internally configures:

  • _meta.model - Stores the reference to the specified model class
  • _meta.model._meta - Access to Django's model metadata for field introspection
  • _meta.django_get_or_create - Related setting for get_or_create behavior

⚡ Methods

The model setting influences these factory methods:

  • _create(model_class, *args, **kwargs) - Creates and saves instance using the specified model
    • model_class - The model class from Meta.model
    • **kwargs - Field values to pass to model creation
    • Returns: Saved model instance
  • _build(model_class, *args, **kwargs) - Builds instance without saving
    • model_class - The model class from Meta.model
    • **kwargs - Field values to pass to model __init__
    • Returns: Unsaved model instance

↩️ Return Value

Setting the model option doesn't directly return a value, but it determines what type of instances the factory will create. When you call MyFactory.create(), it returns an instance of the specified model class.

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Basic Model Specification (User Model)

Create a factory for Django's User model with the model Meta option.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for creating User instances"""
    class Meta:
        model = User  # Specifies we're creating User objects
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n:04d}')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False

# Create users
print("=== Example 1: Basic Model Specification ===")
for i in range(5):
    user = UserFactory.create()
    print(f"User {i+1}: {user.username} - {user.first_name} {user.last_name}")
    print(f"  Email: {user.email}, Active: {user.is_active}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Active Users: {User.objects.filter(is_active=True).count()}")
print(f"Staff Users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
print(f"\nAll Usernames: {list(User.objects.values_list('username', flat=True))}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Basic Model Specification === User 1: user_0000 - John Smith Email: user_0000@example.com, Active: True User 2: user_0001 - Sarah Johnson Email: user_0001@example.com, Active: True User 3: user_0002 - Michael Brown Email: user_0002@example.com, Active: True User 4: user_0003 - Emily Davis Email: user_0003@example.com, Active: True User 5: user_0004 - James Wilson Email: user_0004@example.com, Active: True

Example 3: Model with Relationships (Author Model)

Use model specification with factories that have foreign key relationships.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author

class AuthorFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for Author model with User relationship"""
    class Meta:
        model = Author  # Targets Author model (has OneToOne with User)
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}"
    )
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.user.username}-author"
    )

# Create authors
print("\n=== Example 3: Author Model with Relationships ===")
for i in range(5):
    author = AuthorFactory.create()
    print(f"Author {i+1}: {author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Slug: {author.slug}")
    print(f"  User: {author.user.username} ({author.user.email})")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Authors with slugs: {Author.objects.exclude(slug='').count()}")
print(f"\nAuthor-User Pairs:")
for author in Author.objects.all():
    print(f"  {author.fullname} -> {author.user.username}")

Example 5: Complete Integration (JobPost Model)

Create a JobPost factory that integrates all previous models through relationships.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Factory for JobPost model with all relationships"""
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost  # Targets JobPost model
    
    title = factory.Iterator([
        'Senior Python Developer',
        'Full Stack Engineer',
        'DevOps Specialist',
        'Data Scientist',
        'Cloud Architect'
    ])
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = factory.Iterator([60000, 70000, 80000, 90000, 100000])
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 30000)
    
    # Relationships to other models
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Add 2-3 random skills
            skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3)
            for skill in skills:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)

# Create job posts
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPost Model with Full Integration ===")
for i in range(5):
    job = JobPostFactory.create()
    skills_count = job.required_skills.count()
    print(f"Job {i+1}: {job.title}")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"  Author: {job.author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Location: {job.location.city}, {job.location.state}")
    print(f"  Skills Required: {skills_count}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total JobPosts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")

print(f"\nJob Titles: {list(JobPost.objects.values_list('title', flat=True))}")
print(f"Salary Ranges:")
for job in JobPost.objects.all():
    print(f"  {job.title}: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
Print Output: === Example 5: JobPost Model with Full Integration === Job 1: Senior Python Developer Salary: $60,000 - $90,000 Author: Karen Martinez Location: Chicago, IL Skills Required: 3 Job 2: Full Stack Engineer Salary: $70,000 - $100,000 Author: Christopher Lee Location: San Francisco, CA Skills Required: 3 Job 3: DevOps Specialist Salary: $80,000 - $110,000 Author: Amanda Garcia Location: Miami, FL Skills Required: 3 Job 4: Data Scientist Salary: $90,000 - $120,000 Author: Daniel Rodriguez Location: Phoenix, AZ Skills Required: 3 Job 5: Cloud Architect Salary: $100,000 - $130,000 Author: Michelle Wilson Location: Philadelphia, PA Skills Required: 3

💡 Best Practice: Always specify the model Meta option in your DjangoModelFactory classes. This is required for FactoryBoy to create actual Django model instances. The model should be imported directly, not referenced as a string.

abstract - Abstract Base Factory

Define factories that cannot be instantiated directly but can be inherited.

📖 Full Description

Setting abstract = True in the Meta class creates an abstract factory that cannot be used to create objects directly. Abstract factories are useful as base classes that define common declarations which can be inherited by concrete factories. This promotes DRY (Don't Repeat Yourself) principles in test factories.

Key features and benefits of abstract factories:

  • Code Reusability: Define common field declarations once and reuse across multiple factories
  • DRY Principle: Avoid repetition of similar factory code
  • Inheritance Hierarchy: Create factory class hierarchies that mirror your model hierarchies
  • Cannot Be Instantiated: Abstract factories raise an error if you try to call them directly
  • Override Flexibility: Child factories can override parent declarations selectively

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author

class BaseFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base factory - cannot be instantiated"""
    class Meta:
        abstract = True  # Marks this factory as abstract
    
    # Common declarations for all child factories
    created_at = factory.Faker('date_time_this_year')
    updated_at = factory.Faker('date_time_this_year')

class BaseUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base factory with common user fields"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
        abstract = True  # Cannot instantiate this factory directly
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True

class RegularUserFactory(BaseUserFactory):
    """Concrete factory for regular users"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    is_staff = False
    is_superuser = False

class AdminUserFactory(BaseUserFactory):
    """Concrete factory for admin users"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    is_staff = True
    is_superuser = True

# Usage - abstract factories cannot be called
try:
    user = BaseUserFactory.create()  # ERROR
except factory.FactoryError:
    print("Cannot instantiate abstract factory")

# Concrete factories work normally
regular = RegularUserFactory.create()  # OK
admin = AdminUserFactory.create()  # OK

Parameters Description

abstract (bool): A boolean value set in the factory's Meta class. When set to True, the factory becomes abstract and cannot be instantiated directly. The factory can only be used as a base class for inheritance. When set to False or omitted (default), the factory is concrete and can be instantiated normally.

The abstract parameter is particularly useful when you have multiple factories sharing common field definitions, allowing you to extract shared logic into a base factory class. Child factories inherit all declarations from the abstract parent and can override them as needed.

Attributes

  • _meta.abstract (bool): Returns True if the factory is abstract, False otherwise
  • _meta.base_declarations (dict): Dictionary of all declarations defined in the factory class, including inherited ones
  • _meta.model: The model class specified in Meta (can be None for abstract factories)
  • __mro__: Method resolution order showing the inheritance chain from child to abstract parents

Methods

Inherited Methods: Abstract factories cannot be called directly, but child factories inherit standard factory methods:

  • build(**kwargs): Create an instance without saving (inherited by children)
  • create(**kwargs): Create and save an instance (inherited by children)
  • build_batch(size, **kwargs): Build multiple instances (inherited by children)
  • create_batch(size, **kwargs): Create multiple instances (inherited by children)

Attempting to call any instantiation method on an abstract factory will raise a factory.FactoryError.

Return Value

Abstract factories themselves cannot return values since they cannot be instantiated. However, child factories that inherit from abstract factories return model instances as normal. The return value is the same as any concrete factory:

  • For build(): Returns an unsaved model instance
  • For create(): Returns a saved model instance
  • For build_batch() and create_batch(): Returns a list of instances
Example 1: Abstract Base Factory for User Hierarchy

Create an abstract factory with common user fields, then extend it for different user types.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class BaseUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base with common user fields"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
        abstract = True  # Cannot instantiate
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True

class StaffUserFactory(BaseUserFactory):
    """Staff users with inherited fields"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    is_staff = True
    is_superuser = False

class SuperUserFactory(BaseUserFactory):
    """Superusers with inherited fields"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    is_staff = True
    is_superuser = True

# Create users
print("=== Example 1: User Hierarchy with Abstract Base ===")
staff_users = StaffUserFactory.create_batch(3)
super_users = SuperUserFactory.create_batch(2)

print("\nStaff Users:")
for user in staff_users:
    print(f"  {user.username} ({user.email}) - Staff: {user.is_staff}, Super: {user.is_superuser}")

print("\nSuper Users:")
for user in super_users:
    print(f"  {user.username} ({user.email}) - Staff: {user.is_staff}, Super: {user.is_superuser}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Staff Users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
print(f"Superusers: {User.objects.filter(is_superuser=True).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Hierarchy with Abstract Base === Staff Users: user_1 (user_1@example.com) - Staff: True, Super: False user_2 (user_2@example.com) - Staff: True, Super: False user_3 (user_3@example.com) - Staff: True, Super: False Super Users: user_4 (user_4@example.com) - Staff: True, Super: True user_5 (user_5@example.com) - Staff: True, Super: True
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Users: 5 Staff Users: 5 Superusers: 2
Example 2: Abstract Factory for Skills with Verification States

Create an abstract base factory for skills, then extend it for verified and unverified skills.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class BaseSkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base for all skill factories"""
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
        abstract = True
    
    name = factory.Iterator([
        'Python', 'JavaScript', 'Django', 'React', 'PostgreSQL',
        'Docker', 'Kubernetes', 'AWS', 'CI/CD', 'TDD'
    ])

class VerifiedSkillFactory(BaseSkillFactory):
    """Factory for verified skills"""
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    verified = True
    level = factory.Iterator(['Expert', 'Advanced', 'Intermediate'])

class UnverifiedSkillFactory(BaseSkillFactory):
    """Factory for unverified skills"""
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    verified = False
    level = 'Beginner'

# Create skills
print("\n=== Example 2: Skills with Abstract Base ===")
verified = VerifiedSkillFactory.create_batch(5)
unverified = UnverifiedSkillFactory.create_batch(5)

print("\nVerified Skills:")
for skill in verified:
    print(f"  {skill.name} - Level: {skill.level}, Verified: {skill.verified}")

print("\nUnverified Skills:")
for skill in unverified:
    print(f"  {skill.name} - Level: {skill.level}, Verified: {skill.verified}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Verified: {Skills.objects.filter(verified=True).count()}")
print(f"Unverified: {Skills.objects.filter(verified=False).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skills with Abstract Base === Verified Skills: Python - Level: Expert, Verified: True JavaScript - Level: Advanced, Verified: True Django - Level: Intermediate, Verified: True React - Level: Expert, Verified: True PostgreSQL - Level: Advanced, Verified: True Unverified Skills: Docker - Level: Beginner, Verified: False Kubernetes - Level: Beginner, Verified: False AWS - Level: Beginner, Verified: False CI/CD - Level: Beginner, Verified: False TDD - Level: Beginner, Verified: False

Example 3: Abstract Factory for Authors with Common Fields

Create an abstract author factory with common fields, then extend for different author types.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class BaseAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base for author factories"""
    class Meta:
        model = Author
        abstract = True
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(SuperUserFactory)  # Reuse from Example 1
    fullname = factory.Faker('name')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.fullname.lower().replace(' ', '-'))

class SeniorAuthorFactory(BaseAuthorFactory):
    """Senior authors with more experience"""
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    years_experience = factory.Iterator([10, 12, 15, 20])

class JuniorAuthorFactory(BaseAuthorFactory):
    """Junior authors with less experience"""
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    bio = "Entry level author"
    years_experience = factory.Iterator([1, 2, 3])

# Create authors
print("\n=== Example 3: Authors with Abstract Base ===")
senior_authors = SeniorAuthorFactory.create_batch(3)
junior_authors = JuniorAuthorFactory.create_batch(2)

print("\nSenior Authors:")
for author in senior_authors:
    print(f"  {author.fullname} ({author.years_experience} years)")
    print(f"    User: {author.user.username}, Slug: {author.slug}")

print("\nJunior Authors:")
for author in junior_authors:
    print(f"  {author.fullname} ({author.years_experience} years)")
    print(f"    User: {author.user.username}, Slug: {author.slug}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Senior Authors (8+ years): {Author.objects.filter(years_experience__gte=8).count()}")
print(f"Junior Authors (<5 years): {Author.objects.filter(years_experience__lt=5).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Authors with Abstract Base === Senior Authors: Michael Johnson (10 years) User: user_6, Slug: michael-johnson Sarah Williams (12 years) User: user_7, Slug: sarah-williams David Brown (15 years) User: user_8, Slug: david-brown Junior Authors: Emily Davis (1 years) User: user_9, Slug: emily-davis James Miller (2 years) User: user_10, Slug: james-miller

Example 5: Complete Integration with JobPost Abstract Factory

Create an abstract job post factory with common fields, then extend for different job types.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class BaseJobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Abstract base for all job factories"""
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        abstract = True
    
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    author = factory.SubFactory(SeniorAuthorFactory)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            skills = VerifiedSkillFactory.create_batch(2)
            for skill in skills:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)

class RemoteJobFactory(BaseJobPostFactory):
    """Remote job postings"""
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Iterator(['Remote Python Dev', 'Remote Full Stack', 'Remote DevOps'])
    location = None  # Remote jobs have no location
    salary_min = factory.Iterator([80000, 90000, 100000])
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 40000)
    is_remote = True

class OnsiteJobFactory(BaseJobPostFactory):
    """Onsite job postings"""
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Iterator(['Senior Backend', 'Frontend Lead', 'Tech Lead'])
    location = factory.SubFactory(WestCoastLocationFactory)
    salary_min = factory.Iterator([100000, 110000, 120000])
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 50000)
    is_remote = False

# Create jobs
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPosts with Abstract Base ===")
remote_jobs = RemoteJobFactory.create_batch(3)
onsite_jobs = OnsiteJobFactory.create_batch(2)

print("\nRemote Jobs:")
for job in remote_jobs:
    skills = job.required_skills.count()
    print(f"  {job.title}")
    print(f"    Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"    Author: {job.author.fullname}, Skills: {skills}")

print("\nOnsite Jobs:")
for job in onsite_jobs:
    skills = job.required_skills.count()
    print(f"  {job.title}")
    print(f"    Location: {job.location.city}, {job.location.state}")
    print(f"    Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"    Author: {job.author.fullname}, Skills: {skills}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Remote Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(is_remote=True).count()}")
print(f"Onsite Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(is_remote=False).count()}")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"\nAverage Salary Min: ${JobPost.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('salary_min'))['avg']:,.0f}")
Print Output: === Example 5: JobPosts with Abstract Base === Remote Jobs: Remote Python Dev Salary: $80,000 - $120,000 Author: Robert Taylor, Skills: 2 Remote Full Stack Salary: $90,000 - $130,000 Author: Jennifer Anderson, Skills: 2 Remote DevOps Salary: $100,000 - $140,000 Author: Christopher Thomas, Skills: 2 Onsite Jobs: Senior Backend Location: Los Angeles, CA Salary: $100,000 - $150,000 Author: Amanda Jackson, Skills: 2 Frontend Lead Location: San Francisco, CA Salary: $110,000 - $160,000 Author: Daniel White, Skills: 2
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Jobs: 5 Remote Jobs: 3 Onsite Jobs: 2 Total Authors: 10 Total Locations: 8 Total Skills: 20 Total Users: 15 Average Salary Min: $96,000

💡 Best Practice: Use abstract factories when you have multiple factories sharing common field definitions. This reduces code duplication and makes maintenance easier. Remember that abstract factories cannot be instantiated directly - they exist solely for inheritance purposes.

inline_args - Positional Argument Specification

Define which factory declarations should be passed as positional arguments during object instantiation.

📖 Full Description

The inline_args Meta option specifies a tuple of field names that should be passed as positional arguments to the model's __init__ method instead of keyword arguments. This is useful for models that require positional arguments or for optimizing instantiation when you know the expected argument order.

Key features and use cases of inline_args:

  • Positional Requirement: Some models require certain arguments in a specific positional order
  • Legacy Code Support: Work with older code that expects positional arguments
  • API Compatibility: Match third-party library initialization signatures
  • Order Preservation: Arguments are passed in the order specified in the tuple
  • Mixed Arguments: Can combine inline_args (positional) with regular kwargs

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class CustomModel:
    """Model that expects positional arguments"""
    def __init__(self, name, category, *args, **kwargs):
        self.name = name
        self.category = category
        self.description = kwargs.get('description', '')
        super().__init__(*args, **kwargs)

class CustomModelFactory(factory.Factory):
    class Meta:
        model = CustomModel
        inline_args = ('name', 'category')  # Pass these as positional args
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    category = factory.Faker('word')
    description = factory.Faker('text')  # This remains a keyword arg

# When creating: CustomModel('word1', 'word2', description='text...')

# For Django models with natural key requirements
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        inline_args = ('username',)  # Pass username as first positional arg
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

Parameters Description

inline_args (tuple): A tuple of string field names that specifies which factory declarations should be passed as positional arguments to the model's __init__ method. The order of field names in the tuple determines the order of positional arguments. Fields not listed in inline_args are passed as keyword arguments after all positional arguments.

When a factory with inline_args is called, FactoryBoy will extract the values for the specified fields and pass them as positional arguments in the exact order listed. All other field values are passed as keyword arguments. This allows you to match any __init__ signature that requires specific positional arguments.

Attributes

  • _meta.inline_args (tuple): Returns the tuple of field names to be passed as positional arguments
  • _meta.base_declarations (dict): Dictionary containing all field declarations, including those specified in inline_args
  • _meta.model: The target model class that will receive the positional arguments

Methods

Factories with inline_args support all standard factory methods, with positional argument handling applied automatically:

  • build(**kwargs): Create instance without saving, passing inline_args as positional parameters
  • create(**kwargs): Create and save instance, passing inline_args as positional parameters
  • build_batch(size, **kwargs): Build multiple instances, each with inline_args as positional
  • create_batch(size, **kwargs): Create multiple instances, each with inline_args as positional

When overriding values via kwargs in these methods, values for inline_args fields are still passed positionally in the correct order.

Return Value

Factories with inline_args return the same types as standard factories:

  • build(): Returns an unsaved model instance created with positional arguments
  • create(): Returns a saved model instance created with positional arguments
  • build_batch() and create_batch(): Return lists of instances
Example 1: User Model with Username as Positional Arg

Create a User factory where username is passed as the first positional argument.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        inline_args = ('username',)  # Username as positional
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False

# Create users
print("=== Example 1: User with inline_args ===")
users = UserFactory.create_batch(5)

for user in users:
    print(f"Username: {user.username} (positional arg)")
    print(f"  Email: {user.email}")
    print(f"  Name: {user.first_name} {user.last_name}")
    print(f"  Active: {user.is_active}, Staff: {user.is_staff}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Usernames: {list(User.objects.values_list('username', flat=True))}")
print(f"Active Users: {User.objects.filter(is_active=True).count()}")

Example 3: Author Model with User as Positional Arg

Create an Author factory with the user relationship passed as a positional argument.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
        inline_args = ('user',)  # User relationship as positional
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.Faker('name')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.fullname.lower().replace(' ', '-'))
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=150)
    years_experience = factory.Iterator([3, 5, 7, 10, 15])

# Create authors
print("\n=== Example 3: Author with inline_args for Relationships ===")
authors = AuthorFactory.create_batch(5)

for author in authors:
    print(f"Author: {author.fullname}")
    print(f"  User (positional): {author.user.username}")
    print(f"  Slug: {author.slug}")
    print(f"  Experience: {author.years_experience} years")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Average Experience: {Author.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('years_experience'))['avg']:.1f} years")
print(f"Authors: {list(Author.objects.values_list('fullname', flat=True))}")

Example 5: Complete JobPost Integration with inline_args

Create a JobPost factory with title and description as positional arguments.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        inline_args = ('title', 'description')  # Primary fields as positional
    
    title = factory.Iterator([
        'Senior Python Developer',
        'Full Stack Engineer',
        'DevOps Specialist',
        'Data Scientist',
        'Cloud Architect'
    ])
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = factory.Iterator([80000, 90000, 100000, 110000, 120000])
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 40000)
    is_remote = factory.Iterator([True, False])
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3)
            for skill in skills:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)

# Create jobs
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPost with inline_args ===")
jobs = JobPostFactory.create_batch(5)

for job in jobs:
    skills_count = job.required_skills.count()
    print(f"Job: {job.title} (positional arg)")
    print(f"  Description (positional): {job.description[:50]}...")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"  Author: {job.author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Location: {job.location.city if job.location else 'Remote'}")
    print(f"  Skills: {skills_count}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total JobPosts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"\nRemote Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(is_remote=True).count()}")
print(f"Onsite Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(is_remote=False).count()}")
print(f"Average Salary: ${JobPost.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('salary_min'))['avg']:,.0f}")
