💡 Best Practice: Use inline_args when your model's __init__ requires positional arguments or when you need to match specific API signatures. The order in the tuple matters - arguments are passed in that exact order. Combine with regular keyword arguments for maximum flexibility.

exclude - Helper Field Exclusion

Declare helper fields that should not be passed to the model during instantiation.

📖 Full Description

The exclude Meta option accepts a tuple of field names that are used as helper values during factory execution but should not be passed to the model's constructor. This is perfect for parameters, intermediate calculations, or values used by LazyAttribute that aren't actual model fields.

Key features and use cases of exclude:

  • Helper Parameters: Define intermediate values used in calculations without cluttering the model
  • Conditional Logic: Create flags or parameters that drive Maybe, LazyAttribute, or post_generation behavior
  • Clean Separation: Separate factory logic from model attributes
  • Parameter Passing: Accept parameters during factory calls that affect generation but aren't model fields
  • Computation Helpers: Store multipliers, flags, or temporary values used in complex field calculations

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills, Author, Location

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        exclude = ('salary_multiplier', 'is_remote', 'experience_level')  # Helper fields
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    # Helper fields - NOT passed to model
    salary_multiplier = 1.0
    is_remote = False
    experience_level = 'mid'  # Helper: 'junior', 'mid', 'senior'
    
    # Use helper fields in LazyAttribute
    salary_min = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: int(50000 * obj.salary_multiplier)
    )
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: int(100000 * obj.salary_multiplier)
    )
    
    # Use is_remote helper in Maybe
    location = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration=None,  # Remote jobs have no location
        no_declaration=factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    )
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)

# Usage examples
normal_job = JobPostFactory.create()  # Uses defaults
senior_job = JobPostFactory.create(
    salary_multiplier=2.0,  # Helper affects salary calculation
    experience_level='senior'
)
remote_job = JobPostFactory.create(
    is_remote=True  # Helper determines location=None
)

Parameters Description

exclude (tuple): A tuple of string field names that specifies which factory declarations should be excluded from being passed to the model's __init__ method. These fields exist only within the factory's build context and are available for use in LazyAttribute, Maybe, post_generation, and other factory declarations, but they never reach the model itself.

Excluded fields can still be accessed within the factory's scope using obj.field_name in LazyAttribute callbacks or by referencing them in conditional declarations like Maybe. They are evaluated normally during factory execution but are stripped out before instantiating the model.

Attributes

  • _meta.exclude (tuple): Returns the tuple of field names that are excluded from model instantiation
  • _meta.base_declarations (dict): Dictionary containing ALL declarations including excluded ones
  • _meta.model: The target model class that receives only non-excluded fields
  • _meta.pre_declarations (dict): Declarations evaluated before model instantiation, includes excluded fields

Methods

Factories with exclude support all standard factory methods:

  • build(**kwargs): Create instance without saving; excluded fields are available during build but not passed to model. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Override any field including excluded ones (excluded remain internal)
  • create(**kwargs): Create and save instance; excluded fields guide generation but aren't saved. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Override any field including excluded ones
  • build_batch(size, **kwargs): Build multiple instances with excluded fields. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances to create
    • **kwargs: Overrides applied to all instances
  • create_batch(size, **kwargs): Create multiple instances with excluded fields. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances to create
    • **kwargs: Overrides applied to all instances

Return Value

Factories with exclude return model instances that do NOT contain the excluded fields:

  • build(): Returns an unsaved model instance without excluded fields
  • create(): Returns a saved model instance without excluded fields
  • build_batch() and create_batch(): Return lists of instances without excluded fields

The returned instances will not have attributes for excluded fields, even though those fields were used during generation.

Example 1: User Model with Role Helper

Use an excluded role helper to determine staff and superuser status.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        exclude = ('role',)  # Helper field for determining permissions
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Helper field - not passed to model
    role = 'regular'  # Options: 'regular', 'staff', 'admin'
    
    # Use role helper to set permissions
    is_active = True
    is_staff = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.role in ['staff', 'admin'])
    is_superuser = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.role == 'admin')

# Create users with different roles
print("=== Example 1: User with Role Helper ===")
regular_users = UserFactory.create_batch(2, role='regular')
staff_users = UserFactory.create_batch(2, role='staff')
admin_user = UserFactory.create(role='admin')

print("\nRegular Users:")
for user in regular_users:
    print(f"  {user.username} - Staff: {user.is_staff}, Super: {user.is_superuser}")

print("\nStaff Users:")
for user in staff_users:
    print(f"  {user.username} - Staff: {user.is_staff}, Super: {user.is_superuser}")

print(f"\nAdmin User: {admin_user.username}")
print(f"  Staff: {admin_user.is_staff}, Super: {admin_user.is_superuser}")

# Verify role helper is not in model
print(f"\nHas 'role' attribute: {hasattr(regular_users[0], 'role')}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Staff Users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
print(f"Superusers: {User.objects.filter(is_superuser=True).count()}")
print(f"Regular Users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=False).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User with Role Helper === Regular Users: user_1 - Staff: False, Super: False user_2 - Staff: False, Super: False Staff Users: user_3 - Staff: True, Super: False user_4 - Staff: True, Super: False Admin User: user_5 Staff: True, Super: True Has 'role' attribute: False
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Users: 5 Staff Users: 3 Superusers: 1 Regular Users: 2
Example 2: Skills Model with Proficiency Multiplier

Use an excluded proficiency multiplier to calculate numeric skill levels.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
        exclude = ('proficiency_multiplier', 'skill_category')  # Helpers
    
    name = factory.Iterator([
        'Python', 'JavaScript', 'Django', 'React', 'PostgreSQL',
        'Docker', 'Kubernetes', 'AWS', 'Git', 'CI/CD'
    ])
    
    # Helper fields
    proficiency_multiplier = 1.0  # 0.5=beginner, 1.0=intermediate, 1.5=advanced, 2.0=expert
    skill_category = 'technical'
    
    # Calculate level based on proficiency
    level = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        0.5: 'Beginner',
        1.0: 'Intermediate',
        1.5: 'Advanced',
        2.0: 'Expert'
    }.get(obj.proficiency_multiplier, 'Intermediate'))
    
    # Calculate numeric score
    score = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: int(50 * obj.proficiency_multiplier))
    
    verified = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.proficiency_multiplier >= 1.5)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.skill_category.title()} skill: {obj.name} at {obj.level} level"
    )

# Create skills with different proficiency levels
print("\n=== Example 2: Skills with Proficiency Helpers ===")
beginner_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2, proficiency_multiplier=0.5)
intermediate_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2, proficiency_multiplier=1.0)
advanced_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2, proficiency_multiplier=1.5)
expert_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2, proficiency_multiplier=2.0)

print("\nAll Skills:")
for skill in Skills.objects.all():
    print(f"  {skill.name}: {skill.level} (Score: {skill.score}, Verified: {skill.verified})")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Verified Skills: {Skills.objects.filter(verified=True).count()}")
print(f"Unverified Skills: {Skills.objects.filter(verified=False).count()}")
print(f"Average Score: {Skills.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('score'))['avg']:.1f}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skills with Proficiency Helpers === All Skills: Python: Beginner (Score: 25, Verified: False) JavaScript: Beginner (Score: 25, Verified: False) Django: Intermediate (Score: 50, Verified: False) React: Intermediate (Score: 50, Verified: False) PostgreSQL: Advanced (Score: 75, Verified: True) Docker: Advanced (Score: 75, Verified: True) Kubernetes: Expert (Score: 100, Verified: True) AWS: Expert (Score: 100, Verified: True)
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Skills: 8 Verified Skills: 4 Unverified Skills: 4 Average Score: 62.5
Example 3: Author Model with Experience Tier Helper

Use an excluded experience tier to calculate years and bio content.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
        exclude = ('experience_tier', 'specialty')  # Helper fields
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.Faker('name')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.fullname.lower().replace(' ', '-'))
    
    # Helper fields
    experience_tier = 'mid'  # 'junior', 'mid', 'senior', 'expert'
    specialty = 'technology'
    
    # Calculate years based on tier
    years_experience = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'junior': factory.random.randint(1, 3),
        'mid': factory.random.randint(4, 7),
        'senior': factory.random.randint(8, 12),
        'expert': factory.random.randint(13, 20)
    }.get(obj.experience_tier, 5))
    
    # Generate bio based on tier and specialty
    bio = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.fullname} is a {obj.experience_tier}-level writer specializing in {obj.specialty}. "
        f"With {obj.years_experience} years of experience, they provide expert content."
    )
    
    verified_author = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.experience_tier in ['senior', 'expert'])

# Create authors with different tiers
print("\n=== Example 3: Authors with Experience Tiers ===")
junior = AuthorFactory.create(experience_tier='junior', specialty='web development')
mid = AuthorFactory.create(experience_tier='mid', specialty='data science')
senior = AuthorFactory.create(experience_tier='senior', specialty='cloud computing')
expert = AuthorFactory.create(experience_tier='expert', specialty='AI/ML')

for author in [junior, mid, senior, expert]:
    print(f"\n{author.fullname}:")
    print(f"  Years: {author.years_experience}")
    print(f"  Verified: {author.verified_author}")
    print(f"  Bio: {author.bio}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Verified Authors: {Author.objects.filter(verified_author=True).count()}")
print(f"Average Experience: {Author.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('years_experience'))['avg']:.1f} years")

With 5 years of experience, they provide expert content. David Brown: Years: 10 Verified: True Bio: David Brown is a senior-level writer specializing in cloud computing. With 10 years of experience, they provide expert content. Emily Davis: Years: 15 Verified: True Bio: Emily Davis is a expert-level writer specializing in AI/ML. With 15 years of experience, they provide expert content.

Example 4: Location Model with Region Helper

Use excluded region helper to determine city, state, and timezone.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
        exclude = ('region', 'location_type')  # Helper fields
    
    street = factory.Faker('street_address')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    
    # Helper fields
    region = 'west'  # 'west', 'east', 'midwest', 'south'
    location_type = 'urban'  # 'urban', 'suburban', 'rural'
    
    # City and state based on region
    city = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'west': factory.random.choice(['San Francisco', 'Seattle', 'Los Angeles']),
        'east': factory.random.choice(['New York', 'Boston', 'Philadelphia']),
        'midwest': factory.random.choice(['Chicago', 'Detroit', 'Minneapolis']),
        'south': factory.random.choice(['Houston', 'Atlanta', 'Miami'])
    }.get(obj.region, 'Unknown'))
    
    state = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'San Francisco': 'CA', 'Seattle': 'WA', 'Los Angeles': 'CA',
        'New York': 'NY', 'Boston': 'MA', 'Philadelphia': 'PA',
        'Chicago': 'IL', 'Detroit': 'MI', 'Minneapolis': 'MN',
        'Houston': 'TX', 'Atlanta': 'GA', 'Miami': 'FL'
    }.get(obj.city, 'XX'))
    
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Description based on helpers
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.location_type.title()} location in {obj.region} region"
    )

# Create locations by region

east_locs = LocationFactory.create_batch(2, region='east', location_type='suburban')
midwest_locs = LocationFactory.create_batch(2, region='midwest', location_type='rural')

print("\nAll Locations:")
for loc in Location.objects.all():
    print(f"  {loc.city}, {loc.state} - {loc.description}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")

Example 5: Complete JobPost Integration with Multiple Helpers

Use multiple excluded helpers to control job post generation comprehensively.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        exclude = ('job_tier', 'is_remote', 'skills_count')  # Multiple helpers
    
    # Helper fields
    job_tier = 'mid'  # 'entry', 'mid', 'senior', 'lead'
    is_remote = False
    skills_count = 3
    
    # Title based on tier
    title = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'entry': factory.random.choice(['Junior Developer', 'Associate Engineer', 'Entry Level Analyst']),
        'mid': factory.random.choice(['Software Engineer', 'Full Stack Developer', 'Backend Developer']),
        'senior': factory.random.choice(['Senior Engineer', 'Lead Developer', 'Principal Engineer']),
        'lead': factory.random.choice(['Tech Lead', 'Engineering Manager', 'Director of Engineering'])
    }.get(obj.job_tier, 'Developer'))
    
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    # Salary based on tier
    salary_min = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'entry': 60000,
        'mid': 90000,
        'senior': 130000,
        'lead': 170000
    }.get(obj.job_tier, 80000))
    
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 40000)
    
    # Location based on remote helper
    location = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration=None,
        no_declaration=factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    )
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Use skills_count helper
            skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(self.skills_count)
            for skill in skills:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)

# Create jobs with different configurations
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPosts with Multiple Helpers ===")
entry_remote = JobPostFactory.create(job_tier='entry', is_remote=True, skills_count=2)
mid_onsite = JobPostFactory.create(job_tier='mid', is_remote=False, skills_count=4)
senior_remote = JobPostFactory.create(job_tier='senior', is_remote=True, skills_count=5)
lead_onsite = JobPostFactory.create(job_tier='lead', is_remote=False, skills_count=6)

for job in [entry_remote, mid_onsite, senior_remote, lead_onsite]:
    skills = job.required_skills.count()
    location_str = job.location.city if job.location else 'Remote'
    print(f"\n{job.title}:")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"  Location: {location_str}")
    print(f"  Skills Required: {skills}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total JobPosts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Remote Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(location__isnull=True).count()}")
print(f"Onsite Jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(location__isnull=False).count()}")
print(f"Average Min Salary: ${JobPost.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('salary_min'))['avg']:,.0f}")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: JobPosts with Multiple Helpers === Junior Developer: Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Location: Remote Skills Required: 2 Software Engineer: Salary: $90,000 - $130,000 Location: Seattle Skills Required: 4 Senior Engineer: Salary: $130,000 - $170,000 Location: Remote Skills Required: 5 Tech Lead: Salary: $170,000 - $210,000 Location: New York Skills Required: 6
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total JobPosts: 4 Remote Jobs: 2 Onsite Jobs: 2 Average Min Salary: $112,500 Total Skills: 17 Total Authors: 4 Total Locations: 2 Total Users: 4

⚠️ Important: Fields listed in exclude will NOT be passed to the model, even if you try to override them during factory calls. They exist only for internal factory logic and are stripped before model instantiation.

💡 Best Practice: Use exclude for any fields that control factory behavior but aren't actual model fields. This keeps your factory logic clean and prevents errors from passing unexpected kwargs to your model's __init__ method.

rename - Field Name Mapping

Map factory declaration names to different model field names.

📖 Full Description

The rename Meta option provides a dictionary mapping factory field names to model field names. This is useful when you want to use a different name in your factory (perhaps to avoid Python reserved keywords like 'class', 'from', 'type', or 'import') but pass it to the model with the correct field name.

Key features and use cases of rename:

  • Keyword Avoidance: Use valid Python identifiers in factories even when model uses reserved keywords
  • API Compatibility: Match external API field names that conflict with Python syntax
  • Legacy Support: Work with models that have unconventional field naming
  • Code Clarity: Use more descriptive names in factories that map to shorter model field names
  • Multiple Mappings: Support multiple field renames in a single factory

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class Message:
    """Model with 'from' field (Python reserved keyword)"""
    def __init__(self, from_user, to_user, content, type_info):
        self.from_user = from_user
        self.to_user = to_user
        self.content = content
        self.type_info = type_info

class MessageFactory(factory.Factory):
    class Meta:
        model = Message
        rename = {
            'from_': 'from_user',  # Map from_ to from_user
            'type_': 'type_info'   # Map type_ to type_info
        }
    
    from_ = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)  # Use from_ in factory
    to_user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    content = factory.Faker('text')
    type_ = factory.Iterator(['email', 'sms', 'notification'])

# Creates: Message(from_user=..., to_user=..., content=..., type_info='email')

# For Django models
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        rename = {'klass': 'class'}  # If model had a 'class' field
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    klass = factory.Iterator(['A', 'B', 'C'])  # Use klass in factory

Parameters Description

rename (dict): A dictionary mapping factory field names (keys) to model field names (values). When the factory builds an object, it will use the keys internally but pass the values to the model's __init__ method. The dictionary allows multiple field mappings, enabling you to rename as many fields as needed in a single factory.

The rename mapping applies during the final instantiation step. Throughout factory execution, the factory uses the original field names (dictionary keys). Only when calling the model's __init__ method does FactoryBoy substitute the mapped names (dictionary values).

Attributes

  • _meta.rename (dict): Returns the dictionary of field name mappings
  • _meta.base_declarations (dict): Contains declarations using factory field names (not renamed)
  • _meta.model: The target model class that receives the renamed field names
  • _meta.pre_declarations (dict): Shows factory field names before renaming is applied

Methods

Factories with rename support all standard factory methods, with name mapping applied automatically:

  • build(**kwargs): Create instance without saving, applying rename mappings. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Use factory field names (not model names) when overriding
  • create(**kwargs): Create and save instance with rename mappings. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Use factory field names for overrides
  • build_batch(size, **kwargs): Build multiple instances with renaming. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances to create
    • **kwargs: Overrides using factory field names
  • create_batch(size, **kwargs): Create multiple instances with renaming. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances to create
    • **kwargs: Overrides using factory field names

Return Value

Factories with rename return model instances with the renamed fields:

  • build(): Returns unsaved model instance with renamed field names as attributes
  • create(): Returns saved model instance with renamed field names as attributes
  • build_batch() and create_batch(): Return lists of instances with renamed fields

The returned instances will have attributes matching the model field names (renamed values), not the factory field names (keys).

Example 1: User Model with 'class' Field Renamed

Handle a User model that has a 'class' field by using 'klass' in the factory.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Assume User model has been extended with a 'class' field
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        rename = {'klass': 'class'}  # Map klass to class
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Use klass in factory (mapped to class in model)
    klass = factory.Iterator(['Premium', 'Standard', 'Basic', 'Trial'])
    
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False

# Create users
print("=== Example 1: User with 'class' Field Renamed ===")
users = UserFactory.create_batch(5)

for user in users:
    # Access using model field name 'class'
    user_class = getattr(user, 'class', 'N/A')
    print(f"{user.username}: {user.first_name} {user.last_name}")
    print(f"  Email: {user.email}")
    print(f"  Class: {user_class}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users: {User.objects.count()}")
for klass in ['Premium', 'Standard', 'Basic', 'Trial']:
    count = User.objects.filter(**{'class': klass}).count()
    print(f"  {klass} users: {count}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User with 'class' Field Renamed === user_1: Michael Johnson Email: user_1@example.com Class: Premium user_2: Sarah Williams Email: user_2@example.com Class: Standard user_3: David Brown Email: user_3@example.com Class: Basic user_4: Emily Davis Email: user_4@example.com Class: Trial user_5: James Miller Email: user_5@example.com Class: Premium
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Users: 5 Premium users: 2 Standard users: 1 Basic users: 1 Trial users: 1

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author

Example 4: Location Model with 'global' Field Renamed

Handle Location model with 'global' reserved keyword using 'is_global' in factory.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
        rename = {
            'is_global': 'global',  # Map is_global to global
            'for_': 'for_companies'  # Map for_ to for_companies
        }
    
    city = factory.Iterator([
        'New York', 'London', 'Tokyo', 'Singapore', 'Dubai',
        'San Francisco', 'Berlin', 'Sydney'
    ])
    state = factory.Iterator(['NY', 'CA', 'TX', 'FL', 'WA'])
    street = factory.Faker('street_address')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = factory.Iterator(['USA', 'UK', 'Japan', 'Singapore', 'UAE'])
    
    # Use renamed fields in factory
    is_global = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: obj.city in ['New York', 'London', 'Tokyo', 'Singapore']
    )
    for_ = factory.Iterator(['Startups', 'Enterprise', 'All', 'Remote-First'])
    
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{'Global' if obj.is_global else 'Regional'} location for {obj.for_}"
    )

# Create locations
print("\n=== Example 4: Locations with 'global' Renamed ===")
locations = LocationFactory.create_batch(6)

for loc in locations:
    is_global = getattr(loc, 'global', False)
    for_companies = getattr(loc, 'for_companies', 'N/A')
    
    print(f"{loc.city}, {loc.country}:")
    print(f"  Global: {is_global}, For: {for_companies}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Global Locations: {Location.objects.filter(**{'global': True}).count()}")
print(f"Regional Locations: {Location.objects.filter(**{'global': False}).count()}")

Example 5: Complete JobPost with Multiple Reserved Keywords

Handle JobPost model with multiple reserved keywords requiring renaming.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        rename = {
            'job_type': 'type',          # Map job_type to type
            'for_': 'for_level',         # Map for_ to for_level
            'import_': 'import_source',  # Map import_ to import_source
            'async_': 'async_work'       # Map async_ to async_work
        }
    
    title = factory.Iterator([
        'Senior Python Developer',
        'Full Stack Engineer',
        'DevOps Specialist',
        'Data Scientist',
        'Cloud Architect'
    ])
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    # Use renamed fields in factory
    job_type = factory.Iterator(['Full-Time', 'Part-Time', 'Contract', 'Freelance'])
    for_ = factory.Iterator(['Junior', 'Mid-Level', 'Senior', 'Lead'])
    import_ = factory.Iterator(['Internal', 'LinkedIn', 'Indeed', 'Direct'])
    async_ = factory.Iterator([True, False])  # Remote async work allowed
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    salary_min = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'Junior': 60000,
        'Mid-Level': 90000,
        'Senior': 120000,
        'Lead': 150000
    }.get(obj.for_, 80000))
    
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 40000)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3)
            for skill in skills:
                self.required_skills.add(skill)

# Create jobs
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPosts with Multiple Renames ===")
jobs = JobPostFactory.create_batch(5)

for job in jobs:
    job_type = getattr(job, 'type', 'N/A')
    for_level = getattr(job, 'for_level', 'N/A')
    import_source = getattr(job, 'import_source', 'N/A')
    async_work = getattr(job, 'async_work', False)
    skills_count = job.required_skills.count()
    
    print(f"\n{job.title}:")
    print(f"  Type: {job_type}, For: {for_level}")
    print(f"  Source: {import_source}, Async: {async_work}")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"  Location: {job.location.city}")
    print(f"  Skills: {skills_count}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total JobPosts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
for jtype in ['Full-Time', 'Part-Time', 'Contract', 'Freelance']:
    count = JobPost.objects.filter(**{'type': jtype}).count()
    print(f"  {jtype}: {count}")
print(f"\nAverage Salary: ${JobPost.objects.aggregate(avg=models.Avg('salary_min'))['avg']:,.0f}")
print(f"Total Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: JobPosts with Multiple Renames === Senior Python Developer: Type: Full-Time, For: Junior Source: Internal, Async: True Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Location: Seattle Skills: 3 Full Stack Engineer: Type: Part-Time, For: Mid-Level Source: LinkedIn, Async: False Salary: $90,000 - $130,000 Location: Austin Skills: 3 DevOps Specialist: Type: Contract, For: Senior Source: Indeed, Async: True Salary: $120,000 - $160,000 Location: Boston Skills: 3 Data Scientist: Type: Freelance, For: Lead Source: Direct, Async: False Salary: $150,000 - $190,000 Location: Chicago Skills: 3 Cloud Architect: Type: Full-Time, For: Junior Source: Internal, Async: True Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Location: Miami Skills: 3
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total JobPosts: 5 Full-Time: 2 Part-Time: 1 Contract: 1 Freelance: 1 Average Salary: $96,000 Total Skills: 15 Total Authors: 5 Total Locations: 5

💡 Best Practice: Use rename whenever your model has field names that are Python reserved keywords (class, from, type, import, global, etc.) or when you want clearer field names in your factories. Always use the factory field names (dictionary keys) when calling the factory, not the model field names.

strategy - Default Creation Strategy

Set the default strategy (BUILD, CREATE, or STUB) for factory invocation.

📖 Full Description

The strategy Meta option sets the default creation strategy when the factory is called without an explicit strategy method. The strategy determines how objects are created and whether they interact with the database.

The three available strategies and their use cases:

  • CREATE_STRATEGY: Default strategy that creates and saves objects to the database using the _create() method. Best for integration tests requiring persisted data.
  • BUILD_STRATEGY: Creates objects without saving to database using the _build() method. Ideal for unit tests that don't need persistence or for performance-critical test suites.
  • STUB_STRATEGY: Creates lightweight stub objects (StubObject) without database interaction. Perfect for testing logic that only accesses object attributes without requiring a real model instance.

Key features of the strategy option:

  • Default Behavior: Sets what happens when you call the factory directly without build()/create()/stub()
  • Override Flexibility: Can always explicitly call build(), create(), or stub() regardless of default strategy
  • Performance Tuning: BUILD and STUB strategies avoid database I/O for faster tests
  • Test Isolation: BUILD_STRATEGY helps with test isolation by not persisting data
  • Mock Testing: STUB_STRATEGY provides lightweight objects for pure logic testing

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Skills, Author

# Default strategy: CREATE (most common)
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
        # strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY  # This is the default
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.Faker('email')

# Override default to BUILD
class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
        strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY  # Default to build (no save)
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Iterator(['Beginner', 'Intermediate', 'Advanced'])

# Override default to STUB
class AuthorStubFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
        strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY  # Default to stub
    
    fullname = factory.Faker('name')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.fullname.lower().replace(' ', '-'))

# Usage examples
user1 = UserFactory()  # CREATE (saved to DB)
skill1 = SkillsFactory()  # BUILD (not saved)
author1 = AuthorStubFactory()  # STUB (StubObject)

# Can always override default strategy
user2 = UserFactory.build()  # BUILD despite CREATE default
skill2 = SkillsFactory.create()  # CREATE despite BUILD default
author2 = AuthorStubFactory.create()  # CREATE despite STUB default

Parameters Description

strategy (factory constant): One of three factory strategy constants that determines the default behavior when calling the factory:

  • factory.CREATE_STRATEGY: Creates and persists objects to the database. Calls the factory's _create() method, which typically calls Model.objects.create() or saves the instance. This is the default for DjangoModelFactory.
  • factory.BUILD_STRATEGY: Creates model instances without saving them. Calls the factory's _build() method, which instantiates the model but never calls save(). Useful for testing model methods and properties without database overhead.
  • factory.STUB_STRATEGY: Creates StubObject instances that have the declared attributes but are not actual model instances. No database interaction occurs. Perfect for testing code that only accesses object attributes.

The strategy can be set to control the default factory behavior, but explicit calls to build(), create(), or stub() always override this default.

Attributes

  • _meta.strategy: Returns the default strategy constant for the factory
  • _meta.model: The target model class (None for STUB_STRATEGY in pure stub factories)
  • _meta.base_factory: Reference to the base factory class

Methods

All factories support these methods regardless of default strategy:

  • __call__(**kwargs): Invokes factory using the default strategy set in Meta. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Field overrides passed to the factory
    Returns: Object created using default strategy
  • build(**kwargs): Explicitly use BUILD_STRATEGY regardless of default. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Field overrides
    Returns: Unsaved model instance
  • create(**kwargs): Explicitly use CREATE_STRATEGY regardless of default. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Field overrides
    Returns: Saved model instance
  • stub(**kwargs): Explicitly use STUB_STRATEGY regardless of default. Parameters:
    • **kwargs: Field overrides
    Returns: StubObject with declared attributes
  • build_batch(size, **kwargs): Build multiple instances. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances
    • **kwargs: Field overrides for all instances
    Returns: List of unsaved instances
  • create_batch(size, **kwargs): Create multiple instances. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances
    • **kwargs: Field overrides for all instances
    Returns: List of saved instances
  • stub_batch(size, **kwargs): Stub multiple instances. Parameters:
    • size (int): Number of instances
    • **kwargs: Field overrides for all instances
    Returns: List of StubObjects

Return Value

The return value depends on the strategy used:

  • CREATE_STRATEGY: Returns a fully initialized and saved model instance with a primary key
  • BUILD_STRATEGY: Returns an initialized model instance without a primary key (not saved)
  • STUB_STRATEGY: Returns a StubObject with attributes matching the factory declarations but not a real model instance
Example 1: User Factory with Different Strategies

Compare CREATE, BUILD, and STUB strategies with User model.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserCreateFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Default CREATE strategy"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
        strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY  # Default, can be omitted
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True

class UserBuildFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """BUILD strategy - no save"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
        strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'build_user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class UserStubFactory(factory.Factory):
    """STUB strategy - lightweight objects"""
    class Meta:
        model = User
        strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'stub_user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')

# Test different strategies
print("=== Example 1: Strategy Comparison ===")

# CREATE strategy
created_user = UserCreateFactory()
print(f"\nCREATE Strategy:")
print(f"  Username: {created_user.username}")
print(f"  Has PK: {created_user.pk is not None}")
print(f"  Type: {type(created_user).__name__}")

# BUILD strategy
built_user = UserBuildFactory()
print(f"\nBUILD Strategy:")
print(f"  Username: {built_user.username}")
print(f"  Has PK: {built_user.pk is not None}")
print(f"  Type: {type(built_user).__name__}")

# STUB strategy
stub_user = UserStubFactory()
print(f"\nSTUB Strategy:")
print(f"  Username: {stub_user.username}")
print(f"  Has PK: {hasattr(stub_user, 'pk')}")
print(f"  Type: {type(stub_user).__name__}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Total Users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Only CREATE strategy saved to DB")
Print Output: === Example 1: Strategy Comparison === CREATE Strategy: Username: user_1 Has PK: True Type: User BUILD Strategy: Username: build_user_1 Has PK: False Type: User STUB Strategy: Username: stub_user_1 Has PK: False Type: StubObject
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Total Users in DB: 1 Only CREATE strategy saved to DB
Example 2: Skills Factory with BUILD Strategy for Fast Tests

Use BUILD strategy for unit tests that don't need persistence.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """BUILD strategy for fast unit tests"""
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
        strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY  # No DB saves
    
    name = factory.Iterator(['Python', 'Django', 'JavaScript', 'React', 'Docker'])
    level = factory.Iterator(['Beginner', 'Intermediate', 'Advanced', 'Expert'])
    verified = True
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} at {obj.level} level")

# Use default strategy (BUILD)
print("\n=== Example 2: BUILD Strategy for Fast Tests ===")
skills = [SkillsFactory() for _ in range(5)]

print("\nBuilt Skills (not saved):")
for skill in skills:
    print(f"  {skill.name}: {skill.level} (PK: {skill.pk})")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Skills in DB: {Skills.objects.count()}")

# Explicitly create if needed
saved_skill = SkillsFactory.create()
print(f"\nExplicitly created skill:")
print(f"  {saved_skill.name}: {saved_skill.level} (PK: {saved_skill.pk})")
print(f"Skills in DB after create(): {Skills.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: BUILD Strategy for Fast Tests === Built Skills (not saved): Python: Beginner (PK: None) Django: Intermediate (PK: None) JavaScript: Advanced (PK: None) React: Expert (PK: None) Docker: Beginner (PK: None)
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Skills in DB: 0 Explicitly created skill: Python: Intermediate (PK: 1) Skills in DB after create(): 1
Example 3: Author Factory with Mixed Strategy Usage

Use CREATE as default but demonstrate overriding with build() and stub().

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """CREATE strategy (default)"""
    class Meta:
        model = Author
        # strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY  # Default, omitted
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserCreateFactory)
    fullname = factory.Faker('name')
    slug = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.fullname.lower().replace(' ', '-'))
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    years_experience = factory.Iterator([3, 5, 7, 10])

# Different strategy invocations
print("\n=== Example 3: Mixed Strategy Usage ===")

# Default strategy (CREATE)
author1 = AuthorFactory()
print(f"\nDefault (CREATE):")
print(f"  {author1.fullname} (PK: {author1.pk})")

# Explicitly BUILD
author2 = AuthorFactory.build()
print(f"\nExplicit build():")
print(f"  {author2.fullname} (PK: {author2.pk})")

# Explicitly STUB
author3 = AuthorFactory.stub()
print(f"\nExplicit stub():")
print(f"  {author3.fullname}")
print(f"  Type: {type(author3).__name__}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Authors in DB: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Only default CREATE saved to DB")
Print Output: === Example 3: Mixed Strategy Usage === Default (CREATE): Michael Johnson (PK: 1) Explicit build(): Sarah Williams (PK: None) Explicit stub(): David Brown Type: StubObject
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Authors in DB: 1 Users in DB: 1 Only default CREATE saved to DB
Example 4: Location Factory with STUB Strategy for Logic Tests

Use STUB strategy for testing business logic without database.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationStubFactory(factory.Factory):
    """STUB strategy for lightweight testing"""
    class Meta:
        model = Location
        strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY
    
    city = factory.Iterator(['New York', 'Los Angeles', 'Chicago', 'Houston'])
    state = factory.Iterator(['NY', 'CA', 'IL', 'TX'])
    street = factory.Faker('street_address')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    region = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 'West' if obj.state in ['CA'] else 'East')

# Function to test with stub objects
def format_location_display(location):
    \"\"\"Business logic function that only needs attributes\"\"\"
    return f"{location.city}, {location.state} ({location.region} Region)"

# Use stub objects for testing
print("\n=== Example 4: STUB Strategy for Logic Tests ===")
locations = [LocationStubFactory() for _ in range(4)]

print("\nStub Locations (no DB):")
for loc in locations:
    display = format_location_display(loc)
    print(f"  {display}")
    print(f"    Type: {type(loc).__name__}, Has save(): {hasattr(loc, 'save')}")

print(f"\n📊 QuerySet Results:")
print(f"Locations in DB: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"STUB objects never touch database")
Print Output: === Example 4: STUB Strategy for Logic Tests === Stub Locations (no DB): New York, NY (East Region) Type: StubObject, Has save(): False Los Angeles, CA (West Region) Type: StubObject, Has save(): False Chicago, IL (East Region) Type: StubObject, Has save(): False Houston, TX (East Region) Type: StubObject, Has save(): False
QuerySet Output: 📊 QuerySet Results: Locations in DB: 0 STUB objects never touch database
Example 5: Performance Comparison Across All Strategies

Demonstrate performance differences and use cases for each strategy with JobPost.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost
import time

class JobPostCreateFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    \"\"\"CREATE - for integration tests\"\"\"
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        strategy = factory.CREATE_STRATEGY
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000

class JobPostBuildFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    \"\"\"BUILD - for unit tests\"\"\"
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000

class JobPostStubFactory(factory.Factory):
    \"\"\"STUB - for logic tests\"\"\"
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
        strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000

# Performance test
print("\n=== Example 5: Strategy Performance Comparison ===")

# CREATE strategy
start = time.time()
created_jobs = [JobPostCreateFactory() for _ in range(3)]
create_time = time.time() - start
print(f"\nCREATE Strategy (3 jobs):")
print(f"  Time: {create_time:.3f}s")
print(f"  Type: {type(created_jobs[0]).__name__}")
print(f"  Has PK: {created_jobs[0].pk is not None}")

# BUILD strategy
start = time.time()
built_jobs = [JobPostBuildFactory() for _ in range(3)]
build_time = time.time() - start
print(f"\nBUILD Strategy (3 jobs):")
print(f"  Time: {build_time:.3f}s")
print(f"  Type: {type(built_jobs[0]).__name__}")
print(f"  Has PK: {built_jobs[0].pk is not None}")

# STUB strategy
start = time.time()
stub_jobs = [JobPostStubFactory() for _ in range(3)]
stub_time = time.time() - start
print(f"\nSTUB Strategy (3 jobs):")
print(f"  Time: {stub_time:.3f}s")
print(f"  Type: {type(stub_jobs[0]).__name__}")
print(f"  Has PK: {hasattr(stub_jobs[0], 'pk')}")

print(f"\n📊 Database State:")
print(f"JobPosts in DB: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Authors in DB: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Locations in DB: {Location.objects.count()}")

print(f"\n⚡ Performance Summary:")
print(f"  STUB:   ~{stub_time/create_time*100:.0f}% of CREATE time (fastest)")
print(f"  BUILD:  ~{build_time/create_time*100:.0f}% of CREATE time (fast)")
print(f"  CREATE: 100% (slowest, needs DB I/O)")

print(f"\n💡 Use Case Recommendations:")
print(f"  CREATE: Integration tests, need relationships, need PKs")
print(f"  BUILD:  Unit tests, test model methods, faster than CREATE")
print(f"  STUB:   Logic tests, attribute access only, no DB needed")
Print Output: === Example 5: Strategy Performance Comparison === CREATE Strategy (3 jobs): Time: 0.124s Type: JobPost Has PK: True BUILD Strategy (3 jobs): Time: 0.067s Type: JobPost Has PK: False STUB Strategy (3 jobs): Time: 0.012s Type: StubObject Has PK: False 📊 Database State: JobPosts in DB: 3 Authors in DB: 3 Locations in DB: 3 ⚡ Performance Summary: STUB: ~10% of CREATE time (fastest) BUILD: ~54% of CREATE time (fast) CREATE: 100% (slowest, needs DB I/O) 💡 Use Case Recommendations: CREATE: Integration tests, need relationships, need PKs BUILD: Unit tests, test model methods, faster than CREATE STUB: Logic tests, attribute access only, no DB needed

💡 Best Practice: Most factories should use CREATE_STRATEGY (default) for simplicity. Override to BUILD_STRATEGY for faster unit tests that don't need persistence. Use STUB_STRATEGY only when testing pure logic that only accesses object attributes. You can always explicitly call .build(), .create(), or .stub() to override the default strategy.

⚠️ Note: StubObject instances from STUB_STRATEGY are not real model instances and don't have methods like save(), delete(), or refresh_from_db(). They only have the attributes defined in your factory declarations.

📝 Declarations

Declarations are the building blocks for generating field values in factories. They provide powerful, flexible ways to create realistic test data using lazy evaluation, sequences, faker integration, and relationships between factories.

Faker - Realistic Test Data Generation

Integrate the Faker library to generate realistic fake data for your model fields.

📖 Full Description

The Faker declaration integrates the popular Faker library with FactoryBoy, enabling you to generate realistic, localized fake data for testing. It provides access to hundreds of data providers covering names, addresses, dates, companies, internet data, and much more.

Key features of Faker declarations:

  • Provider Integration: Access to 100+ Faker providers (name, address, company, email, etc.)
  • Localization Support: Generate data in different locales (en_US, fr_FR, de_DE, etc.)
  • Parameter Passing: Pass parameters to Faker providers for customized output
  • Lazy Evaluation: Values generated at object creation time, ensuring uniqueness
  • Deterministic Testing: Can seed Faker for reproducible test data

Faker is particularly useful for creating realistic test data that resembles production data without exposing sensitive information. It's ideal for demonstrations, development databases, and comprehensive testing scenarios.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    # Basic Faker usage with provider name
    field_name = factory.Faker('provider_name')
    
    # Faker with parameters
    field_with_params = factory.Faker('provider_name', param1='value1', param2='value2')
    
    # Faker with locale
    localized_field = factory.Faker('provider_name', locale='fr_FR')
    
    # Common Faker providers
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    company = factory.Faker('company')
    address = factory.Faker('address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    job = factory.Faker('job')
    text = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    date = factory.Faker('date')
    url = factory.Faker('url')
    phone_number = factory.Faker('phone_number')
    ipv4 = factory.Faker('ipv4')
    uuid4 = factory.Faker('uuid4')

📋 Parameters Description

  • provider - String name of the Faker provider to use (e.g., 'name', 'email', 'company'). This is the first positional argument and is required.
  • locale - String specifying the locale for generated data (e.g., 'en_US', 'fr_FR', 'de_DE'). Defaults to 'en_US'. Different locales generate culturally appropriate data.
  • **kwargs - Additional keyword arguments passed directly to the underlying Faker provider. Each provider accepts different parameters for customization.

🔧 Attributes

Faker instances have the following attributes:

  • provider - The name of the Faker provider being used
  • locale - The locale configuration for the Faker instance
  • kwargs - Dictionary of additional parameters passed to the provider

⚡ Methods

Faker declarations use internal methods for evaluation:

  • evaluate() - Called at object creation time to generate the fake value using the specified provider
  • generate() - Internal method that invokes the Faker provider with configured parameters

↩️ Return Value

The Faker declaration returns values based on the specified provider:

  • String providers: Return strings (name, email, company, text, etc.)
  • Numeric providers: Return integers or floats (random_int, pyfloat, etc.)
  • Date/Time providers: Return date, datetime, or time objects
  • Complex providers: Return structured data like addresses, profiles, or custom objects

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Basic Faker Usage with Multiple Providers (User & Author)

This example demonstrates using various Faker providers to generate realistic user and author data with names, emails, and profile information.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """User factory with Faker for realistic profile data."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    # Basic Faker providers
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    username = factory.Faker('user_name')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    
    # Additional fields
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False

class AuthorFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Author factory with Faker for profile generation."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}")
    slug = factory.Faker('slug')

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Faker Example 1: Basic Providers (User & Author)")
print("=" * 60)

# Create users with Faker-generated data
print("\n1. Creating users with Faker providers:")
for i in range(3):
    user = UserFactory.create()
    print(f"   User {i+1}: {user.first_name} {user.last_name}")
    print(f"           Username: {user.username}, Email: {user.email}")

# Create authors
print("\n2. Creating authors with Faker:")
authors = AuthorFactory.create_batch(3)
for idx, author in enumerate(authors, 1):
    print(f"   Author {idx}: {author.fullname}")
    print(f"            User: {author.user.username}, Slug: {author.slug}")
Print Output: ============================================================ Faker Example 1: Basic Providers (User & Author) ============================================================ 1. Creating authors with Faker: Author 1: Robert Taylor User: rtaylor, Slug: modern-web-development Author 2: Emily Rodriguez User: erodriguez21, Slug: data-science-insights Author 3: David Kim User: davidkim, Slug: cloud-computing-tips

Example 2: Faker with Parameters for Customized Output (Location)

This example shows how to pass parameters to Faker providers to customize the generated data, creating location entries with specific address formats and text lengths.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Location factory with parameterized Faker providers."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Faker with parameters for address components
    address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('postcode')

class DetailedLocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Location factory with more specific Faker parameters."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Building number + street name
    address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    
    # Use state_abbr for two-letter codes
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    
    # Customize zipcode format
    zipcode = factory.Faker('zipcode')

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Faker Example 2: Parameterized Providers (Location)")
print("=" * 60)

# Create locations with basic Faker
print("\n1. Creating locations with basic Faker:")
for i in range(3):
    location = LocationFactory.create()
    print(f"   Location {i+1}: {location.address}")
    print(f"               {location.city}, {location.state} {location.zipcode}")

# Create locations with detailed Faker
print("\n2. Creating locations with state abbreviations:")
for i in range(3):
    location = DetailedLocationFactory.create()
    print(f"   Location {i+1}: {location.address}")
    print(f"               {location.city}, {location.state} {location.zipcode}")

# Override Faker values
print("\n3. Creating location with custom city:")
custom_loc = LocationFactory.create(city="San Francisco", state="CA")
print(f"   Custom: {custom_loc.address}")
print(f"          {custom_loc.city}, {custom_loc.state} {custom_loc.zipcode}")
Print Output: ============================================================ Faker Example 2: Parameterized Providers (Location) ============================================================ 1. Example 3: Faker with Text Generation (JobPost)

This example demonstrates using Faker's text generation providers with parameters to create job descriptions and requirements with controlled lengths.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location
import datetime

class JobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """JobPost factory with Faker text generation."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    # Company information
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email')
    
    # Job information
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    slug = factory.Faker('slug')
    
    # Text fields with max length parameters
    job_description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=500)
    requirements = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=300)
    
    # Relationships
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Dates
    application_start = factory.Faker('date_this_month')
    application_end = factory.Faker('date_between', start_date='+30d', end_date='+60d')

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Faker Example 3: Text Generation (JobPost)")
print("=" * 60)

# Create job posts with Faker text

    job = JobPostFactory.create()
    print(f"\n   Job Post {i+1}:")
    print(f"   Company: {job.company_name}")
    print(f"   Title: {job.job_title}")
    print(f"   Email: {job.company_email}")
    print(f"   Description length: {len(job.job_description)} chars")
    print(f"   Description preview: {job.job_description[:100]}...")
    print(f"   Requirements length: {len(job.requirements)} chars")
    print(f"   Application period: {job.application_start} to {job.application_end}")

# Create with custom descriptions
print("\n2. Creating job post with short description:")
short_job = JobPostFactory.create(
    job_description="Looking for an experienced developer.",
    requirements="3+ years Python experience required."
)
print(f"   Title: {short_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Description: {short_job.job_description}")
print(f"   Requirements: {short_job.requirements}")

Example 4: Faker with Localization (International JobPosts)

This example demonstrates using Faker with different locales to generate culturally appropriate data for international job postings.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import Location, JobPost, Author

class FrenchLocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Location factory with French locale."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    address = factory.Faker('street_address', locale='fr_FR')
    city = factory.Faker('city', locale='fr_FR')
    state = factory.Faker('region', locale='fr_FR')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('postcode', locale='fr_FR')

class GermanLocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Location factory with German locale."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    address = factory.Faker('street_address', locale='de_DE')
    city = factory.Faker('city', locale='de_DE')
    state = factory.Faker('state', locale='de_DE')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('postcode', locale='de_DE')

class JapaneseLocationFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Location factory with Japanese locale."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    address = factory.Faker('address', locale='ja_JP')
    city = factory.Faker('city', locale='ja_JP')
    state = factory.Faker('prefecture', locale='ja_JP')
    zipcode = factory.Faker('postcode', locale='ja_JP')

class InternationalJobPostFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """JobPost factory with localized company names."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    company_name = factory.Faker('company', locale='en_US')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email', locale='en_US')
    job_title = factory.Faker('job', locale='en_US')
    slug = factory.Faker('slug')
    job_description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=300)
    requirements = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    application_start = factory.Faker('date_this_month')
    application_end = factory.Faker('date_this_month')

# Example usage

print("=" * 60)

for idx, loc in enumerate(de_locations, 1):
    print(f"   {idx}. {loc.address}, {loc.city}, {loc.state} {loc.zipcode}")

# Create Japanese locations
print("\n3. Japanese Locations (locale='ja_JP'):")
jp_locations = JapaneseLocationFactory.create_batch(2)
for idx, loc in enumerate(jp_locations, 1):
    print(f"   {idx}. {loc.address}")
    print(f"      {loc.city}, {loc.state} {loc.zipcode}")

# Create international job posts
print("\n4. International job posts:")
job_fr = InternationalJobPostFactory.create(location=fr_locations[0])
job_de = InternationalJobPostFactory.create(location=de_locations[0])
job_jp = InternationalJobPostFactory.create(location=jp_locations[0])

print(f"   French: {job_fr.job_title} at {job_fr.company_name}")
print(f"          Location: {job_fr.location.city}, {job_fr.location.state}")
print(f"   German: {job_de.job_title} at {job_de.company_name}")
print(f"          Location: {job_de.location.city}, {job_de.location.state}")
print(f"   Japanese: {job_jp.job_title} at {job_jp.company_name}")
print(f"            Location: {job_jp.location.city}, {job_jp.location.state}")

Example 5: Advanced Faker with Skills and Multiple Providers

This example demonstrates advanced Faker usage combining multiple providers, including job-related providers for creating comprehensive skill sets and job postings.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from jobpost.models import Skills, JobPost, Author, Location

class TechSkillsFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Skills factory with tech-specific Faker providers."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    # Using various providers for tech skills
    skills = factory.Faker(
        'random_element',
        elements=[
            'Python', 'JavaScript', 'TypeScript', 'Java', 'C++', 'Go', 'Rust',
            'React', 'Angular', 'Vue.js', 'Django', 'Flask', 'Node.js',
            'Docker', 'Kubernetes', 'AWS', 'Azure', 'GCP',
            'PostgreSQL', 'MongoDB', 'Redis', 'Elasticsearch',
            'Machine Learning', 'Data Science', 'DevOps', 'CI/CD'
        ]
    )

class JobSkillsFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Skills factory with job-related providers."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    # Use Faker's job provider
    skills = factory.Faker('job')

class ComprehensiveJobFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    """Comprehensive JobPost factory with advanced Faker usage."""
    
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    # Company details
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    company_email = factory.Faker('company_email')
    
    # Job details with catch_phrase for titles
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    slug = factory.Faker('slug')
    
    # Rich text generation
    job_description = factory.Faker('paragraph', nb_sentences=10)
    requirements = factory.Faker('paragraph', nb_sentences=6)
    
    # Relationships
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Date range
    application_start = factory.Faker('date_between', start_date='today', end_date='+7d')
    application_end = factory.Faker('date_between', start_date='+30d', end_date='+60d')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def skills_list(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.skills_list.add(skill)
        else:
            # Add 4-6 tech skills
            num_skills = factory.Faker('random_int', min=4, max=6).generate({})
            for _ in range(num_skills):
                skill = TechSkillsFactory.create()
                self.skills_list.add(skill)

# Example usage
print("=" * 60)
print("Faker Example 5: Advanced Multiple Providers")
print("=" * 60)

# Create tech skills
print("\n1. Creating tech skills with random_element:")
tech_skills = TechSkillsFactory.create_batch(8)
for idx, skill in enumerate(tech_skills, 1):
    print(f"   Skill {idx}: {skill.skills}")

# Create job-based skills
print("\n2. Creating job-based skills:")
job_skills = JobSkillsFactory.create_batch(5)
for idx, skill in enumerate(job_skills, 1):
    print(f"   Skill {idx}: {skill.skills}")

# Create comprehensive job posts
print("\n3. Creating comprehensive job posts:")
jobs = ComprehensiveJobFactory.create_batch(2)
for idx, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
    print(f"\n   Job Post {idx}:")
    print(f"   Company: {job.company_name}")
    print(f"   Title: {job.job_title}")
    print(f"   Location: {job.location.city}, {job.location.state}")
    print(f"   Period: {job.application_start} to {job.application_end}")
    print(f"   Skills ({job.skills_list.count()}): {', '.join([s.skills for s in job.skills_list.all()])}")
    print(f"   Description: {job.job_description[:80]}...")

# Create job post with specific skills
print("\n4. Creating job post with custom skills:")
python_django_skills = [
    TechSkillsFactory.create(skills='Python'),
    TechSkillsFactory.create(skills='Django'),
    TechSkillsFactory.create(skills='PostgreSQL')
]
custom_job = ComprehensiveJobFactory.create(
    job_title='Senior Django Developer',
    skills_list=python_django_skills
)
print(f"   Title: {custom_job.job_title}")
print(f"   Skills: {', '.join([s.skills for s in custom_job.skills_list.all()])}")

LazyFunction - Delayed Function Execution

Execute functions at object creation time, perfect for dynamic values like current timestamps.

📖 Full Description

The LazyFunction declaration defers function execution until the factory instance is created. This powerful feature allows you to generate dynamic values that depend on the exact moment of object creation, such as timestamps, random UUIDs, or any computed value that should be fresh for each instance.

Key characteristics of LazyFunction:

  • Deferred Execution: Functions are called only when the object is being created
  • No Parameters: The function receives no arguments from the factory
  • Fresh Values: Each instance gets a newly computed value
  • Dynamic Content: Perfect for timestamps, UUIDs, current user context, or random data
  • Simple Syntax: Just wrap any callable in LazyFunction

LazyFunction is ideal for generating creation timestamps, unique identifiers, current user references, or any value that needs to be computed fresh for each test instance.

Syntax

import factory
import datetime
import uuid
from django.contrib.auth import get_user_model

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    # Current date/time when object is created
    created_at = factory.LazyFunction(datetime.datetime.now)
    
    # Current date only
    published_date = factory.LazyFunction(datetime.date.today)
    
    # UUID generation
    unique_id = factory.LazyFunction(uuid.uuid4)
    
    # Lambda functions for custom logic
    random_priority = factory.LazyFunction(lambda: random.randint(1, 10))
    
    # Complex computations
    computed_field = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: f"generated-{datetime.datetime.now().strftime('%Y%m%d-%H%M%S')}"
    )

📋 Parameters Description

  • function - A callable (function, lambda, method) that will be executed at object creation time. The function should accept no arguments and return the desired value for the field.

🚀 Practical Examples

Example 1: Dynamic Timestamps
import factory
import datetime
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    
    # Current timestamp when job is created
    created_at = factory.LazyFunction(datetime.datetime.now)
    
    # Posted date (current date only)
    posted_date = factory.LazyFunction(datetime.date.today)
    
    # Application deadline (30 days from creation)
    application_deadline = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: datetime.date.today() + datetime.timedelta(days=30)
    )
    
    # Job start date (random future date)
    job_start_date = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: datetime.date.today() + datetime.timedelta(
            days=random.randint(7, 90)
        )
    )

# Create instances with timestamps
print("=== Example 1: Dynamic Timestamps ===")
job1 = JobPostFactory()
print(f"Job 1 - {job1.job_title}")
print(f"  Created: {job1.created_at}")
print(f"  Posted: {job1.posted_date}")
print(f"  Deadline: {job1.application_deadline}")
print(f"  Start: {job1.job_start_date}")

# Wait a moment and create another
time.sleep(2)
job2 = JobPostFactory()
print(f"\nJob 2 - {job2.job_title}")
print(f"  Created: {job2.created_at}")
print(f"  Posted: {job2.posted_date}")
print(f"  Different timestamp: {job1.created_at != job2.created_at}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Dynamic Timestamps === Job 1 - Software Engineer Created: 2024-12-19 10:30:45.123456 Posted: 2024-12-19 Deadline: 2025-01-18 Start: 2025-01-15 Job 2 - Data Scientist Created: 2024-12-19 10:30:47.789012 Posted: 2024-12-19 Different timestamp: True
Example 2: UUID and Unique IDs
import factory
import uuid
import random
import string
from jobpost.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Faker('user_name')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    
    # UUID for external integration
    external_id = factory.LazyFunction(uuid.uuid4)
    
    # Custom unique identifier
    user_token = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: ''.join(random.choices(string.ascii_uppercase + string.digits, k=12))
    )
    
    # API key generation
    api_key = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: f"ak_{''.join(random.choices(string.ascii_lowercase + string.digits, k=24))}"
    )
    
    # Session ID
    session_id = factory.LazyFunction(
        lambda: f"sess_{uuid.uuid4().hex[:16]}"
    )

# Create users with unique identifiers
print("\n=== Example 2: Unique Identifiers ===")
for i in range(3):
    user = UserFactory()
    print(f"User {i+1}: {user.username}")
    print(f"  External ID: {user.external_id}")
    print(f"  Token: {user.user_token}")
    print(f"  API Key: {user.api_key}")
    print(f"  Session: {user.session_id}")
    print()

# Verify uniqueness
users = [UserFactory() for _ in range(5)]
external_ids = [str(u.external_id) for u in users]
print(f"Created 5 users with unique UUIDs: {len(set(external_ids)) == 5}")

💡 Best Practice: Use LazyFunction for values that must be computed at object creation time, like timestamps, UUIDs, or current user context. This ensures each test instance gets fresh, accurate values rather than stale data from factory definition time.

LazyAttribute - Context-Aware Field Generation

Generate field values based on other fields in the same factory instance.

📖 Full Description

The LazyAttribute declaration allows you to generate field values that depend on other fields within the same factory instance. The function receives the object being built as its parameter, enabling you to access and use values from previously defined fields to compute the current field's value.

Key characteristics of LazyAttribute:

  • Object Context: Access to the partially built object via the 'obj' parameter
  • Field Dependencies: Can reference values from other fields in the factory
  • Lazy Evaluation: Computed only when the object is being created
  • Dynamic Computation: Supports complex logic based on multiple field values
  • Order Dependent: Can only access fields defined earlier in the factory

LazyAttribute is perfect for creating derived fields, composite values, conditional logic, and any field that needs to be computed based on other field values within the same instance.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    # Basic field
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # LazyAttribute that uses other fields
    full_name = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.first_name} {obj.last_name}"
    )
    
    # Email based on name
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.first_name.lower()}.{obj.last_name.lower()}@example.com"
    )
    
    # Conditional logic
    display_name = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: obj.full_name if len(obj.full_name) <= 20 else f"{obj.first_name} {obj.last_name[0]}."
    )

📋 Parameters Description

  • function - A callable that receives one argument (obj) representing the object being built. The function should return the computed value for the field. The obj parameter provides access to all previously defined fields.

🚀 Practical Examples

Example 1: User Profile with Derived Fields
import factory
from jobpost.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Full name derived from first and last name
    full_name = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.first_name} {obj.last_name}"
    )
    
    # Username based on names
    username = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.first_name.lower()}.{obj.last_name.lower()}"
    )
    
    # Email derived from username
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.username}@company.com"
    )
    
    # Display name with length limit
    display_name = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: obj.full_name if len(obj.full_name) <= 15 
        else f"{obj.first_name} {obj.last_name[0]}."
    )

# Create users with derived fields
print("=== Example 1: User Profile with Derived Fields ===")
for i in range(3):
    user = UserFactory()
    print(f"User {i+1}:")
    print(f"  Name: {user.first_name} {user.last_name}")
    print(f"  Full Name: {user.full_name}")
    print(f"  Username: {user.username}")
    print(f"  Email: {user.email}")
    print(f"  Display: {user.display_name}")
    print()
Print Output: === Example 1: User Profile with Derived Fields === User 1: Name: John Smith Full Name: John Smith Username: john.smith Email: john.smith@company.com Display: John Smith User 2: Name: Alexandra Rodriguez Full Name: Alexandra Rodriguez Username: alexandra.rodriguez Email: alexandra.rodriguez@company.com Display: Alexandra R. User 3: Name: Mike Johnson Full Name: Mike Johnson Username: mike.johnson Email: mike.johnson@company.com Display: Mike Johnson
Example 2: Job Post with Salary and Requirements
import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    experience_level = factory.Iterator(['ENTRY', 'MID', 'SENIOR', 'LEAD'])
    
    # Salary range based on experience level
    min_salary = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'ENTRY': 40000,
        'MID': 65000,
        'SENIOR': 85000,
        'LEAD': 120000
    }[obj.experience_level])
    
    max_salary = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'ENTRY': 60000,
        'MID': 85000,
        'SENIOR': 120000,
        'LEAD': 180000
    }[obj.experience_level])
    
    # Salary display string
    salary_range = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"${obj.min_salary:,} - ${obj.max_salary:,}"
    )
    
    # Job description with dynamic content
    job_description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        f"We are seeking a {obj.experience_level.lower()} level {obj.job_title.lower()} "
        f"to join {obj.company_name}. This position offers a competitive salary "
        f"range of {obj.salary_range} and excellent growth opportunities."
    )

# Create job posts with computed fields
print("\n=== Example 2: Job Post with Salary and Requirements ===")
for level in ['ENTRY', 'MID', 'SENIOR', 'LEAD']:
    job = JobPostFactory(experience_level=level)
    print(f"{level} Level: {job.job_title}")
    print(f"  Company: {job.company_name}")
    print(f"  Salary: {job.salary_range}")
    print(f"  Description: {job.job_description[:80]}...")
    print()
