LEAD Level: Engineering Manager Company: Future Systems Salary: $120,000 - $180,000 Description: We are seeking a lead level engineering manager to join Future Systems. T...

💡 Best Practice: Use LazyAttribute when you need fields that depend on other field values in the same instance. Order matters - define base fields first, then derived fields. This pattern is essential for creating realistic, internally consistent test data.

Sequence - Auto-Incrementing Counters

Generate sequential, unique values using auto-incrementing counters.

📖 Full Description

The Sequence declaration provides an auto-incrementing counter that ensures unique values across all instances of a factory. It's perfect for creating unique identifiers, usernames, email addresses, or any field that needs to be unique and sequential.

Key characteristics of Sequence:

  • Auto-Increment: Automatically increments for each new instance
  • Global Counter: Sequence counter is shared across all instances of the factory
  • Guaranteed Uniqueness: Each instance gets a unique sequence number
  • Flexible Formatting: Supports string formatting and custom logic
  • Persistent: Counter continues across multiple test runs within the same session

Sequence is ideal for creating unique usernames, email addresses, identifiers, codes, or any field where uniqueness is required and sequential numbering is acceptable.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    # Basic sequence starting at 1
    sequence_field = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"item_{n}")
    
    # Sequence with custom formatting
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"user{n:03d}")
    
    # Email with sequence
    email = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"test{n}@example.com")
    
    # Complex sequence logic
    code = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"ID-{n:04d}-{n%10}")

📋 Parameters Description

  • function - A callable that receives one argument (n) representing the current sequence number (starting from 1). The function should return the formatted value for the field.

🚀 Practical Examples

Example 1: Unique User Credentials
import factory
from jobpost.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Sequential username with zero-padding
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"user{n:04d}")
    
    # Sequential email addresses
    email = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"testuser{n}@company.com")
    
    # Employee ID with department prefix
    employee_id = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"EMP-DEV-{n:03d}")
    
    # Badge number
    badge_number = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"B{n:06d}")

# Create users with sequential values
print("=== Example 1: Unique User Credentials ===")
for i in range(5):
    user = UserFactory()
    print(f"User {i+1}:")
    print(f"  Username: {user.username}")
    print(f"  Email: {user.email}")
    print(f"  Employee ID: {user.employee_id}")
    print(f"  Badge: {user.badge_number}")
    print()

print(f"Total users created: {User.objects.count()}")
print("All usernames:", list(User.objects.values_list('username', flat=True)))
Print Output: === Example 1: Unique User Credentials === User 1: Username: user0001 Email: testuser1@company.com Employee ID: EMP-DEV-001 Badge: B000001 User 2: Username: user0002 Email: testuser2@company.com Employee ID: EMP-DEV-002 Badge: B000002 User 3: Username: user0003 Email: testuser3@company.com Employee ID: EMP-DEV-003 Badge: B000003 User 4: Username: user0004 Email: testuser4@company.com Employee ID: EMP-DEV-004 Badge: B000004 User 5: Username: user0005 Email: testuser5@company.com Employee ID: EMP-DEV-005 Badge: B000005 Total users created: 5 All usernames: ['user0001', 'user0002', 'user0003', 'user0004', 'user0005']
Example 2: Job Posts with Sequential Identifiers
import factory
import datetime
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    
    # Job reference number
    job_ref = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"JOB-{datetime.date.today().year}-{n:04d}")
    
    # Application code
    application_code = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"APP{n:05d}")
    
    # Internal ID with year and sequence
    internal_id = factory.Sequence(
        lambda n: f"INT-{datetime.date.today().strftime('%Y%m')}-{n:03d}"
    )
    
    # Priority sequence (cycles through 1-3)
    priority_level = factory.Sequence(lambda n: (n % 3) + 1)

# Create job posts with sequential codes
print("\n=== Example 2: Job Posts with Sequential Identifiers ===")
jobs = [JobPostFactory() for _ in range(6)]

for i, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
    print(f"Job {i}: {job.job_title}")
    print(f"  Company: {job.company_name}")
    print(f"  Job Ref: {job.job_ref}")
    print(f"  App Code: {job.application_code}")
    print(f"  Internal ID: {job.internal_id}")
    print(f"  Priority: {job.priority_level}")
    print()

print("Unique job references:")
refs = [job.job_ref for job in jobs]
print(f"  Created: {len(refs)}")
print(f"  Unique: {len(set(refs))}")
print(f"  All unique: {len(refs) == len(set(refs))}")

💡 Best Practice: Use Sequence for fields that must be unique across all instances. The auto-incrementing nature guarantees uniqueness, making it perfect for usernames, IDs, and reference numbers. Combine with formatting functions for professional-looking identifiers.

LazyAttributeSequence - Combined Context and Sequence

Generate values based on both object attributes and sequence counter.

📖 Full Description

The LazyAttributeSequence combines the features of LazyAttribute and Sequence, providing access to both the object being built and an auto-incrementing sequence counter. This powerful declaration enables you to generate unique values that also depend on other field values in the same factory.

Key characteristics of LazyAttributeSequence:

  • Dual Context: Access to both object context (obj) and sequence number (n)
  • Automatic Uniqueness: Sequence counter ensures unique values across instances
  • Context-Aware Generation: Can reference other field values during computation
  • Flexible Computation: Supports complex logic combining multiple factors
  • Lazy Evaluation: Evaluated at object creation time, ensuring all dependencies are available

LazyAttributeSequence is ideal for generating unique identifiers that incorporate meaningful prefixes or patterns, creating sequential usernames based on names, or building unique URLs with path components.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = MyModel
    
    # LazyAttributeSequence with lambda
    field_name = factory.LazyAttributeSequence(lambda obj, n: f'{obj.prefix}{n}')
    
    # LazyAttributeSequence with function
    def generate_unique_code(obj, n):
        return f'{obj.category_code}-{n:05d}'
    
    unique_code = factory.LazyAttributeSequence(generate_unique_code)

📋 Parameters Description

  • method - A callable that accepts two positional arguments: obj (the factory stub object with all previously declared attributes) and n (an integer sequence counter starting from 0), and returns the computed field value. Can be a lambda function or a regular function.

🔧 Attributes

LazyAttributeSequence instances have the following internal attributes:

  • function - The callable method used to generate values
  • sequence - Reference to the factory's sequence tracker

⚡ Methods

LazyAttributeSequence uses these internal methods during evaluation:

  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Main evaluation method that computes the value
    • instance - The object being built with its current attribute values
    • step - The factory's build step object containing sequence information
    • extra - Additional context or overrides
    • Returns: The computed value based on both instance context and sequence

↩️ Return Value

Returns the value computed by the callable function, which can be any Python type (string, integer, object, etc.) based on both the object context and the sequence number. The return type depends entirely on what your function returns.

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Sequential Usernames Based on First and Last Name (User Model)

This example shows how to create sequential, unique usernames that incorporate the user's actual name, ensuring both uniqueness and meaningfulness.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Create username like: john.smith_001, jane.doe_002, etc.
    username = factory.LazyAttributeSequence(
        lambda obj, n: f'{obj.first_name.lower()}.{obj.last_name.lower()}_{n:03d}'
    )
    
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    is_staff = False
    is_superuser = False
    is_active = True

# Create instances
print("=== Example 1: Sequential Usernames ===")
user1 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 1: username={user1.username}, email={user1.email}, name={user1.first_name} {user1.last_name}")

user2 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 2: username={user2.username}, email={user2.email}, name={user2.first_name} {user2.last_name}")

user3 = UserFactory(first_name='Bob', last_name='Wilson')
print(f"User 3: username={user3.username}, email={user3.email}, name={user3.first_name} {user3.last_name}")

print(f"\nTotal users created: {User.objects.count()}")
print("\nAll usernames in database:")
for user in User.objects.all():
    print(f"  - {user.username} ({user.email})")
Print Output: === Example 1: Sequential Usernames === User 1: username=john.smith_000, email=john.smith_000@example.com, name=John Smith User 2: username=sarah.johnson_001, email=sarah.johnson_001@example.com, name=Sarah Johnson User 3: username=bob.wilson_002, email=bob.wilson_002@example.com, name=Bob Wilson Total users created: 3 All usernames in database: - john.smith_000 (john.smith_000@example.com) - sarah.johnson_001 (sarah.johnson_001@example.com) - bob.wilson_002 (bob.wilson_002@example.com)
Example 2: Sequential Skill Codes with Category Prefix (Skills Model)

Generate unique skill codes that combine a category indicator with a sequential number for easy categorization and identification.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

    )

Example 3: Sequential Location Codes with City Abbreviation (Location Model)

Create unique location identifiers that combine city abbreviations with sequence numbers for warehouse or office tracking.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Generate location codes like: NYC-LOC-001, LA-LOC-002, etc.
    @staticmethod
    def generate_location_code(obj, n):
        # Take first 3 letters of city, uppercase
        city_abbr = obj.city[:3].upper()
        return f'{city_abbr}-LOC-{n:03d}'
    
    location_code = factory.LazyAttributeSequence(generate_location_code)

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 3: Sequential Location Codes ===")

print(f"Location 1: {loc1.location_code} - {loc1.street_address}, {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")

loc4 = LocationFactory(city='Houston')
print(f"Location 4: {loc4.location_code} - {loc4.street_address}, {loc4.city}, {loc4.state}")

print(f"\nTotal locations created: {Location.objects.count()}")
print("\nAll locations in database:")

Example 5: Sequential Job Post Reference Numbers (JobPost Model)

Create unique job posting reference numbers that combine year, month, and a sequential counter for tracking and auditing purposes.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost
from datetime import datetime

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = 50000
    salary_max = 100000
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Generate reference numbers like: JOB-2025-12-0001
    @staticmethod
    def create_reference_number(obj, n):
        now = datetime.now()
        year = now.year
        month = now.month
        return f'JOB-{year}-{month:02d}-{n:04d}'
    
    reference_number = factory.LazyAttributeSequence(create_reference_number)
    
    # Add reference to description
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f'[Ref: {obj.reference_number}] {factory.Faker("text", max_nb_chars=150).evaluate(None, None, {"locale": None})}'
    )

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 5: Sequential Job Post References ===")
job1 = JobPostFactory(title='Senior Python Developer')
print(f"Job 1: {job1.reference_number}")
print(f"  Title: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job1.location.city}, {job1.location.state}")

job2 = JobPostFactory(title='Data Scientist')
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.reference_number}")
print(f"  Title: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,} - ${job2.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job2.location.city}, {job2.location.state}")

job3 = JobPostFactory(title='DevOps Engineer')
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.reference_number}")
print(f"  Title: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job3.salary_min:,} - ${job3.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job3.location.city}, {job3.location.state}")

print(f"\nTotal job posts created: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print("\nAll job posts in database:")
for job in JobPost.objects.all():
    print(f"  - {job.reference_number}: {job.title} in {job.location.city}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Sequential Job Post References === Job 1: JOB-2025-12-0000 Title: Senior Python Developer Salary: $50,000 - $100,000 Location: Seattle, WA Job 2: JOB-2025-12-0001 Title: Data Scientist Salary: $50,000 - $100,000 Location: Boston, MA Job 3: JOB-2025-12-0002 Title: DevOps Engineer Salary: $50,000 - $100,000 Location: Austin, TX Total job posts created: 3 All job posts in database: - JOB-2025-12-0000: Senior Python Developer in Seattle - JOB-2025-12-0001: Data Scientist in Boston - JOB-2025-12-0002: DevOps Engineer in Austin

💡 Best Practice: LazyAttributeSequence is ideal when you need unique identifiers that also convey meaning through prefixes, codes, or references to other field values. It ensures uniqueness while maintaining readability and context.

SubFactory - Create Related Objects

Generate related model instances using other factories for foreign key relationships.

📖 Full Description

The SubFactory declaration creates instances of related models using their respective factories. This powerful feature enables you to build complex object graphs with proper foreign key relationships, ensuring that your test data maintains referential integrity and realistic associations.

Key characteristics of SubFactory:

  • Automatic Relationships: Automatically creates related objects for foreign key fields
  • Factory Delegation: Uses other factories to generate related instances
  • Parameter Passing: Can pass specific values to the related factory
  • Lazy Creation: Related objects are created only when needed
  • Deep Nesting: Supports multiple levels of factory relationships

SubFactory is essential for creating realistic test data with proper model relationships, such as users with profiles, posts with authors, or orders with customers.

Syntax

import factory

class RelatedFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = RelatedModel
    
    field1 = factory.Faker('text')

class MainFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = MainModel
    
    # Basic SubFactory
    related_field = factory.SubFactory(RelatedFactory)
    
    # SubFactory with specific parameters
    custom_related = factory.SubFactory(
        RelatedFactory,
        field1='specific_value',
        field2=factory.Faker('name')
    )
    
    # SubFactory with lazy parameters
    contextual_related = factory.SubFactory(
        RelatedFactory,
        field1=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"related_to_{obj.name}")
    )

📋 Parameters Description

  • factory - The factory class to use for creating the related object. This is the first positional argument and is required.
  • **kwargs - Keyword arguments to pass to the related factory. These override or supplement the default field values in the related factory.

🚀 Practical Examples

Example 1: User and Author Relationship
import factory
from jobpost.models import User, Author

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"user{n:03d}")
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.username}@example.com"
    )

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    # Create a User instance for the foreign key relationship
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    
    # Generate fullname based on the created user
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}"
    )
    
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)

# Create authors with related users
print("=== Example 1: User and Author Relationship ===")
for i in range(3):
    author = AuthorFactory()
    print(f"Author {i+1}:")
    print(f"  Fullname: {author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Bio: {author.bio[:50]}...")
    print(f"  User Details:")
    print(f"    Username: {author.user.username}")
    print(f"    Email: {author.user.email}")
    print(f"    Name: {author.user.first_name} {author.user.last_name}")
    print()

print(f"Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User and Author Relationship === Author 1: Fullname: John Smith Bio: Lorem ipsum dolor sit amet, consectetur adipiscing... User Details: Username: user001 Email: user001@example.com Name: John Smith Author 2: Fullname: Sarah Johnson Bio: Sed do eiusmod tempor incididunt ut labore et dol... User Details: Username: user002 Email: user002@example.com Name: Sarah Johnson Author 3: Fullname: Michael Brown Bio: Ut enim ad minim veniam, quis nostrud exercitatio... User Details: Username: user003 Email: user003@example.com Name: Michael Brown Total authors: 3 Total users: 3
Example 2: Job Posts with Authors and Custom User Data
import factory
from jobpost.models import User, Author, JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    
    # Create an author with custom user data for this job post
    author = factory.SubFactory(
        AuthorFactory,

            UserFactory,

        ),

        bio="Professional HR Manager responsible for talent acquisition and employee relations."
    )

        f"Position: {obj.job_title} at {obj.company_name}. "
        f"Posted by {obj.author.fullname}. "
        f"Join our dynamic team and contribute to our company's success."
    )
    
    # Contact email based on the author's user
    contact_email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"jobs+{obj.author.user.username}@{obj.company_name.lower().replace(' ', '')}.com"
    )

# Create job posts with nested relationships
print("\n=== Example 2: Job Posts with Authors and Custom User Data ===")

    print(f"  Author User: {job.author.user.username}")
    print(f"  Contact: {job.contact_email}")
    print(f"  Description: {job.job_description[:80]}...")
    print()

# Verify object creation
print(f"Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total users: {User.objects.count()}")

Example 3: Multi-Level Factory Relationships
import factory
from jobpost.models import User, Author, JobPost, Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state')
    country = factory.Faker('country')

class CompanyUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    # Company email domain based on location
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f"company_user_{n}")
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.username}@company.com")

class CompanyAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(CompanyUserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}"
    )
    bio = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        f"Professional recruiter at company. Contact: {obj.user.email}"
    )

class DetailedJobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    job_title = factory.Faker('job')
    company_name = factory.Faker('company')
    
    # Related objects with nested factories
    author = factory.SubFactory(CompanyAuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Fields that depend on multiple related objects
    job_description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        f"{obj.job_title} position at {obj.company_name} in {obj.location.city}, "
        f"{obj.location.state}. Posted by {obj.author.fullname}. "
        f"Contact {obj.author.user.email} for more information."
    )
    
    # Contact information based on related objects
    contact_info = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        f"Email: {obj.author.user.email} | "
        f"Office: {obj.location.city}, {obj.location.state}"
    )

# Create complex nested objects
print("\n=== Example 3: Multi-Level Factory Relationships ===")
jobs = [DetailedJobPostFactory() for _ in range(2)]

for i, job in enumerate(jobs, 1):
    print(f"Job {i}:")
    print(f"  Position: {job.job_title} at {job.company_name}")
    print(f"  Location: {job.location.city}, {job.location.state}, {job.location.country}")
    print(f"  Recruiter: {job.author.fullname} ({job.author.user.username})")
    print(f"  Contact: {job.contact_info}")
    print(f"  Description: {job.job_description[:100]}...")
    print()

# Verify all objects were created
print("Object counts:")
print(f"  Job Posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")  
print(f"  Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")

print("\nAll company users:")
for user in User.objects.filter(username__startswith='company_user'):
    print(f"  {user.username}: {user.first_name} {user.last_name} ({user.email})")
Print Output: === Example 3: Multi-Level Factory Relationships === Job 1: Position: Software Developer at TechnoVision Corp Location: Portland, Oregon, United States Recruiter: Jennifer Wilson (company_user_1) Contact: Email: company_user_1@company.com | Office: Portland, Oregon Description: Software Developer position at TechnoVision Corp in Portland, Oregon. Posted by Jennifer ... Job 2: Position: Marketing Specialist at DataFlow Systems Location: Austin, Texas, United States Recruiter: David Martinez (company_user_2) Contact: Email: company_user_2@company.com | Office: Austin, Texas Description: Marketing Specialist position at DataFlow Systems in Austin, Texas. Posted by David Mar... Object counts: Job Posts: 2 Authors: 2 Users: 2 Locations: 2 All company users: company_user_1: Jennifer Wilson (company_user_1@company.com) company_user_2: David Martinez (company_user_2@company.com)

💡 Best Practice: Use SubFactory to maintain proper foreign key relationships in your test data. This ensures referential integrity and creates realistic object graphs. Pass specific parameters to customize related objects for different test scenarios while maintaining the relationships.

Iterator - Cycle Through Values

Use successive values from an iterable for field generation.

📖 Full Description

The Iterator declaration cycles through successive values from a provided iterable (list, tuple, generator, or any iterable object). Each time a new factory instance is created, it retrieves the next value from the iterable. When the iterable is exhausted, it can either restart from the beginning (if cycle=True) or raise an error (if cycle=False).

Key features of Iterator:

  • Sequential Access: Values are retrieved in order from the iterable
  • Cycling Control: Can repeat values indefinitely or stop after one pass
  • Getter Transformation: Apply a function to transform values before use
  • Manual Reset: Can reset iteration to start from the first value
  • Shared State: All instances of the factory share the same iterator position

Iterator is particularly useful for rotating through predefined categories, assigning round-robin values, or creating test data with controlled variation patterns.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = MyModel
    
    # Basic iterator with list
    field_name = factory.Iterator(['value1', 'value2', 'value3'])
    
    # Iterator with tuple and getter
    role = factory.Iterator(
        [('admin', 'Administrator'), ('user', 'Regular User')],
        getter=lambda x: x[0]  # Extract first element
    )
    
    # Non-cycling iterator (stops after exhausting values)
    country = factory.Iterator(['US', 'UK', 'FR'], cycle=False)
    
    # Iterator with generator
    status_codes = factory.Iterator(range(200, 206))

# Reset iterator manually
MyFactory.field_name.reset()

📋 Parameters Description

  • iterable - Required. The sequence of values to iterate through. Can be a list, tuple, generator, range object, or any Python iterable. Values are consumed in the order they appear.
  • cycle - Optional boolean (default: True). If True, the iterator restarts from the beginning when exhausted. If False, raises StopIteration error when all values have been used.
  • getter - Optional callable function that transforms each value before it's assigned. Receives the current iterated value and returns the transformed value. Useful for extracting specific elements from tuples or performing transformations.

🔧 Attributes

Iterator instances have the following internal attributes:

  • iterator - The Python iterator object wrapping the iterable
  • cycle - Boolean flag indicating whether the iterator should cycle
  • getter - The transformation function (if provided)
  • _original_iterable - Reference to the original iterable for reset purposes

⚡ Methods

  • reset() - Resets the iterator to start from the first value again
    • Parameters: None
    • Returns: None
    • Usage: Call on the factory class attribute (e.g., MyFactory.field_name.reset())
  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Internal evaluation method that retrieves the next value
    • instance - The object being built
    • step - The factory's build step
    • extra - Additional context
    • Returns: The next value from the iterator (optionally transformed by getter)

↩️ Return Value

Returns the next value from the iterable sequence. If a getter function is provided, returns the transformed value. The return type depends on what's in the iterable and what the getter function returns (if applicable).

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Rotating Through User Roles (User Model)

Cycle through predefined user roles to create diverse test users with different permission levels.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    
    # Rotate through role configurations
    role_config = factory.Iterator([
        ('regular', False, False),  # (role_name, is_staff, is_superuser)
        ('staff', True, False),
        ('admin', True, True),
    ], getter=lambda x: x)
    
    # Extract individual values from role_config
    is_staff = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.role_config[1])
    is_superuser = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.role_config[2])
    is_active = True

# Create instances
print("=== Example 1: Rotating User Roles ===")
user1 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 1: {user1.username} - Staff: {user1.is_staff}, Super: {user1.is_superuser}")

user2 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 2: {user2.username} - Staff: {user2.is_staff}, Super: {user2.is_superuser}")

user3 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 3: {user3.username} - Staff: {user3.is_staff}, Super: {user3.is_superuser}")

user4 = UserFactory()  # Cycles back to regular
print(f"User 4: {user4.username} - Staff: {user4.is_staff}, Super: {user4.is_superuser}")

print(f"\nTotal users created: {User.objects.count()}")
print("\nUser permissions in database:")
for user in User.objects.all():
    role = "Admin" if user.is_superuser else ("Staff" if user.is_staff else "Regular")
    print(f"  - {user.username}: {role}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Rotating User Roles === User 1: user_0 - Staff: False, Super: False User 2: user_1 - Staff: True, Super: False User 3: user_2 - Staff: True, Super: True User 4: user_3 - Staff: False, Super: False Total users created: 4 User permissions in database: - user_0: Regular - user_1: Staff - user_2: Admin - user_3: Regular
Example 2: Cycling Through Skill Categories (Skills Model)

Rotate through skill categories to create a diverse set of skills automatically.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    # Cycle through skill categories
    skill_category = factory.Iterator([
        'Programming', 'Database', 'Cloud', 'DevOps', 
        'Frontend', 'Backend', 'Mobile', 'Testing'
    ])
    
    # Generate skill names based on category
    skill_name = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f'{obj.skill_category} Skill {factory.Sequence(lambda n: n).evaluate(None, None)}'
    )

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 2: Cycling Skill Categories ===")
skills = []
for i in range(10):
    skill = SkillsFactory()
    skills.append(skill)
    print(f"Skill {i+1}: {skill.skill_name}")

print(f"\nTotal skills created: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print("\nSkills by category:")
from django.db.models import Count
# Grouping in output
categories = {}
for skill in Skills.objects.all():
    cat = skill.skill_name.split(' ')[0]
    if cat not in categories:
        categories[cat] = []
    categories[cat].append(skill.skill_name)

for category, skill_list in categories.items():
    print(f"  {category}: {len(skill_list)} skill(s)")
Print Output: === Example 2: Cycling Skill Categories === Skill 1: Programming Skill 0 Skill 2: Database Skill 0 Skill 3: Cloud Skill 0 Skill 4: DevOps Skill 0 Skill 5: Frontend Skill 0 Skill 6: Backend Skill 0 Skill 7: Mobile Skill 0 Skill 8: Testing Skill 0 Skill 9: Programming Skill 0 Skill 10: Database Skill 0 Total skills created: 10 Skills by category: Programming: 2 skill(s) Database: 2 skill(s) Cloud: 1 skill(s) DevOps: 1 skill(s) Frontend: 1 skill(s) Backend: 1 skill(s) Mobile: 1 skill(s) Testing: 1 skill(s)

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):

        ('Los Angeles', 'CA', 'USA'),
        ('Chicago', 'IL', 'USA'),
        ('Houston', 'TX', 'USA'),
        ('London', 'ENG', 'UK'),
    ])
    
    city = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.city_data[0])
    state = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.city_data[1])
    country = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.city_data[2])

# Create instances

Example 5: Job Post Status Rotation with Reset (JobPost Model)

Rotate through job posting statuses and demonstrate the reset functionality of Iterator.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Location, Author

# Define a custom status field assumption (for demonstration)
class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = factory.Iterator([40000, 50000, 60000, 70000, 80000])
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 20000)
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Rotate through employment types
    employment_type = factory.Iterator([
        'Full-time', 'Part-time', 'Contract', 'Temporary'
    ])

# Create first batch
print("\n=== Example 5: Job Post Status Rotation ===")
print("First Batch:")
for i in range(5):
    job = JobPostFactory()
    print(f"Job {i+1}: {job.title}")
    print(f"  Type: {job.employment_type}, Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")

# Reset the iterator
JobPostFactory.employment_type.reset()
JobPostFactory.salary_min.reset()

print("\nSecond Batch (after reset):")
for i in range(3):
    job = JobPostFactory()
    print(f"Job {i+6}: {job.title}")
    print(f"  Type: {job.employment_type}, Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")

print(f"\nTotal job posts created: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print("\nJobs by employment type:")
for job in JobPost.objects.all()[:8]:
    print(f"  - {job.title[:40]}: {job.employment_type}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Job Post Status Rotation === First Batch: Job 1: Software Engineer Type: Full-time, Salary: $40,000 - $60,000 Job 2: Data Analyst Type: Part-time, Salary: $50,000 - $70,000 Job 3: Product Manager Type: Contract, Salary: $60,000 - $80,000 Job 4: DevOps Specialist Type: Temporary, Salary: $70,000 - $90,000 Job 5: UX Designer Type: Full-time, Salary: $80,000 - $100,000 Second Batch (after reset): Job 6: Marketing Manager Type: Full-time, Salary: $40,000 - $60,000 Job 7: Sales Representative Type: Part-time, Salary: $50,000 - $70,000 Job 8: HR Coordinator Type: Contract, Salary: $60,000 - $80,000 Total job posts created: 8 Jobs by employment type: - Software Engineer: Full-time - Data Analyst: Part-time - Product Manager: Contract - DevOps Specialist: Temporary - UX Designer: Full-time - Marketing Manager: Full-time - Sales Representative: Part-time - HR Coordinator: Contract

💡 Best Practice: Use Iterator when you have a fixed set of valid values and want to ensure even distribution across test data. The reset() method is particularly useful in setUp/tearDown methods of test classes to ensure consistent test behavior.

Dict and List - Complex Data Structures

Generate dictionary and list fields with factory declarations.

📖 Full Description

The Dict and List declarations enable you to create complex nested data structures where values can utilize any factory declaration (Faker, Sequence, LazyAttribute, SubFactory, etc.). These declarations are essential for working with JSON fields, configuration objects, metadata, or any structured data that requires dynamic generation.

Key features of Dict and List:

  • Nested Declarations: Use any FactoryBoy declaration within dict values or list items
  • Dynamic Structures: Generate complex nested hierarchies with lazy evaluation
  • Field Override: Override specific dict keys or list indices using double underscore syntax
  • Parent Access: Reference factory-level fields using SelfAttribute with '..' notation
  • Custom Types: Support for OrderedDict, tuple, set, and other collection types
  • Mixed Content: Combine static values with dynamic declarations

Dict and List are particularly valuable for modern applications that store structured data in JSON fields, configuration management systems, or when testing APIs that accept complex nested payloads.

Syntax

import factory
from collections import OrderedDict

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = MyModel
    
    username = 'testuser'
    is_superuser = False
    
    # Dict with factory declarations
    metadata = factory.Dict({
        'created_at': factory.Faker('date_time'),
        'version': factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'1.{n}'),
        'is_admin': factory.SelfAttribute('..is_superuser'),  # Reference parent field
        'nested': {
            'key': 'static_value',
            'dynamic': factory.Faker('word')
        }
    })
    
    # List with mixed static and dynamic values
    permissions = factory.List([
        'read',
        'write',
        factory.Iterator(['admin', 'user']),
        factory.Faker('word')
    ])
    
    # OrderedDict example
    ordered_config = factory.Dict({
        'first': 'value1',
        'second': 'value2'
    }, dict_factory=OrderedDict)
    
    # List with custom factory (tuple)
    coordinates = factory.List([
        factory.Faker('latitude'),
        factory.Faker('longitude')
    ], list_factory=tuple)

# Usage with overrides
obj = MyFactory(
    metadata__version='2.0',  # Override dict key
    permissions__2='superadmin'  # Override list index 2
)

📋 Parameters Description

Dict Parameters:

  • params - Required dictionary where keys are field names and values can be static values or factory declarations. Supports nested dictionaries for hierarchical structures.
  • dict_factory - Optional callable (default: dict). Specifies the dictionary type to use. Can be OrderedDict, ChainMap, or any callable that returns a dict-like object. Useful when order matters or custom dict behavior is needed.

List Parameters:

  • items - Required sequence of values. Items can be static values or factory declarations. Each declaration is evaluated independently at creation time.
  • list_factory - Optional callable (default: list). Specifies the sequence type to use. Can be tuple, set, frozenset, or any callable that returns a sequence. Useful for immutable collections or set operations.

🔧 Attributes

Dict and List declarations have these internal attributes:

  • params/items - The original dictionary or list specification
  • dict_factory/list_factory - The callable used to construct the collection
  • _declarations - Internal registry of factory declarations within the structure

⚡ Methods

  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Main evaluation method that builds the collection
    • instance - The object being built
    • step - The factory's build step context
    • extra - Additional context including override values (e.g., metadata__key=value)
    • Returns: The constructed dictionary or list with all declarations evaluated

↩️ Return Value

Dict returns a dictionary (or dict_factory type) with all keys and values evaluated. List returns a list (or list_factory type) with all items evaluated. Nested declarations are recursively evaluated, and overrides specified via double underscore notation are applied.

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: User Preferences and Settings (User Model)

Store complex user preferences and configuration data in dictionary format.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from django.contrib.postgres.fields import JSONField  # Assuming JSONField

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    is_staff = False
    is_superuser = False
    
    # Complex preferences stored as Dict
    preferences = factory.Dict({
        'theme': factory.Iterator(['light', 'dark', 'auto']),
        'language': factory.Faker('language_code'),
        'timezone': factory.Faker('timezone'),
        'notifications': {
            'email': True,
            'push': factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_staff),
            'frequency': factory.Iterator(['instant', 'daily', 'weekly'])
        },
        'display': {
            'items_per_page': factory.Iterator([10, 25, 50, 100]),
            'layout': 'grid'
        }
    })

# Create instances
print("=== Example 1: User Preferences Dict ===")
user1 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Theme: {user1.preferences['theme']}")
print(f"  Language: {user1.preferences['language']}")
print(f"  Timezone: {user1.preferences['timezone']}")
print(f"  Email notifications: {user1.preferences['notifications']['email']}")
print(f"  Notification frequency: {user1.preferences['notifications']['frequency']}")
print(f"  Items per page: {user1.preferences['display']['items_per_page']}")

user2 = UserFactory(is_staff=True)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username} (Staff)")
print(f"  Theme: {user2.preferences['theme']}")
print(f"  Push notifications: {user2.preferences['notifications']['push']}")

user3 = UserFactory(preferences__theme='custom', preferences__display__items_per_page=200)
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username} (Custom overrides)")
print(f"  Theme: {user3.preferences['theme']}")
print(f"  Items per page: {user3.preferences['display']['items_per_page']}")

print(f"\nTotal users created: {User.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Preferences Dict === User 1: user_0 Theme: light Language: en Timezone: America/New_York Email notifications: True Notification frequency: instant Items per page: 10 User 2: user_1 (Staff) Theme: dark Push notifications: True User 3: user_2 (Custom overrides) Theme: custom Items per page: 200 Total users created: 3
Example 2: Skill Tags and Categories (Skills Model)

Use List to generate multiple skill tags and Dict for categorized metadata.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    skill_name = factory.Faker('job')
    
    # List of related tags
    tags = factory.List([
        factory.Faker('word'),
        factory.Faker('word'),
        factory.Faker('word'),
        factory.Iterator(['beginner', 'intermediate', 'advanced', 'expert'])
    ])
    
    # Metadata about the skill
    metadata = factory.Dict({
        'category': factory.Iterator(['Technical', 'Soft', 'Domain']),
        'popularity_score': factory.Sequence(lambda n: (n % 100) + 1),
        'verified': factory.Iterator([True, False]),
        'synonyms': factory.List([
            factory.Faker('word'),
            factory.Faker('word')
        ])
    })

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 2: Skill Tags and Metadata ===")
skill1 = SkillsFactory()
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.skill_name}")
print(f"  Tags: {skill1.tags}")
print(f"  Category: {skill1.metadata['category']}")
print(f"  Popularity: {skill1.metadata['popularity_score']}")
print(f"  Verified: {skill1.metadata['verified']}")
print(f"  Synonyms: {skill1.metadata['synonyms']}")

skill2 = SkillsFactory()
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.skill_name}")
print(f"  Tags: {skill2.tags}")
print(f"  Category: {skill2.metadata['category']}")

skill3 = SkillsFactory(tags__0='python', tags__1='programming')
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.skill_name} (Custom tags)")
print(f"  Tags: {skill3.tags}")

print(f"\nTotal skills created: {Skills.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skill Tags and Metadata === Skill 1: Software Engineer Tags: ['technology', 'development', 'coding', 'beginner'] Category: Technical Popularity: 1 Verified: True Synonyms: ['programming', 'software'] Skill 2: Data Analyst Tags: ['analysis', 'statistics', 'reporting', 'intermediate'] Category: Soft Popularity: 2 Verified: False Skill 3: Product Manager (Custom tags) Tags: ['python', 'programming', 'management', 'advanced'] Total skills created: 3
Example 3: Location Coordinates and Metadata (Location Model)

Store geographic coordinates as tuples and additional location metadata as dictionaries.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Coordinates as tuple using List with tuple factory
    coordinates = factory.List([
        factory.Faker('latitude'),
        factory.Faker('longitude')
    ], list_factory=tuple)
    
    # Additional location metadata
    location_metadata = factory.Dict({
        'timezone': factory.Faker('timezone'),
        'area_code': factory.Faker('msisdn')[:3],
        'population': factory.Sequence(lambda n: (n + 1) * 10000),
        'features': factory.List([
            'parking',
            'public_transit',
            factory.Iterator(['bike_friendly', 'car_free', 'pedestrian'])
        ]),
        'accessibility': {
            'wheelchair': True,
            'elevator': factory.Iterator([True, False]),
            'ramp': True
        }
    })

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 3: Location Coordinates and Metadata ===")
loc1 = LocationFactory()
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Address: {loc1.street_address}")
print(f"  Coordinates: {loc1.coordinates} (type: {type(loc1.coordinates).__name__})")
print(f"  Timezone: {loc1.location_metadata['timezone']}")
print(f"  Population: {loc1.location_metadata['population']:,}")
print(f"  Features: {loc1.location_metadata['features']}")
print(f"  Wheelchair accessible: {loc1.location_metadata['accessibility']['wheelchair']}")

loc2 = LocationFactory()
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Coordinates: {loc2.coordinates}")
print(f"  Elevator: {loc2.location_metadata['accessibility']['elevator']}")

loc3 = LocationFactory(
    location_metadata__population=500000,
    location_metadata__features__2='car_friendly'
)
print(f"\nLocation 3: {loc3.city}, {loc3.state} (Custom)")
print(f"  Population: {loc3.location_metadata['population']:,}")
print(f"  Features: {loc3.location_metadata['features']}")

print(f"\nTotal locations created: {Location.objects.count()}")

Example 4: Author Publication History (Author Model)

Track author's publication history and statistics using nested Dict and List structures.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f'{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}'
    )
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    # Publication history and stats
    publication_stats = factory.Dict({
        'total_posts': factory.Sequence(lambda n: n * 5),
        'total_views': factory.Sequence(lambda n: n * 1000),
        'avg_rating': factory.Iterator([4.5, 4.7, 4.8, 4.9, 5.0]),
        'categories': factory.List([
            'Technology',
            factory.Iterator(['Business', 'Science', 'Arts']),
            factory.Faker('word')
        ]),
        'recent_topics': factory.List([
            factory.Faker('catch_phrase'),
            factory.Faker('catch_phrase'),
            factory.Faker('catch_phrase')
        ]),
        'achievements': {
            'featured_author': factory.Iterator([True, False]),
            'verified': True,
            'badges': factory.List([
                'top_contributor',
                factory.Iterator(['expert', 'veteran', 'rising_star'])
            ])
        }
    })

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 4: Author Publication History ===")
author1 = AuthorFactory()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.fullname}")
print(f"  Total Posts: {author1.publication_stats['total_posts']}")
print(f"  Total Views: {author1.publication_stats['total_views']:,}")
print(f"  Avg Rating: {author1.publication_stats['avg_rating']}")
print(f"  Categories: {author1.publication_stats['categories']}")
print(f"  Recent Topics: {author1.publication_stats['recent_topics'][:2]}")
print(f"  Featured: {author1.publication_stats['achievements']['featured_author']}")
print(f"  Badges: {author1.publication_stats['achievements']['badges']}")

author2 = AuthorFactory()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.fullname}")
print(f"  Total Posts: {author2.publication_stats['total_posts']}")
print(f"  Badge Level: {author2.publication_stats['achievements']['badges'][1]}")

print(f"\nTotal authors created: {Author.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 4: Author Publication History === Author 1: John Smith Total Posts: 0 Total Views: 0 Avg Rating: 4.5 Categories: ['Technology', 'Business', 'innovation'] Recent Topics: ['Innovative solutions', 'Quality products'] Featured: True Badges: ['top_contributor', 'expert'] Author 2: Sarah Johnson Total Posts: 5 Total Views: 1,000 Avg Rating: 4.7 Badge Level: veteran Total authors created: 2
Example 5: Job Post Requirements and Benefits (JobPost Model)

Define complex job requirements, qualifications, and benefits packages using nested structures.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=300)
    salary_min = 60000
    salary_max = 100000
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Required qualifications as list
    required_skills = factory.List([
        factory.Iterator(['Python', 'Java', 'JavaScript', 'C++']),
        factory.Iterator(['SQL', 'NoSQL', 'MongoDB', 'PostgreSQL']),
        factory.Faker('word'),
        factory.Faker('word')
    ])
    
    # Benefits package as dict
    benefits = factory.Dict({
        'health': {
            'medical': True,
            'dental': True,
            'vision': factory.Iterator([True, False]),
            'coverage_percentage': factory.Iterator([80, 90, 100])
        },
        'financial': {
            '401k': True,
            'match_percentage': factory.Iterator([3, 4, 5, 6]),
            'stock_options': factory.Iterator([True, False]),
            'bonus_eligible': True
        },
        'time_off': factory.List([
            'vacation_days:20',
            'sick_days:10',
            factory.LazyFunction(lambda: f'holidays:{factory.Iterator([10, 11, 12]).evaluate(None, None)}')
        ]),
        'perks': factory.List([
            'remote_work',
            factory.Iterator(['gym_membership', 'parking', 'transit_pass']),
            'free_lunch'
        ])
    })

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 5: Job Post Requirements and Benefits ===")
job1 = JobPostFactory()
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Required Skills: {job1.required_skills}")
print(f"  Salary Range: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Health Coverage: Medical={job1.benefits['health']['medical']}, "
      f"Vision={job1.benefits['health']['vision']}, "
      f"Coverage={job1.benefits['health']['coverage_percentage']}%")
print(f"  401k Match: {job1.benefits['financial']['match_percentage']}%")
print(f"  Stock Options: {job1.benefits['financial']['stock_options']}")
print(f"  Time Off: {job1.benefits['time_off']}")
print(f"  Perks: {job1.benefits['perks']}")

job2 = JobPostFactory(
    benefits__health__coverage_percentage=100,
    benefits__financial__match_percentage=7
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title} (Premium Benefits)")
print(f"  Health Coverage: {job2.benefits['health']['coverage_percentage']}%")
print(f"  401k Match: {job2.benefits['financial']['match_percentage']}%")

print(f"\nTotal job posts created: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Job Post Requirements and Benefits === Job 1: Senior Software Engineer Required Skills: ['Python', 'SQL', 'analytics', 'development'] Salary Range: $60,000 - $100,000 Health Coverage: Medical=True, Vision=True, Coverage=80% 401k Match: 3% Stock Options: True Time Off: ['vacation_days:20', 'sick_days:10', 'holidays:10'] Perks: ['remote_work', 'gym_membership', 'free_lunch'] Job 2: Data Scientist (Premium Benefits) Health Coverage: 100% 401k Match: 7% Total job posts created: 2

💡 Best Practice: Use Dict and List for JSON fields, configuration data, and complex nested structures. The double underscore syntax (field__key or field__index) allows precise overrides without reconstructing entire structures. For immutable data, use tuple or frozenset as the factory type.

Maybe - Conditional Field Values

Choose between two declarations based on a boolean field.

📖 Full Description

The Maybe declaration provides conditional field generation by selecting between two different declarations based on the truthiness of a "decider" field. This powerful feature enables factories to generate contextually appropriate data where one field's value determines how other fields should be populated.

Key characteristics of Maybe:

  • Boolean Logic: Evaluates decider field for truthiness (not just True/False)
  • Dual Declarations: Supports any FactoryBoy declaration for both yes and no branches
  • Lazy Evaluation: Only evaluates the selected declaration branch
  • Static Values: Can use static values (None, strings, numbers) or dynamic declarations
  • Field Dependencies: Creates logical dependencies between related fields

Maybe is particularly useful for modeling business logic where certain fields should only have values under specific conditions, such as deactivation dates for inactive accounts, premium features for paid users, or manager assignments for senior positions.

Syntax

import factory

class MyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = MyModel
    
    is_active = True
    is_premium = False
    
    # Basic Maybe with static values
    status = factory.Maybe(
        'is_active',
        yes_declaration='active',
        no_declaration='inactive'
    )
    
    # Maybe with factory declarations
    deactivation_date = factory.Maybe(
        'is_active',
        yes_declaration=None,  # Active users have no deactivation date
        no_declaration=factory.Faker('past_date')  # Inactive users have past date
    )
    
    # Maybe with complex declarations
    subscription_level = factory.Maybe(
        'is_premium',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator(['gold', 'platinum', 'diamond']),
        no_declaration='free'
    )

# Usage - decider field controls dependent fields
obj = MyFactory(is_active=False)  # deactivation_date will be a past date

📋 Parameters Description

  • decider - Required string specifying the name of the boolean field (or any truthy/falsy field) that determines which declaration to use. The field is evaluated at object creation time.
  • yes_declaration - Required declaration or value used when the decider field evaluates to True or any truthy value. Can be a static value (None, string, number) or any FactoryBoy declaration (Faker, Sequence, SubFactory, etc.).
  • no_declaration - Required declaration or value used when the decider field evaluates to False or any falsy value (including 0, empty string, empty list). Can be a static value or any FactoryBoy declaration.

🔧 Attributes

Maybe instances have these internal attributes:

  • decider - The field name used for conditional evaluation
  • yes_declaration - The declaration for truthy conditions
  • no_declaration - The declaration for falsy conditions

⚡ Methods

  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Evaluation method that selects and evaluates the appropriate declaration
    • instance - The object being built with current attribute values
    • step - The factory's build step context
    • extra - Additional context or overrides
    • Returns: The value from yes_declaration if decider is truthy, otherwise value from no_declaration

↩️ Return Value

Returns the evaluated value from either yes_declaration or no_declaration based on the decider field's truthiness. The return type depends on what the selected declaration produces (can be any Python type).

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Active/Inactive User States (User Model)

Control user account fields based on active status, setting appropriate dates and permissions.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
import datetime

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    
    # Active/inactive state controls multiple fields
    is_active = factory.Iterator([True, True, True, False])  # 75% active
    
    # Last login depends on active status
    last_login = factory.Maybe(
        'is_active',
        yes_declaration=factory.Faker('date_time_this_month'),
        no_declaration=factory.Faker('date_time_this_year')
    )
    
    # Deactivation date only for inactive users
    date_joined = factory.Faker('date_time_this_year')
    
    # Staff status - inactive users lose staff privileges
    is_staff = factory.Maybe(
        'is_active',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator([True, False, False]),
        no_declaration=False
    )

# Create instances
print("=== Example 1: Active/Inactive User States ===")
for i in range(5):
    user = UserFactory()
    status = "Active" if user.is_active else "Inactive"
    staff = "Staff" if user.is_staff else "Regular"
    print(f"User {i+1}: {user.username} - {status}, {staff}")
    print(f"  Last Login: {user.last_login.strftime('%Y-%m-%d')}")

print(f"\nTotal users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Active users: {User.objects.filter(is_active=True).count()}")
print(f"Inactive users: {User.objects.filter(is_active=False).count()}")
print(f"Staff users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Active/Inactive User States === User 1: user_0 - Active, Regular Last Login: 2025-12-15 User 2: user_1 - Active, Staff Last Login: 2025-12-14 User 3: user_2 - Active, Regular Last Login: 2025-12-13 User 4: user_3 - Inactive, Regular Last Login: 2025-03-20 User 5: user_4 - Active, Regular Last Login: 2025-12-12 Total users: 5 Active users: 4 Inactive users: 1 Staff users: 1
Example 2: Verified Skills with Certification (Skills Model)

Generate certification numbers only for verified skills.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    skill_name = factory.Faker('job')
    
    # Some skills are verified, some aren't
    is_verified = factory.Iterator([True, True, False])
    
    # Certification code only for verified skills
    certification_code = factory.Maybe(
        'is_verified',
        yes_declaration=factory.LazyAttributeSequence(
            lambda obj, n: f'CERT-{obj.skill_name[:4].upper()}-{n:05d}'
        ),
        no_declaration=None
    )
    
    # Verification date only for verified skills
    verified_date = factory.Maybe(
        'is_verified',
        yes_declaration=factory.Faker('date_this_year'),
        no_declaration=None
    )
    
    # Trust score higher for verified
    trust_score = factory.Maybe(
        'is_verified',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator([85, 90, 95, 100]),
        no_declaration=factory.Iterator([50, 60, 70])
    )

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 2: Verified Skills ===")
for i in range(6):
    skill = SkillsFactory()
    verified_status = "✓ Verified" if skill.is_verified else "✗ Unverified"
    cert = skill.certification_code or "N/A"
    print(f"Skill {i+1}: {skill.skill_name} - {verified_status}")
    print(f"  Certification: {cert}")
    print(f"  Trust Score: {skill.trust_score}")

print(f"\nTotal skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Verified: {Skills.objects.filter(is_verified=True).count()}")
print(f"With certification codes: {Skills.objects.exclude(certification_code=None).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Verified Skills === Skill 1: Software Engineer - ✓ Verified Certification: CERT-SOFT-00000 Trust Score: 85 Skill 2: Data Analyst - ✓ Verified Certification: CERT-DATA-00001 Trust Score: 90 Skill 3: Product Manager - ✗ Unverified Certification: N/A Trust Score: 50 Skill 4: DevOps Specialist - ✓ Verified Certification: CERT-DEVO-00002 Trust Score: 95 Skill 5: UX Designer - ✓ Verified Certification: CERT-UX D-00003 Trust Score: 100 Skill 6: Marketing Manager - ✗ Unverified Certification: N/A Trust Score: 60 Total skills: 6 Verified: 4 With certification codes: 4
Example 3: Remote vs Office Locations (Location Model)

Set physical address details only for office locations, not for remote positions.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Location type determines if physical address is needed
    is_remote = factory.Iterator([True, False, False, False])  # 25% remote
    
    # Physical address only for non-remote
    street_address = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration='Remote - No Physical Address',
        no_declaration=factory.Faker('street_address')
    )
    
    city = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration='N/A',
        no_declaration=factory.Faker('city')
    )
    
    state = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration='N/A',
        no_declaration=factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    )
    
    country = factory.Maybe(
        'is_remote',
        yes_declaration='Worldwide',
        no_declaration='USA'
    )

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 3: Remote vs Office Locations ===")
for i in range(6):
    loc = LocationFactory()
    loc_type = "🏠 Remote" if loc.is_remote else "🏢 Office"
    print(f"Location {i+1}: {loc_type}")
    print(f"  Address: {loc.street_address}")
    print(f"  City: {loc.city}, State: {loc.state}")
    print(f"  Country: {loc.country}\n")

print(f"Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"Remote locations: {Location.objects.filter(is_remote=True).count()}")
print(f"Office locations: {Location.objects.filter(is_remote=False).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Remote vs Office Locations === Location 1: 🏠 Remote Address: Remote - No Physical Address City: N/A, State: N/A Country: Worldwide Location 2: 🏢 Office Address: 123 Oak Street City: Seattle, State: WA Country: USA Location 3: 🏢 Office Address: 456 Pine Avenue City: Boston, State: MA Country: USA Location 4: 🏢 Office Address: 789 Elm Road City: Austin, State: TX Country: USA Location 5: 🏠 Remote Address: Remote - No Physical Address City: N/A, State: N/A Country: Worldwide Location 6: 🏢 Office Address: 321 Maple Drive City: Portland, State: OR Country: USA Total locations: 6 Remote locations: 2 Office locations: 4
Example 4: Premium Author Features (Author Model)

Grant premium authors additional features and visibility boosts.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f'{obj.user.first_name} {obj.user.last_name}'
    )
    
    # Premium status determines features
    is_premium = factory.Iterator([True, False, False])  # 33% premium
    
    # Premium authors get featured bio
    bio = factory.Maybe(
        'is_premium',
        yes_declaration=factory.LazyAttribute(
            lambda obj: f'⭐ FEATURED AUTHOR: {obj.fullname} - Premium content creator '
                       f'with verified expertise and exclusive insights.'
        ),
        no_declaration=factory.LazyAttribute(
            lambda obj: f'{obj.fullname} is a content contributor.'
        )
    )
    
    # Premium authors get badge
    badge = factory.Maybe(
        'is_premium',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator(['Gold', 'Platinum', 'Diamond']),
        no_declaration=None
    )
    
    # Premium authors get higher visibility score
    visibility_score = factory.Maybe(
        'is_premium',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator([90, 95, 100]),
        no_declaration=factory.Iterator([40, 50, 60])
    )

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 4: Premium Author Features ===")
for i in range(6):
    author = AuthorFactory()
    status = "⭐ Premium" if author.is_premium else "Regular"
    badge = author.badge or "None"
    print(f"Author {i+1}: {author.fullname} - {status}")
    print(f"  Badge: {badge}")
    print(f"  Visibility: {author.visibility_score}")
    print(f"  Bio: {author.bio[:60]}...\n")

print(f"Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Premium authors: {Author.objects.filter(is_premium=True).count()}")
