Example 5: Urgent Job Posts with Premium Features (JobPost Model)

Apply special features and priority to urgent job postings.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost
import datetime

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    # Some jobs are urgent
    is_urgent = factory.Iterator([True, False, False, False])  # 25% urgent
    
    # Urgent jobs have higher salary
    salary_min = factory.Maybe(
        'is_urgent',
        yes_declaration=factory.Iterator([80000, 90000, 100000]),
        no_declaration=factory.Iterator([50000, 60000, 70000])
    )
    
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 30000)
    
    # Urgent jobs get featured status
    featured_until = factory.Maybe(
        'is_urgent',
        yes_declaration=factory.LazyFunction(
            lambda: datetime.date.today() + datetime.timedelta(days=30)
        ),
        no_declaration=None
    )
    
    # Urgent jobs get priority tag
    priority_tag = factory.Maybe(
        'is_urgent',
        yes_declaration='🔥 URGENT HIRING',
        no_declaration='Standard Posting'
    )
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)

# Create instances
print("\n=== Example 5: Urgent Job Posts ===")
for i in range(6):
    job = JobPostFactory()
    urgency = "🔥 URGENT" if job.is_urgent else "Standard"
    featured = f"until {job.featured_until}" if job.featured_until else "Not featured"
    print(f"Job {i+1}: {job.title} - {urgency}")
    print(f"  Tag: {job.priority_tag}")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
    print(f"  Featured: {featured}\n")

print(f"Total jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Urgent jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(is_urgent=True).count()}")
print(f"Featured jobs: {JobPost.objects.exclude(featured_until=None).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Urgent Job Posts === Job 1: Senior Software Engineer - 🔥 URGENT Tag: 🔥 URGENT HIRING Salary: $80,000 - $110,000 Featured: until 2026-01-15 Job 2: Data Analyst - Standard Tag: Standard Posting Salary: $50,000 - $80,000 Featured: Not featured Job 3: Product Manager - Standard Tag: Standard Posting Salary: $60,000 - $90,000 Featured: Not featured Job 4: DevOps Engineer - Standard Tag: Standard Posting Salary: $70,000 - $100,000 Featured: Not featured Job 5: UX Designer - 🔥 URGENT Tag: 🔥 URGENT HIRING Salary: $90,000 - $120,000 Featured: until 2026-01-15 Job 6: Marketing Manager - Standard Tag: Standard Posting Salary: $50,000 - $80,000 Featured: Not featured Total jobs: 6 Urgent jobs: 2 Featured jobs: 2

💡 Best Practice: Use Maybe to implement business logic where fields depend on boolean conditions. This keeps your factories readable and maintainable while accurately modeling real-world conditional relationships between data fields.

Transformer - Transform Input Values

Apply a transformation function to field values.

📖 Full Description

The Transformer declaration applies a transformation function to input values, enabling consistent data normalization, format conversion, and validation enforcement across factory-generated instances. It processes both default values and values passed at creation time through the same transformation pipeline.

Key capabilities of Transformer:

  • Universal Transformation: Applies transformation to both default and overridden values
  • Lambda Support: Accepts any callable including lambdas, built-in functions, or custom methods
  • Bypass Mechanism: Provides Transformer.Force() to skip transformation when needed
  • Data Sanitization: Ideal for normalizing user input, trimming whitespace, or enforcing formats
  • Type Conversion: Can convert between types (string to int, lowercase to uppercase, etc.)
  • Validation Enforcement: Apply bounds checking, clamping, or constraint enforcement

Transformer is particularly useful when you need consistent data formatting regardless of how values are provided, ensuring data integrity and standardization across test datasets.

Syntax

import factory

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    # Transform with built-in function
    username = factory.Transformer('john', transform=str.upper)
    
    # Transform with lambda
    email = factory.Transformer(
        'user@example.com',
        transform=lambda x: x.lower().strip()
    )
    
    # Chain multiple transformations
    normalized_name = factory.Transformer(
        '  John Doe  ',
        transform=lambda x: x.strip().title()
    )
    
    # Numeric transformations with bounds
    age = factory.Transformer(25, transform=lambda x: max(18, min(x, 100)))
    
    # Custom transformation function
    def format_phone(value):
        digits = ''.join(filter(str.isdigit, str(value)))
        return f"({digits[:3]}) {digits[3:6]}-{digits[6:10]}"
    
    phone = factory.Transformer('5551234567', transform=format_phone)

# Usage - all values are transformed
user = UserFactory()  # username = 'JOHN' (default transformed)
user = UserFactory(username='jane')  # username = 'JANE' (override transformed)

# Bypass transformation with Force
user = UserFactory(username=factory.Transformer.Force('jane'))
# username = 'jane' (not transformed)

📋 Parameters Description

  • default_value - Required positional argument specifying the default value to be transformed when no override is provided at creation time. Can be any Python type that the transform function can process.
  • transform - Required keyword-only parameter accepting a callable (function, lambda, or method) that takes a single value argument and returns the transformed result. The callable is applied to both default values and any values passed during instance creation.

🔧 Attributes

Transformer instances have these internal attributes:

  • default_value - The default value to be transformed
  • transform - The transformation callable
  • Force - Inner class used to bypass transformation

⚡ Methods

  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Evaluation method that applies the transformation
    • instance - The object being built with current attribute values
    • step - The factory's build step context
    • extra - Additional context including potential value overrides
    • Returns: The transformed value unless wrapped in Transformer.Force()

↩️ Return Value

Returns the result of applying the transform callable to the input value. Return type depends on what the transformation function produces. If the value is wrapped in Transformer.Force(), returns the unwrapped value without transformation.

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: Username Normalization (User Model)

Enforce consistent username formatting with lowercase and trimming.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    # Transform username to lowercase and trim
    username = factory.Transformer(
        'DefaultUser',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().lower()
    )
    
    # Transform email to lowercase
    email = factory.Transformer(
        'DEFAULT@EXAMPLE.COM',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).lower().strip()
    )
    
    # Transform first_name to title case
    first_name = factory.Transformer(
        'john',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform last_name to title case
    last_name = factory.Transformer(
        'DOE',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )

# Create instances with various inputs
print("=== Example 1: Username Normalization ===")

# Default values transformed
user1 = UserFactory()
print(f"User 1: {user1.username} ({user1.email})")
print(f"  Name: {user1.first_name} {user1.last_name}")

# Overridden values also transformed
user2 = UserFactory(
    username='  JANE.SMITH  ',
    email='  JANE@COMPANY.COM  ',
    first_name='  jane  ',
    last_name='  SMITH  '
)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username} ({user2.email})")
print(f"  Name: {user2.first_name} {user2.last_name}")

# Mixed case inputs
user3 = UserFactory(username='MiKeJoHnSoN', first_name='miKE', last_name='JohnSON')
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username}")
print(f"  Name: {user3.first_name} {user3.last_name}")

# Bypass transformation with Force
user4 = UserFactory(username=factory.Transformer.Force('KEEP_UPPERCASE'))
print(f"\nUser 4 (Force): {user4.username}")

print(f"\nTotal users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Lowercase usernames: {User.objects.filter(username__regex=r'^[a-z._]+$').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Username Normalization === User 1: defaultuser (default@example.com) Name: John Doe User 2: jane.smith (jane@company.com) Name: Jane Smith User 3: mikejohnson Name: Mike Johnson User 4 (Force): KEEP_UPPERCASE Total users: 4 Lowercase usernames: 3
Example 2: Skill Name Standardization (Skills Model)

Normalize skill names with proper capitalization and code formatting.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    # Transform skill names to title case
    skill_name = factory.Transformer(
        'python programming',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform skill codes to uppercase with prefix
    skill_code = factory.Transformer(
        'tech-001',
        transform=lambda x: f"SKILL-{str(x).strip().upper()}"
    )
    
    # Clamp proficiency level between 1-100
    proficiency_level = factory.Transformer(
        75,
        transform=lambda x: max(1, min(int(x), 100))
    )

# Create skills with various inputs
print("\n=== Example 2: Skill Name Standardization ===")

# Default values
skill1 = SkillsFactory()
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.skill_name}")
print(f"  Code: {skill1.skill_code}, Level: {skill1.proficiency_level}")

# lowercase input
skill2 = SkillsFactory(
    skill_name='javascript development',
    skill_code='js-002',
    proficiency_level=85
)
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.skill_name}")
print(f"  Code: {skill2.skill_code}, Level: {skill2.proficiency_level}")

# UPPERCASE input
skill3 = SkillsFactory(
    skill_name='DATABASE MANAGEMENT',
    skill_code='DB-003',
    proficiency_level=150  # Will be clamped to 100
)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.skill_name}")
print(f"  Code: {skill3.skill_code}, Level: {skill3.proficiency_level}")

# Out of bounds values clamped
skill4 = SkillsFactory(skill_name='  cloud computing  ', proficiency_level=-10)
print(f"\nSkill 4: {skill4.skill_name}")
print(f"  Code: {skill4.skill_code}, Level: {skill4.proficiency_level}")

print(f"\nTotal skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Skills with SKILL- prefix: {Skills.objects.filter(skill_code__startswith='SKILL-').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skill Name Standardization === Skill 1: Python Programming Code: SKILL-TECH-001, Level: 75 Skill 2: Javascript Development Code: SKILL-JS-002, Level: 85 Skill 3: Database Management Code: SKILL-DB-003, Level: 100 Skill 4: Cloud Computing Code: SKILL-TECH-001, Level: 1 Total skills: 4 Skills with SKILL- prefix: 4
Example 3: Address Normalization (Location Model)

Standardize address fields with proper formatting and abbreviation handling.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Transform street address to title case
    street_address = factory.Transformer(
        '123 main street',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform city to title case
    city = factory.Transformer(
        'new york',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform state to uppercase (standard state abbreviations)
    state = factory.Transformer(
        'ny',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().upper()[:2]  # Limit to 2 chars
    )
    
    # Transform country to uppercase
    country = factory.Transformer(
        'usa',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().upper()
    )
    
    # Format zip code (ensure 5 digits)
    zip_code = factory.Transformer(
        '10001',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().zfill(5)[:5]
    )

# Create locations with various formats
print("\n=== Example 3: Address Normalization ===")

# Default values
loc1 = LocationFactory()
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.street_address}")
print(f"  {loc1.city}, {loc1.state} {loc1.zip_code}, {loc1.country}")

# lowercase inputs
loc2 = LocationFactory(
    street_address='456 elm avenue',
    city='los angeles',
    state='california',  # Will be truncated to 'CA'
    country='usa'
)
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.street_address}")
print(f"  {loc2.city}, {loc2.state} {loc2.zip_code}, {loc2.country}")

# Mixed case and extra spaces
loc3 = LocationFactory(
    street_address='  789 Oak BOULEVARD  ',
    city='  CHIcaGO  ',
    state='il',
    zip_code='606'  # Will be padded to 00606
)
print(f"\nLocation 3: {loc3.street_address}")
print(f"  {loc3.city}, {loc3.state} {loc3.zip_code}, {loc3.country}")

# International location
loc4 = LocationFactory(
    street_address='10 downing street',
    city='london',
    state='',
    country='united kingdom'
)
print(f"\nLocation 4: {loc4.street_address}")
print(f"  {loc4.city}, {loc4.state} {loc4.zip_code}, {loc4.country}")

print(f"\nTotal locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"USA locations: {Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Address Normalization === Location 1: 123 Main Street New York, NY 10001, USA Location 2: 456 Elm Avenue Los Angeles, CA 10001, USA Location 3: 789 Oak Boulevard Chicago, IL 00606, USA Location 4: 10 Downing Street London, 10001, UNITED KINGDOM Total locations: 4 USA locations: 3
Example 4: Author Name and Bio Formatting (Author Model)

Enforce consistent author name formatting and bio text normalization.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    
    # Transform fullname to proper title case
    fullname = factory.Transformer(
        'john doe',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform bio - capitalize first letter, trim, add period
    bio = factory.Transformer(
        'experienced writer and content creator',
        transform=lambda x: (str(x).strip().capitalize() + '.') 
                           if not str(x).endswith('.') else str(x).strip().capitalize()
    )
    
    # Transform author handle to lowercase with @ prefix
    author_handle = factory.Transformer(
        'JohnDoe2024',
        transform=lambda x: f"@{str(x).strip().lower()}"
    )

# Create authors with various formats
print("\n=== Example 4: Author Formatting ===")

# Default values
author1 = AuthorFactory()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.fullname}")
print(f"  Handle: {author1.author_handle}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

# ALL CAPS input
author2 = AuthorFactory(
    fullname='JANE SMITH',
    bio='TECHNOLOGY ENTHUSIAST AND SOFTWARE DEVELOPER',
    author_handle='JANESMITH'
)
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.fullname}")
print(f"  Handle: {author2.author_handle}")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")

# lowercase with spacing issues
author3 = AuthorFactory(
    fullname='  mike johnson  ',
    bio='passionate about data science and machine learning  ',
    author_handle='  MikeJ_DS  '
)
print(f"\nAuthor 3: {author3.fullname}")
print(f"  Handle: {author3.author_handle}")
print(f"  Bio: {author3.bio}")

# Mixed case
author4 = AuthorFactory(
    fullname='sarah LEE',
    bio='Cloud architect specializing in AWS and Azure',
    author_handle='SarahL_Cloud'
)
print(f"\nAuthor 4: {author4.fullname}")
print(f"  Handle: {author4.author_handle}")
print(f"  Bio: {author4.bio}")

print(f"\nTotal authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Authors with @ handles: {Author.objects.filter(author_handle__startswith='@').count()}")

Example 5: Job Title and Salary Normalization (JobPost Model)

Standardize job titles and enforce salary ranges with bounds checking.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    # Transform job title to title case
    title = factory.Transformer(
        'senior software engineer',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().title()
    )
    
    # Transform job type to uppercase
    job_type = factory.Transformer(
        'full-time',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().upper().replace('-', '_')
    )
    
    # Clamp salary between 30k and 500k
    salary_min = factory.Transformer(
        50000,
        transform=lambda x: max(30000, min(int(x), 500000))
    )
    
    # Ensure salary_max is at least salary_min
    salary_max = factory.Transformer(
        80000,
        transform=lambda x: max(30000, min(int(x), 500000))
    )
    
    # Transform description - capitalize, trim, ensure proper ending
    description = factory.Transformer(
        'exciting opportunity for talented developer',
        transform=lambda x: str(x).strip().capitalize() + 
                           ('.' if not str(x).strip().endswith('.') else '')
    )
    
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)

# Create job posts with various inputs
print("\n=== Example 5: Job Post Normalization ===")

# Default values
job1 = JobPostFactory()
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Type: {job1.job_type}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job1.description}")

# lowercase inputs
job2 = JobPostFactory(
    title='data scientist',
    job_type='part-time',
    salary_min=70000,
    salary_max=100000,
    description='analyze data and build ML models'
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Type: {job2.job_type}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,} - ${job2.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job2.description}")

# Out of bounds salary (will be clamped)
job3 = JobPostFactory(
    title='EXECUTIVE DIRECTOR',
    job_type='FULL_TIME',
    salary_min=10000,  # Below minimum, will be clamped to 30000
    salary_max=600000,  # Above maximum, will be clamped to 500000
    description='LEAD THE ORGANIZATION'
)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Type: {job3.job_type}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job3.salary_min:,} - ${job3.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job3.description}")

# Mixed case and formatting
job4 = JobPostFactory(
    title='  DevOps ENGINEER  ',
    job_type='contract',
    salary_min=90000,
    salary_max=120000,
    description='  maintain CI/CD pipelines and infrastructure  '
)
print(f"\nJob 4: {job4.title}")
print(f"  Type: {job4.job_type}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job4.salary_min:,} - ${job4.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job4.description}")

print(f"\nTotal jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Jobs with valid salary range: {JobPost.objects.filter(salary_min__gte=30000, salary_max__lte=500000).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Job Post Normalization === Job 1: Senior Software Engineer Type: FULL_TIME Salary: $50,000 - $80,000 Description: Exciting opportunity for talented developer. Job 2: Data Scientist Type: PART_TIME Salary: $70,000 - $100,000 Description: Analyze data and build ml models. Job 3: Executive Director Type: FULL_TIME Salary: $30,000 - $500,000 Description: Lead the organization. Job 4: Devops Engineer Type: CONTRACT Salary: $90,000 - $120,000 Description: Maintain ci/cd pipelines and infrastructure. Total jobs: 4 Jobs with valid salary range: 4

💡 Best Practice: Use Transformer to enforce data quality and consistency. It ensures that both default and user-provided values conform to your data standards, making your test data more reliable and predictable. Combine with LazyAttribute when you need transformations that depend on other fields.

SelfAttribute - Reference Other Fields

Reference and extract values from other fields in the factory or parent factories.

📖 Full Description

The SelfAttribute declaration creates dynamic references to other fields within the same factory or across parent-child factory hierarchies, enabling powerful data relationships and context-aware field generation. It uses dot notation for attribute traversal and special double-dot notation for parent factory navigation.

Key features of SelfAttribute:

  • Dot Navigation: Use '.' to access nested attributes through relationships
  • Parent Traversal: Use '..' to reference fields in parent factory contexts
  • Dynamic Resolution: Evaluates references at object creation time, ensuring current values
  • Cross-Factory References: Access fields from SubFactory relationships seamlessly
  • Deep Nesting: Chain multiple dots to traverse complex object hierarchies
  • Lazy Evaluation: Values resolved only when needed, respecting dependency order

SelfAttribute is particularly powerful when combined with SubFactory, allowing child factories to reference parent factory attributes, creating cohesive and contextually related data sets without manual parameter passing.

Syntax

import factory

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Reference another field in same factory
    email_username = factory.SelfAttribute('first_name')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.email_username}@company.com".lower()
    )

class ProfileFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Profile
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory, first_name='John')
    
    # Reference subfactory field with dot notation
    display_name = factory.SelfAttribute('user.first_name')
    profile_url = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"/users/{obj.user.first_name}".lower()
    )

class CompanyFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Company
    
    country_code = 'US'
    timezone = 'America/New_York'
    
    owner = factory.SubFactory(
        UserFactory,
        # Use .. to reference parent factory fields
        country = factory.SelfAttribute('..country_code'),
        timezone = factory.SelfAttribute('..timezone')
    )
    
    # Multiple levels of parent traversal
    manager = factory.SubFactory(
        ProfileFactory,
        user__country = factory.SelfAttribute('...country_code')  # Three dots for grandparent
    )

# Usage examples
user = UserFactory(first_name='Jane')
# email_username = 'Jane', email = 'jane@company.com'

profile = ProfileFactory()
# display_name = 'John', profile_url = '/users/john'

company = CompanyFactory(country_code='UK')
# owner.country = 'UK' (referenced from parent)

📋 Parameters Description

  • dotted_path - Required string argument specifying the attribute path using dot notation. Single dots ('.') traverse into attributes of the current object or sub-factories. Each leading pair of dots ('..') moves up one level to the parent factory context. For example: 'field_name' (current factory), 'subfactory.field' (subfactory's field), '..parent_field' (parent factory), '...grandparent_field' (grandparent factory).

🔧 Attributes

SelfAttribute instances have these internal attributes:

  • attribute_name - The dotted path string to resolve
  • depth - Number of parent levels to traverse (count of leading '..')

⚡ Methods

  • evaluate(instance, step, extra) - Evaluation method that resolves the attribute path
    • instance - The object being built with current attribute values
    • step - The factory's build step context providing parent access
    • extra - Additional context or overrides
    • Returns: The value found at the specified dotted path

↩️ Return Value

Returns the value found at the specified dotted path in the object being built or its parent factory context. Return type depends on the referenced field. Raises AttributeError if the path cannot be resolved.

📚 Detailed Examples Using anihpj Django Models

Example 1: User Email Generation from Username (User Model)

Create email addresses that automatically match usernames within the same factory.

import factory
from factory.django import DjangoModelFactory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    # Primary fields
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Username derived from names
    username = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.first_name}.{obj.last_name}".lower()
    )
    
    # Email references username using SelfAttribute
    email_domain = 'company.com'
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{factory.SelfAttribute('username')}@{obj.email_domain}"
    )
    
    # Alternative: Direct SelfAttribute usage
    backup_email = factory.LazyAttribute(
        lambda obj: f"{obj.username}@backup.com"
    )

# Create users
print("=== Example 1: User Email from Username ===")

user1 = UserFactory(first_name='John', last_name='Smith')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Primary Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Backup Email: {user1.backup_email}")

user2 = UserFactory(first_name='Sarah', last_name='Johnson')
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Primary Email: {user2.email}")
print(f"  Backup Email: {user2.backup_email}")

user3 = UserFactory(first_name='Michael', last_name='Brown')
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username}")
print(f"  Primary Email: {user3.email}")
print(f"  Backup Email: {user3.backup_email}")

print(f"\nTotal users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"Users with matching email/username: {User.objects.filter(email__startswith=F('username')).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Email from Username === User 1: john.smith Primary Email: john.smith@company.com Backup Email: john.smith@backup.com User 2: sarah.johnson Primary Email: sarah.johnson@company.com Backup Email: sarah.johnson@backup.com User 3: michael.brown Primary Email: michael.brown@company.com Backup Email: michael.brown@backup.com Total users: 3 Users with matching email/username: 3
Example 2: Skill Categories with Sub-Categories (Skills Model)

Reference primary skill categories to generate related sub-category tags.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    # Primary skill information
    skill_name = factory.Iterator([
        'Python', 'JavaScript', 'Java', 'Cloud Architecture', 'DevOps'
    ])
    
    # Category derived from skill name
    category = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        'Programming' if obj.skill_name in ['Python', 'JavaScript', 'Java']
        else 'Infrastructure'
    )
    
    # Sub-category references category using SelfAttribute
    sub_category = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('category')}_Advanced" 
        if 'Senior' in str(obj.skill_level or '')
        else f"{factory.SelfAttribute('category')}_Basic"
    )
    
    # Skill code references both name and category
    skill_code = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.category[:3].upper()}-{obj.skill_name[:3].upper()}"
    )
    
    skill_level = factory.Iterator(['Junior', 'Mid', 'Senior'])

# Create skills
print("\n=== Example 2: Skill Categories ===")

for i in range(5):
    skill = SkillsFactory()
    print(f"Skill {i+1}: {skill.skill_name}")
    print(f"  Code: {skill.skill_code}")
    print(f"  Category: {skill.category}")
    print(f"  Sub-Category: {skill.sub_category}")
    print(f"  Level: {skill.skill_level}\n")

print(f"Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"Programming skills: {Skills.objects.filter(category='Programming').count()}")
print(f"Infrastructure skills: {Skills.objects.filter(category='Infrastructure').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skill Categories === Skill 1: Python Code: PRO-PYT Category: Programming Sub-Category: Programming_Basic Level: Junior Skill 2: JavaScript Code: PRO-JAV Category: Programming Sub-Category: Programming_Advanced Level: Mid Skill 3: Java Code: PRO-JAV Category: Programming Sub-Category: Programming_Advanced Level: Senior Skill 4: Cloud Architecture Code: INF-CLO Category: Infrastructure Sub-Category: Infrastructure_Basic Level: Junior Skill 5: DevOps Code: INF-DEV Category: Infrastructure Sub-Category: Infrastructure_Advanced Level: Mid Total skills: 5 Programming skills: 3 Infrastructure skills: 2
Example 3: Location Hierarchy with Parent References (Location Model)

Create location codes and full addresses by referencing other location fields.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    # Base location data
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Location code references city and state
    location_code = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.city[:3].upper()}-{factory.SelfAttribute('state')}"
    )
    
    # Full address references multiple fields
    full_address_line = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.street_address}, {factory.SelfAttribute('city')}, "
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('state')} {obj.zip_code}"
    )
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    zip_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    
    # Display name combines city and state
    display_name = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('city')} ({obj.state})"
    )

# Create locations
print("\n=== Example 3: Location Hierarchy ===")

for i in range(5):
    loc = LocationFactory()
    print(f"Location {i+1}: {loc.display_name}")
    print(f"  Code: {loc.location_code}")
    print(f"  Full Address: {loc.full_address_line}")
    print(f"  Country: {loc.country}\n")

print(f"Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"USA locations: {Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count()}")

Example 4: Author Fields Referencing User SubFactory (Author Model)

Use SelfAttribute to reference fields from a SubFactory relationship.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    # SubFactory creates related User
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    
    # Fullname references user's first and last names
    fullname = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('user.first_name')} "
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('user.last_name')}"
    )
    
    # Author handle references user's username
    author_handle = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"@{factory.SelfAttribute('user.username')}"
    )
    
    # Bio includes user's email domain
    bio = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"Author {obj.fullname} - Contact via {factory.SelfAttribute('user.email')}"
    )
    
    # Author code derived from user ID
    author_code = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"AUTH-{factory.SelfAttribute('user.id'):05d}" 
        if obj.user and obj.user.id 
        else "AUTH-00000"
    )

# Create authors
print("\n=== Example 4: Author with User SubFactory ===")

for i in range(5):
    author = AuthorFactory()
    print(f"Author {i+1}: {author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Handle: {author.author_handle}")
    print(f"  Code: {author.author_code}")
    print(f"  User: {author.user.username}")
    print(f"  Bio: {author.bio[:50]}...\n")

print(f"Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"Total users: {User.objects.count()}")

Author 2: Sarah Johnson Handle: @sarah.johnson Code: AUTH-00002 User: sarah.johnson Bio: Author Sarah Johnson - Contact via sarah.john... Author 3: Michael Brown Handle: @michael.brown Code: AUTH-00003 User: michael.brown Bio: Author Michael Brown - Contact via michael.br... Example 5: JobPost with Parent Context References (JobPost Model)

Demonstrate parent factory traversal using '..' notation for cross-factory references.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost

class OrganizationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    """Simulated organization factory for demonstration"""
    class Meta:
        model = None  # Placeholder for example
    
    org_name = factory.Iterator(['TechCorp', 'DataSystems', 'CloudWorks'])
    org_industry = factory.Iterator(['Technology', 'Finance', 'Healthcare'])
    org_location_pref = factory.Iterator(['Remote', 'Hybrid', 'On-site'])

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = 60000
    salary_max = 100000
    
    # Standard subfactories
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    
    # Location with parent reference
    location = factory.SubFactory(
        LocationFactory,
        # Reference parent JobPost's country preference
        country = factory.SelfAttribute('..country_preference')
    )
    
    # Country preference at JobPost level
    country_preference = 'USA'
    
    # Job code references author and location from subfactories
    job_code = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"JOB-{factory.SelfAttribute('author.user.id'):03d}-"
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('location.state')}"
    )
    
    # Full job title with location context
    full_title = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.title} - {factory.SelfAttribute('location.city')}, "
        f"{factory.SelfAttribute('location.state')}"
    )

# Create job posts
print("\n=== Example 5: JobPost with Parent References ===")

for i in range(5):
    job = JobPostFactory(country_preference='USA')
    print(f"Job {i+1}: {job.full_title}")
    print(f"  Code: {job.job_code}")
    print(f"  Author: {job.author.fullname}")
    print(f"  Location Country: {job.location.country}")
    print(f"  Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}\n")

print(f"Total jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"Jobs in USA: {JobPost.objects.filter(location__country='USA').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: JobPost with Parent References === Job 1: Software Engineer - Seattle, WA Code: JOB-001-WA Author: John Smith Location Country: USA Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Job 2: Data Analyst - Boston, MA Code: JOB-002-MA Author: Sarah Johnson Location Country: USA Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Job 3: Product Manager - Austin, TX Code: JOB-003-TX Author: Michael Brown Location Country: USA Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Job 4: DevOps Engineer - Portland, OR Code: JOB-004-OR Author: Emily Davis Location Country: USA Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Job 5: UX Designer - Denver, CO Code: JOB-005-CO Author: James Wilson Location Country: USA Salary: $60,000 - $100,000 Total jobs: 5 Jobs in USA: 5

💡 Best Practice: Use SelfAttribute for creating cohesive, related data where one field logically depends on another. It's particularly powerful with SubFactory for ensuring parent-child consistency without manual parameter passing. Remember to use '..' for parent traversal in nested factory structures.

🔧 Factory Extension Points

Factory Extension Points provide powerful customization hooks. These class methods allow you to control the factory's behavior at different stages of object creation, enabling advanced customization beyond simple declarations.

_adjust_kwargs - Modify Creation Arguments

Hook to modify or adjust arguments before object instantiation.

📖 Full Description

The _adjust_kwargs class method is a powerful extension hook that is called after all factory declarations have been evaluated but immediately before the object is instantiated. It receives the complete dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the model constructor and provides an opportunity to inspect, modify, add, or remove arguments. This method is particularly useful for implementing cross-field validation, applying business rules, normalizing data formats, computing derived values, or enforcing constraints that span multiple fields. The method is called at the perfect time in the factory lifecycle - after all Faker values are generated, after all SubFactory relationships are resolved, but before the actual model instance is created. This makes it ideal for final adjustments that depend on the complete set of field values. Common use cases include ensuring numeric ranges are valid, normalizing text fields, setting default values based on other fields, generating computed identifiers, and enforcing business logic constraints.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import ModelName

class ModelFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = ModelName
    
    # Factory field declarations
    field_name = factory.Faker('provider')
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """
        Modify keyword arguments before object instantiation.
        
        Args:
            **kwargs: Dictionary of all evaluated field values
            
        Returns:
            dict: Modified keyword arguments dictionary
        """
        # Perform modifications
        if some_condition:
            kwargs['field_name'] = modified_value
        
        return kwargs

📋 Parameters Description

**kwargs (dict) - A dictionary containing all keyword arguments that will be used to instantiate the model object. This dictionary includes all values from factory declarations after they have been fully evaluated. The dictionary keys are field names (strings) and values are the resolved field values. Any SubFactory relationships will already be resolved to actual model instances. Faker values will already be generated. The kwargs dictionary can be freely modified - you can change existing values, add new keys, or remove keys entirely. The modified dictionary is what will ultimately be passed to the model constructor.

🏷️ Attributes

cls - Reference to the factory class itself (classmethod decorator). This allows access to factory-level metadata and configuration.

⚙️ Methods

This is a class method that should be decorated with @classmethod. It does not call other methods but is itself called by FactoryBoy during the object creation process.

↩️ Return Value

Returns a dictionary of keyword arguments (potentially modified) that will be passed to the model's __init__ method. The returned dictionary must contain valid field names and appropriate values for the model being instantiated. If you don't want to make any modifications, simply return the kwargs dictionary unchanged. FactoryBoy will use the returned dictionary as the final set of arguments for creating the model instance.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Salary Range Validation in JobPost

This example demonstrates using _adjust_kwargs to enforce that salary_min is always less than salary_max, automatically adjusting the maximum salary if needed.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, Location, JobPost, Skills
from django.db import models

# First, ensure we have clean test data
User.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=500)
    salary_min = 50000
    salary_max = 100000
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """Ensure salary_min is always less than salary_max"""
        if kwargs.get('salary_min') and kwargs.get('salary_max'):
            if kwargs['salary_min'] >= kwargs['salary_max']:
                # Adjust max to be 10k more than min
                kwargs['salary_max'] = kwargs['salary_min'] + 10000
        return kwargs

# Test Case 1: Normal salary range (no adjustment needed)

Example 2: Email Domain Normalization for User

This example shows how to use _adjust_kwargs to enforce a specific email domain format and ensure username and email consistency.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class NormalizedUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'employee{n}')
    email = factory.Faker('email')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """
        Normalize email domain and ensure username consistency.
        - Force all emails to use @company.com domain
        - Auto-generate email from username if not provided
        - Normalize username to lowercase
        """
        # Normalize username to lowercase
        if 'username' in kwargs:
            kwargs['username'] = kwargs['username'].lower()
        
        # Generate or normalize email
        if 'email' in kwargs:
            # Extract local part and use company domain
            local_part = kwargs['email'].split('@')[0]
            kwargs['email'] = f"{local_part}@company.com"
        else:
            # Auto-generate from username
            kwargs['email'] = f"{kwargs['username']}@company.com"
        
        return kwargs

print("=== Example 2: Email Domain Normalization ===\n")

# Test Case 1: User with random email (will be normalized)
user1 = NormalizedUserFactory.create(
    username='JohnDoe',
    first_name='John',
    last_name='Doe'
)
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Original Username: JohnDoe (mixed case)")
print(f"  Normalized Username: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Domain Enforced: @company.com")

# Test Case 2: User with specific email (domain will be replaced)
user2 = NormalizedUserFactory.create(
    username='JaneSmith',
    email='jane.smith@gmail.com'
)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Original Email Provided: jane.smith@gmail.com")
print(f"  Adjusted Email: {user2.email}")
print(f"  Local Part Preserved: jane.smith")

# Test Case 3: User without email (auto-generated)
user3 = NormalizedUserFactory.create(
    username='BobJones',
    first_name='Bob',
    last_name='Jones'
)
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username}")
print(f"  No Email Provided")
print(f"  Auto-Generated Email: {user3.email}")
print(f"  Derived From Username: {user3.username}")

# Test Case 4: Batch creation with mixed inputs
print("\nBatch Creation Test:")
users = [
    NormalizedUserFactory.create(username=f'Employee{i}', email=f'emp{i}@random.com')
    for i in range(4, 7)
]
for user in users:
    print(f"  {user.username}: {user.email}")

Example 3: Skill Level Calculation from Name

This example demonstrates deriving a numeric level field from skill name patterns, automatically categorizing skills into proficiency levels.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = 5  # Default middle level
    description = factory.Faker('sentence')
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """
        Auto-calculate skill level based on name patterns.
        - 'Senior', 'Expert', 'Advanced' → level 9-10
        - 'Intermediate', 'Proficient' → level 6-7
        - 'Junior', 'Basic', 'Beginner' → level 2-3
        - 'Master', 'Architect' → level 10
        """
        name = kwargs.get('name', '').lower()
        
        # Check for level indicators in name
        if any(keyword in name for keyword in ['master', 'architect', 'guru']):
            kwargs['level'] = 10
        elif any(keyword in name for keyword in ['senior', 'expert', 'advanced']):
            kwargs['level'] = 9
        elif any(keyword in name for keyword in ['intermediate', 'proficient']):
            kwargs['level'] = 7
        elif any(keyword in name for keyword in ['junior', 'basic', 'beginner', 'novice']):
            kwargs['level'] = 3
        elif 'level' not in kwargs:
            # No keyword found and no explicit level, use default
            kwargs['level'] = 5
        
        return kwargs

print("=== Example 3: Skill Level Auto-Calculation ===\n")

# Test Case 1: Skills with level keywords in name
skill1 = SkillFactory.create(name="Senior Python Development")
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Detected Keyword: 'Senior'")
print(f"  Auto-Calculated Level: {skill1.level}/10")

skill2 = SkillFactory.create(name="Junior JavaScript")
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name}")
print(f"  Detected Keyword: 'Junior'")
print(f"  Auto-Calculated Level: {skill2.level}/10")

skill3 = SkillFactory.create(name="Master Database Architect")
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Detected Keywords: 'Master', 'Architect'")
print(f"  Auto-Calculated Level: {skill3.level}/10")

# Test Case 2: Skills without keywords (default level)
skill4 = SkillFactory.create(name="Django Framework")
print(f"\nSkill 4: {skill4.name}")
print(f"  No Level Keyword Detected")
print(f"  Default Level Applied: {skill4.level}/10")

# Test Case 3: Explicit level override (should be preserved)
skill5 = SkillFactory.create(
    name="Advanced Data Science",
    level=8  # Explicit override
)
print(f"\nSkill 5: {skill5.name}")
print(f"  Detected Keyword: 'Advanced' (would suggest level 9)")
print(f"  Explicit Level Provided: {skill5.level}/10")
print(f"  Note: Explicit values override auto-calculation")

# Summary statistics
print("\n" + "="*50)
print("Level Distribution Summary:")
all_skills = Skills.objects.all().order_by('-level')
for skill in all_skills:
    category = "Master" if skill.level == 10 else "Senior" if skill.level >= 8 else "Mid" if skill.level >= 5 else "Junior"
    print(f"  [{category:6}] Level {skill.level}: {skill.name}")

Example 4: Location Postal Code Format Validation

This example shows enforcing postal code format based on country, automatically formatting US ZIP codes and Canadian postal codes correctly.

import factory
import re
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """
        Format postal code based on country standards.
        - USA: Force 5-digit or 5+4 format (XXXXX or XXXXX-XXXX)
        - Canada: Force format A1A 1A1
        - Other: No formatting
        """
        country = kwargs.get('country', 'USA')
        postal = str(kwargs.get('postal_code', ''))
        
        if country == 'USA':
            # Remove all non-digits
            digits = re.sub(r'\D', '', postal)
            if len(digits) >= 9:
                # Format as XXXXX-XXXX
                kwargs['postal_code'] = f"{digits[:5]}-{digits[5:9]}"
            elif len(digits) >= 5:
                # Format as XXXXX
                kwargs['postal_code'] = digits[:5]
            else:
                # Pad with zeros if needed
                kwargs['postal_code'] = digits.zfill(5)
                
        elif country == 'Canada':
            # Remove spaces and format as A1A 1A1
            postal_clean = postal.upper().replace(' ', '')
            if len(postal_clean) >= 6:
                kwargs['postal_code'] = f"{postal_clean[:3]} {postal_clean[3:6]}"
        
        return kwargs

print("=== Example 4: Postal Code Format Validation ===\n")

# Test Case 1: USA 5-digit ZIP
loc1 = LocationFactory.create(
    city="New York",
    state="NY",
    postal_code="10001",
    country="USA"
)
print(f"Location 1 - {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Country: {loc1.country}")
print(f"  Postal Code: {loc1.postal_code}")
print(f"  Format: 5-digit ZIP")

# Test Case 2: USA ZIP+4 with improper formatting
loc2 = LocationFactory.create(
    city="Los Angeles",
    state="CA",
    postal_code="900121234",  # Missing dash
    country="USA"
)
print(f"\nLocation 2 - {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Input Postal: 900121234")
print(f"  Formatted Postal: {loc2.postal_code}")
print(f"  Format: ZIP+4 (auto-hyphenated)")

# Test Case 3: Canada postal code without space
loc3 = LocationFactory.create(
    street_address="123 Main St",
    city="Toronto",
    state="ON",
    postal_code="M5V3A8",  # Missing space
    country="Canada"
)
print(f"\nLocation 3 - {loc3.city}, {loc3.state}")
print(f"  Input Postal: M5V3A8")
print(f"  Formatted Postal: {loc3.postal_code}")
print(f"  Format: Canadian (A1A 1A1)")

# Test Case 4: USA ZIP with extra characters
loc4 = LocationFactory.create(
    city="Chicago",
    state="IL",
    postal_code="ZIP: 60601",  # Has text prefix
    country="USA"
)
print(f"\nLocation 4 - {loc4.city}, {loc4.state}")
print(f"  Input Postal: 'ZIP: 60601'")
print(f"  Cleaned Postal: {loc4.postal_code}")
print(f"  Format: Digits extracted, formatted")

# Test Case 5: Short ZIP code (needs padding)
loc5 = LocationFactory.create(
    city="Small Town",
    state="VT",
    postal_code="123",  # Too short
    country="USA"
)
print(f"\nLocation 5 - {loc5.city}, {loc5.state}")
print(f"  Input Postal: '123'")
print(f"  Padded Postal: {loc5.postal_code}")
print(f"  Format: Zero-padded to 5 digits")

print("\n" + "="*50)
print("All Location Postal Codes:")
for loc in Location.objects.all().order_by('country', 'state'):
    print(f"  [{loc.country}] {loc.city}, {loc.state}: {loc.postal_code}")

Example 5: Author Experience Level from Bio Length

This example demonstrates automatically determining an author's experience level based on their bio length and setting related flags.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author

class ExperiencedAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    @classmethod
    def _adjust_kwargs(cls, **kwargs):
        """
        Determine author experience level from bio content.
        Also adds computed fields for categorization.
        
        Logic:
        - Bio length < 50 chars: Novice (add "New to the platform" note)
        - Bio length 50-150 chars: Intermediate
        - Bio length > 150 chars: Expert (add "Verified Expert" note)
        - Also tracks bio_word_count for analytics
        """
        bio = kwargs.get('bio', '')
        bio_length = len(bio)
        word_count = len(bio.split())
        
        # Add computed metadata to bio
        if bio_length > 150:
            experience_level = "Expert"
            if not bio.endswith("[Verified Expert]"):
                kwargs['bio'] = f"{bio} [Verified Expert]"
        elif bio_length >= 50:
            experience_level = "Intermediate"
        else:
            experience_level = "Novice"
            if not bio.endswith("[New Author]"):
                kwargs['bio'] = f"{bio} [New Author]"
        
        # Store metadata in bio field (in real app, might use separate fields)
        print(f"  [_adjust_kwargs] Bio length: {bio_length} chars, {word_count} words → {experience_level}")
        
        return kwargs

print("=== Example 5: Author Experience Level Calculation ===\n")

# Test Case 1: Short bio (Novice)
print("Test 1 - Novice Author (Short Bio):")
author1 = ExperiencedAuthorFactory.create(
    bio="I love writing about tech."
)
print(f"Author: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"Bio: {author1.bio}")
print(f"Experience Category: Novice\n")

# Test Case 2: Medium bio (Intermediate)
print("Test 2 - Intermediate Author (Medium Bio):")
author2 = ExperiencedAuthorFactory.create(
    bio="I have been writing technical articles for several years, covering topics in Python, Django, and web development. I enjoy sharing knowledge with the community."
)
print(f"Author: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"Bio Length: ~130 chars")
print(f"Bio: {author2.bio}")
print(f"Experience Category: Intermediate\n")

# Test Case 3: Long bio (Expert)
print("Test 3 - Expert Author (Long Bio):")

    bio="I am a seasoned technology writer with over 15 years of experience in software development and technical communication. I have authored multiple books, contributed to major tech publications, and spoken at international conferences. My expertise spans multiple programming languages, software architecture, cloud computing, and DevOps practices. I'm passionate about making complex technical concepts accessible to developers of all skill levels."
)
print(f"Author: {author3.user.username}")
print(f"Bio Length: ~390 chars")
print(f"Bio: {author3.bio[:100]}...")
print(f"Badge: [Verified Expert]")
print(f"Experience Category: Expert\n")

# Batch test with varying lengths
print("Test 4 - Batch Creation with Mixed Experience:")
bio_templates = [
    "Quick bio.",  # Novice
    "Medium length bio with some experience details included here to meet character requirements for intermediate level.",  # Intermediate
    "Very short",  # Novice
]

batch_authors = []
for i, bio_template in enumerate(bio_templates, start=4):
    author = ExperiencedAuthorFactory.create(bio=bio_template)
    batch_authors.append(author)
    level = "Novice" if len(bio_template) < 50 else "Intermediate" if len(bio_template) <= 150 else "Expert"
    print(f"Author {i}: {level} ({len(bio_template)} chars)")

# Summary
print("\n" + "="*50)
print("Author Experience Distribution:")
all_authors = Author.objects.all()
novice = sum(1 for a in all_authors if '[New Author]' in a.bio)
expert = sum(1 for a in all_authors if '[Verified Expert]' in a.bio)
intermediate = all_authors.count() - novice - expert
print(f"  Novice Authors: {novice}")
print(f"  Intermediate Authors: {intermediate}")
print(f"  Expert Authors: {expert}")
print(f"  Total Authors: {all_authors.count()}")

_setup_next_sequence - Control Sequence Initialization

Hook to determine the starting value for Sequence declarations.

📖 Full Description

The _setup_next_sequence class method is a critical extension hook that controls the initial value for all factory.Sequence declarations within a factory. By default, FactoryBoy queries the database to find the maximum ID of existing objects and starts the sequence from max_id + 1. This ensures that sequence-generated values don't collide with existing database records. However, you can override this method to implement custom sequencing strategies such as starting from a specific number for test isolation, using time-based sequences, implementing gap-based numbering, or coordinating sequences across multiple factory classes. This method is called once per factory class when the first object is created, and the returned value becomes the starting point for incrementing sequence numbers. Understanding this hook is essential for scenarios where you need predictable sequence values in tests, want to separate test data ranges from production data, or need to implement business-specific numbering schemes like invoice numbers, employee IDs, or order numbers that follow specific patterns.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import ModelName

class ModelFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = ModelName
    
    # Sequence-based field declarations
    field_name = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'value_{n}')
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """
        Determine the starting sequence value.
        
        Returns:
            int: The initial sequence number (default behavior queries database)
        """
        # Custom logic to determine starting sequence
        # Default implementation:
        # try:
        #     return cls._meta.model.objects.latest('id').id + 1
        # except cls._meta.model.DoesNotExist:
        #     return 0
        
        return custom_starting_value

📋 Parameters Description

cls - The factory class itself (classmethod decorator). Provides access to the Meta configuration, model class, and other factory-level information. No arguments are passed to this method.

🏷️ Attributes

cls._meta - Access to factory metadata including the model class, strategy, and configuration options.

cls._meta.model - The Django model class this factory creates instances for. Used to query database for existing records.

⚙️ Methods

This classmethod doesn't typically call other methods, but it can access the model's manager methods like .objects.count(), .objects.latest(), or .objects.aggregate() to determine the appropriate starting sequence based on database state.

↩️ Return Value

Returns an integer representing the starting sequence value. All factory.Sequence declarations in this factory will begin counting from this number. The default implementation returns 0 if no objects exist, or max_id + 1 if objects are found. You can return any non-negative integer. Subsequent factory calls will increment this value automatically (starting_value, starting_value+1, starting_value+2, etc.).

💡 Examples

Example 1: Test Isolation with High Starting Sequence

Start user sequences at 10000 to keep test data separate from production-like data ranges.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()

class IsolatedUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'testuser_{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@test.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """Start sequence at 10000 for test data isolation"""
        return 10000

print("=== Example 1: Test Isolation with High Starting Sequence ===\n")

# Create first user - sequence starts at 10000
user1 = IsolatedUserFactory.create(first_name="Alice")
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Sequence started at: 10000")

# Create more users - sequence increments
user2 = IsolatedUserFactory.create(first_name="Bob")
user3 = IsolatedUserFactory.create(first_name="Charlie")

print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"User 3: {user3.username}")

# Batch create
batch_users = IsolatedUserFactory.create_batch(3)
print(f"\nBatch created users:")
for user in batch_users:
    print(f"  {user.username} ({user.first_name})")

# Verify range
all_users = User.objects.all().order_by('id')
print(f"\n📊 Sequence Range Summary:")
print(f"  Total users created: {all_users.count()}")
print(f"  First username: {all_users.first().username}")
print(f"  Last username: {all_users.last().username}")
print(f"  All usernames in 10000+ range: Test data clearly separated")
Print Output: === Example 1: Test Isolation with High Starting Sequence === User 1: testuser_10000 Email: testuser_10000@test.com Sequence started at: 10000 User 2: testuser_10001 User 3: testuser_10002 Batch created users: testuser_10003 (David) testuser_10004 (Emma) testuser_10005 (Frank) 📊 Sequence Range Summary: Total users created: 6 First username: testuser_10000 Last username: testuser_10005 All usernames in 10000+ range: Test data clearly separated

Example 2: Skill ID Starting from Current Year

Use year-based sequences for skills to create time-stamped identifiers (e.g., 2025_0, 2025_1).

import factory
from datetime import datetime
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean database
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class YearBasedSkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'{datetime.now().year}_{n}')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.Faker('sentence')
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """Start sequence at current year * 1000"""
        current_year = datetime.now().year
        starting_value = current_year * 1000
        print(f"[_setup_next_sequence] Starting skill sequence at: {starting_value}")
        return starting_value

print("=== Example 2: Year-Based Skill Sequences ===\n")

# Create skills - sequence embeds year
skill1 = YearBasedSkillFactory.create()
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name} (Level {skill1.level})")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")

skill2 = YearBasedSkillFactory.create()
skill3 = YearBasedSkillFactory.create()

print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name} (Level {skill2.level})")
print(f"Skill 3: {skill3.name} (Level {skill3.level})")

# Create skills with specific names but sequence-based IDs
class NamedYearSkillFactory(YearBasedSkillFactory):
    name = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Skill-{datetime.now().year}-{n:04d}')

print(f"\n--- Named Year Skills with Zero-Padded Sequences ---")
named_skill1 = NamedYearSkillFactory.create()
named_skill2 = NamedYearSkillFactory.create()
named_skill3 = NamedYearSkillFactory.create()

print(f"Named Skill 1: {named_skill1.name}")
print(f"Named Skill 2: {named_skill2.name}")
print(f"Named Skill 3: {named_skill3.name}")

# Summary
all_skills = Skills.objects.all().order_by('id')
print(f"\n📊 Year-Based Sequence Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills: {all_skills.count()}")
print(f"  Sequence year: {datetime.now().year}")
print(f"  First skill: {all_skills.first().name}")
print(f"  Last skill: {all_skills.last().name}")
print(f"  Pattern: Year embedded in name for audit trail")

Example 3: Location Sequence Based on Existing Count

Continue sequence from existing database records, querying current count to avoid collisions.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

# Create some initial locations manually
Location.objects.create(
    street_address="100 Main St",
    city="Springfield",
    state="IL",
    postal_code="62701",
    country="USA"
)
Location.objects.create(
    street_address="200 Oak Ave",
    city="Portland",
    state="OR",
    postal_code="97201",
    country="USA"
)

print("=== Example 3: Sequence Continuation from Existing Data ===\n")
print(f"Existing locations in database: {Location.objects.count()}")

class ContinuingLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'{n * 100} Factory St')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """
        Start sequence from current database count.
        This ensures sequence-generated addresses don't duplicate.
        """
        current_count = cls._meta.model.objects.count()
        print(f"[_setup_next_sequence] Found {current_count} existing locations")
        print(f"[_setup_next_sequence] Starting sequence at: {current_count}")
        return current_count

# Create new locations - sequence continues from count
loc1 = ContinuingLocationFactory.create(city="Boston")
print(f"\nLocation 1 (Factory): {loc1.street_address}, {loc1.city}")
print(f"  Sequence value: 2 (based on 2 existing records)")

loc2 = ContinuingLocationFactory.create(city="Seattle")
print(f"\nLocation 2 (Factory): {loc2.street_address}, {loc2.city}")

loc3 = ContinuingLocationFactory.create(city="Austin")
print(f"\nLocation 3 (Factory): {loc3.street_address}, {loc3.city}")

# Show all locations
print(f"\n{'='*60}")
print("All Locations (Manual + Factory):")
for i, loc in enumerate(Location.objects.all().order_by('id'), 1):
    source = "Manual" if i <= 2 else "Factory"
    print(f"  {i}. [{source:7}] {loc.street_address}, {loc.city}, {loc.state}")

# Statistics
manual_count = 2
factory_count = Location.objects.count() - manual_count
print(f"\n📊 Sequence Continuation Summary:")
print(f"  Manual locations: {manual_count}")
print(f"  Factory locations: {factory_count}")
print(f"  Total: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Sequence started at: {manual_count} (no conflicts!)")

Example 4: Author Sequential Bio Numbering

Create authors with sequential identifiers in their bio field for tracking and testing purposes.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author

# Clean author data
Author.objects.all().delete()

class SequentialAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(
        'IsolatedUserFactory',  # Reuse user factory from Example 1
        username=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'author_user_{n}')
    )
    bio = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Author biography #{n:05d}. Experienced writer in technology.')
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """Start author sequence at 1 for clean numbering"""
        return 1

print("=== Example 4: Author Sequential Bio Numbering ===\n")

# Create authors with sequential bios
author1 = SequentialAuthorFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

author2 = SequentialAuthorFactory.create()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")

# Batch create with sequential numbering
print(f"\n--- Batch Creating 5 More Authors ---")
batch_authors = SequentialAuthorFactory.create_batch(5)
for author in batch_authors:
    print(f"  {author.user.username}: {author.bio[:40]}...")

# Different factory with different starting point
class SeniorAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(
        'IsolatedUserFactory',
        username=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'senior_author_{n}')
    )
    bio = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Senior Author #{n}. 10+ years of experience.')
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """Start senior authors at 1000"""
        return 1000

print(f"\n--- Senior Authors (Different Sequence) ---")
senior1 = SeniorAuthorFactory.create()
senior2 = SeniorAuthorFactory.create()
print(f"Senior 1: {senior1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {senior1.bio}")
print(f"Senior 2: {senior2.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {senior2.bio}")

# Summary
all_authors = Author.objects.all()
print(f"\n📊 Author Sequence Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {all_authors.count()}")
print(f"  Regular authors (seq 1+): 7")
print(f"  Senior authors (seq 1000+): 2")
print(f"  Multiple factories can have independent sequences")

Example 5: JobPost with Invoice-Style Sequential IDs

Generate job posts with invoice-style numbering using a prefix and zero-padded sequential numbers.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Location, Author

# Clean job posts
JobPost.objects.all().delete()

class InvoiceStyleJobFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Job-REQ-2025-{n:06d}')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = 60000
    salary_max = 120000
    author = factory.SubFactory(SequentialAuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(
        ContinuingLocationFactory,
        city=factory.Faker('city')
    )
    
    @classmethod
    def _setup_next_sequence(cls):
        """Start job requisition numbers at 100"""
        return 100

print("=== Example 5: Invoice-Style Sequential Job IDs ===\n")

# Create jobs with invoice-style IDs
job1 = InvoiceStyleJobFactory.create(
    salary_min=80000,
    salary_max=100000
)
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Location: {job1.location.city}, {job1.location.state}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Author: {job1.author.user.username}")

job2 = InvoiceStyleJobFactory.create(
    salary_min=90000,
    salary_max=130000
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Location: {job2.location.city}, {job2.location.state}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,} - ${job2.salary_max:,}")

# Batch create
print(f"\n--- Batch Creating Job Requisitions ---")
batch_jobs = InvoiceStyleJobFactory.create_batch(5)
for job in batch_jobs:
    print(f"  {job.title}")
    print(f"    Author: {job.author.user.username} | Location: {job.location.city}")

# Summary with sequence analysis
all_jobs = JobPost.objects.all().order_by('id')
print(f"\n{'='*60}")
print("Job Requisition Sequence Analysis:")
for job in all_jobs:
    req_number = job.title.split('-')[-1]
    print(f"  {job.title} → Sequence: {req_number}")

print(f"\n📊 Job Requisition Summary:")

print(f"  First requisition: {all_jobs.first().title}")
print(f"  Last requisition: {all_jobs.last().title}")
print(f"  Format: Job-REQ-2025-NNNNNN")
print(f"  Starting sequence: 100")
print(f"  Zero-padded: 6 digits for clean sorting")
print(f"  Use case: Professional job requisition tracking")

_build & _create - Object Instantiation Hooks

Hooks to customize how objects are instantiated and saved.

📖 Full Description

The _build and _create class methods are fundamental extension hooks that control precisely how model objects are instantiated. The _build method creates objects in memory without persisting them to the database (corresponding to the BUILD strategy), while _create creates and saves objects to the database (corresponding to the CREATE strategy). These methods receive the model class and all resolved field values, allowing you to customize the instantiation process. Common customizations include using custom model managers (like Django's User.objects.create_user for proper password hashing), implementing bulk creation optimizations, adding transaction management, implementing custom validation before saving, handling special field types that require preprocessing, or integrating with external systems during creation. The _build method is particularly useful for testing scenarios where you want to verify object state without database overhead, while _create is essential when you need to override default save behavior. Both methods have access to the factory's _get_manager() helper which returns the appropriate model manager. Understanding these hooks is critical when working with Django models that have custom managers or when you need fine-grained control over the object lifecycle.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import ModelName

class ModelFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = ModelName
    
    # Field declarations
    field_name = factory.Faker('provider')
    
    @classmethod
    def _build(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """
        Instantiate object without saving to database.
        
        Args:
            model_class: The Django model class
            *args: Positional arguments (if Meta.inline_args defined)
            **kwargs: Resolved keyword arguments
            
        Returns:
            Unsaved model instance
        """
        # Default implementation:
        return model_class(*args, **kwargs)
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """
        Instantiate object and save to database.
        
        Args:
            model_class: The Django model class
            *args: Positional arguments (if Meta.inline_args defined)
            **kwargs: Resolved keyword arguments
            
        Returns:
            Saved model instance
        """
        # Default implementation:
        manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
        return manager.create(*args, **kwargs)

📋 Parameters Description

cls - The factory class itself (classmethod decorator). Provides access to factory configuration and helper methods.

model_class (class) - The Django model class specified in Meta.model. This is the class that will be instantiated, not an instance of it.

*args (tuple) - Positional arguments to pass to the model constructor. Only present if Meta.inline_args is defined in the factory, which specifies which fields should be passed as positional arguments.

**kwargs (dict) - Keyword arguments to pass to the model constructor or manager's create method. These are all the resolved field values after factory declarations have been evaluated and _adjust_kwargs has been applied.

🏷️ Attributes

cls._meta - Factory metadata containing model class, strategy configuration, and other factory-level settings.

cls._meta.model - The Django model class this factory creates. Same as the model_class parameter.

⚙️ Methods

cls._get_manager(model_class) - Helper method that returns the appropriate Django model manager. By default, returns model_class._default_manager or model_class.objects. You can override the manager by setting Meta.django_get_or_create or using a custom manager.

↩️ Return Value

_build returns an unsaved model instance - the object exists in memory with all fields set, but has not been saved to the database. The instance will not have a primary key (id) and related objects cannot be assigned. Useful for unit tests that don't require database interaction.

_create returns a saved model instance - the object has been persisted to the database and has a primary key. All database constraints have been validated and the instance is fully ready for use with relationships.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Custom User Creation with Password Hashing

Override _create to use Django's create_user manager method for proper password handling.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()

class SecureUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Use create_user for proper password hashing"""
        # Extract password (default if not provided)
        password = kwargs.pop('password', 'testpass123')
        
        # Get the User manager
        manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
        
        # Use create_user method which hashes password
        if hasattr(manager, 'create_user'):
            # create_user(username, email, password, **extra_fields)
            username = kwargs.pop('username')
            email = kwargs.pop('email', '')
            user = manager.create_user(username, email, password, **kwargs)
            print(f"  [_create] Created user with hashed password: {username}")
            return user
        else:
            # Fallback to regular create
            return manager.create(*args, **kwargs)

print("=== Example 1: Custom User Creation with Password Hashing ===\n")

# Create user with custom password
user1 = SecureUserFactory.create(
    username='alice',
    password='secure_password_123'
)
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Password is hashed: {user1.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can authenticate: {user1.check_password('secure_password_123')}")

# Create user with default password
user2 = SecureUserFactory.create(username='bob')
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Used default password: testpass123")
print(f"  Password is hashed: {user2.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can authenticate: {user2.check_password('testpass123')}")

# Build vs Create comparison
built_user = SecureUserFactory.build(username='charlie')
print(f"\nBuilt User (not in DB): {built_user.username}")
print(f"  Has ID: {built_user.id}")  # None - not saved
print(f"  In database: {User.objects.filter(username='charlie').exists()}")

# Now create the same username
created_user = SecureUserFactory.create(username='charlie')
print(f"\nCreated User (in DB): {created_user.username}")
print(f"  Has ID: {created_user.id}")  # Has ID - saved
print(f"  In database: {User.objects.filter(username='charlie').exists()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  All passwords properly hashed via create_user method")
