Example 2: Skills with Validation in _build and _create

Add custom validation logic in both _build and _create hooks to ensure skill level constraints.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean database
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class ValidatedSkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.Faker('sentence')
    
    @classmethod
    def _build(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Build with validation (no DB save)"""
        # Validate level range
        level = kwargs.get('level', 5)
        if level < 1 or level > 10:
            print(f"  [_build] WARNING: Level {level} out of range, clamping to 1-10")
            kwargs['level'] = max(1, min(10, level))
        
        print(f"  [_build] Building skill: {kwargs.get('name')} (level {kwargs['level']})")
        return model_class(*args, **kwargs)
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Create with validation and DB save"""
        # Validate level range
        level = kwargs.get('level', 5)
        if level < 1 or level > 10:
            print(f"  [_create] WARNING: Level {level} out of range, clamping to 1-10")
            kwargs['level'] = max(1, min(10, level))
        
        # Additional validation: ensure description mentions level
        name = kwargs.get('name', 'Unknown')
        level = kwargs['level']
        desc = kwargs.get('description', '')
        
        # Enhance description with level info
        if 'level' not in desc.lower():
            kwargs['description'] = f"{desc} [Skill level: {level}/10]"
        
        print(f"  [_create] Creating skill: {name} (level {level})")
        
        manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
        return manager.create(*args, **kwargs)

print("=== Example 2: Skills with Validation ===\n")

# Test 1: Normal creation
skill1 = ValidatedSkillFactory.create(
    name="Python Programming",
    level=8
)
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description[:50]}...")

# Test 2: Build without saving (validation still applies)
skill2_built = ValidatedSkillFactory.build(
    name="JavaScript",
    level=15  # Invalid - will be clamped
)
print(f"\nSkill 2 (built): {skill2_built.name}")
print(f"  Level (clamped): {skill2_built.level}")
print(f"  Saved to DB: {skill2_built.id is not None}")

# Test 3: Create with invalid level
skill3 = ValidatedSkillFactory.create(
    name="C++ Development",
    level=-5  # Invalid - will be clamped to 1
)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Original level: -5")
print(f"  Clamped level: {skill3.level}")
print(f"  Description enhanced: {'[Skill level:' in skill3.description}")

# Test 4: Batch create with mixed valid/invalid levels
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
skills = []
test_levels = [3, 20, 7, -10, 5]
for i, level in enumerate(test_levels):
    skill = ValidatedSkillFactory.create(
        name=f"Skill {i+4}",
        level=level
    )
    skills.append(skill)
    print(f"  Skill {i+4}: Level {level} → {skill.level}")

print(f"\n📊 Validation Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills in DB: {Skills.objects.count()}")
all_skills = Skills.objects.all()
print(f"  All levels in valid range: {all(1 <= s.level <= 10 for s in all_skills)}")
print(f"  Built objects not in DB: {Skills.objects.filter(name='JavaScript').count() == 0}")

Example 3: Location with Geocoding Simulation in _create

Simulate adding computed fields (like coordinates) during the create process.

import factory
import hashlib
from jobpost.models import Location

# Clean database
Location.objects.all().delete()

class GeocodedLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """
        Add geocoding simulation during creation.
        In real app, this would call a geocoding API.
        """
        city = kwargs.get('city', '')
        state = kwargs.get('state', '')
        
        # Simulate geocoding with deterministic "coordinates"
        # (Real app would use Google Maps API, etc.)
        location_string = f"{city}{state}".lower()
        hash_value = int(hashlib.md5(location_string.encode()).hexdigest()[:8], 16)
        
        # Generate fake lat/long within USA bounds
        latitude = 25.0 + (hash_value % 25)  # 25-50° N
        longitude = -125.0 + (hash_value % 60)  # -125° to -65° W
        
        print(f"  [_create] Geocoding: {city}, {state}")
        print(f"  [_create] Coordinates: ({latitude:.4f}, {longitude:.4f})")
        
        # In real app, you might store coordinates in database
        # For this example, we'll just print them
        
        manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
        instance = manager.create(*args, **kwargs)
        
        # Simulate storing metadata (in real app, might be separate fields)
        instance._geocoded = True
        instance._coordinates = (latitude, longitude)
        
        return instance

print("=== Example 3: Location with Geocoding ===\n")

# Create locations with geocoding
loc1 = GeocodedLocationFactory.create(
    street_address="123 Tech Blvd",
    city="San Francisco",
    state="CA"
)
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.street_address}")
print(f"  City/State: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Geocoded: {loc1._geocoded}")
print(f"  Coordinates: {loc1._coordinates}")

loc2 = GeocodedLocationFactory.create(
    city="New York",
    state="NY"
)
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.street_address}")
print(f"  City/State: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Coordinates: {loc2._coordinates}")

# Batch create different cities
print(f"\n--- Batch Geocoding ---")
cities = [
    ("Austin", "TX"),
    ("Seattle", "WA"),
    ("Miami", "FL")
]

locations = []
for city, state in cities:
    loc = GeocodedLocationFactory.create(city=city, state=state)
    locations.append(loc)

print(f"\n📊 Geocoding Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations created: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  All locations geocoded: {all(hasattr(loc, '_geocoded') for loc in locations)}")
print("\nCoordinate Map:")
for loc in Location.objects.all():
    if hasattr(loc, '_coordinates'):
        lat, lon = loc._coordinates
        print(f"  {loc.city:15} {loc.state}: ({lat:.2f}°N, {lon:.2f}°W)")

Example 4: Author with Transaction Management in _create

Wrap object creation in database transaction for atomicity, demonstrating error handling.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from django.db import transaction
from jobpost.models import Author

# Clean database
Author.objects.all().delete()
# Keep users from Example 1

class TransactionalAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(SecureUserFactory, username=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'author{n}'))
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """
        Create author within transaction for atomicity.
        If anything fails, entire creation is rolled back.
        """
        print(f"  [_create] Starting transaction for author creation")
        
        try:
            with transaction.atomic():
                manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
                instance = manager.create(*args, **kwargs)
                
                print(f"  [_create] Created author: {instance.user.username}")
                print(f"  [_create] Bio length: {len(instance.bio)} chars")
                print(f"  [_create] Transaction committed successfully")
                
                return instance
        except Exception as e:
            print(f"  [_create] ERROR: Transaction rolled back: {e}")
            raise

print("=== Example 4: Transactional Author Creation ===\n")

# Test 1: Successful creation
print("Test 1 - Normal Creation:")
author1 = TransactionalAuthorFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  ID: {author1.id}")
print(f"  In DB: {Author.objects.filter(id=author1.id).exists()}\n")

# Test 2: Batch creation (each in own transaction)
print("Test 2 - Batch Creation:")
authors = TransactionalAuthorFactory.create_batch(3)
for i, author in enumerate(authors, 2):
    print(f"Author {i}: {author.user.username} (ID: {author.id})")

# Test 3: Demonstrate build doesn't use _create
print(f"\nTest 3 - Build vs Create:")
built_author = TransactionalAuthorFactory.build()
print(f"Built Author: {built_author.user.username}")
print(f"  Uses _create: No (built only, no transaction)")
print(f"  Has ID: {built_author.id}")
print(f"  In DB: {Author.objects.filter(user__username=built_author.user.username).exists()}")

# Summary
print(f"\n📊 Transaction Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors in DB: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  All created within atomic transactions")
print(f"  Built object not in DB (bypasses _create)")
print(f"  Total users created: {User.objects.count()}")  # Includes SubFactory users

Example 5: JobPost with Logging and Audit Trail

Implement comprehensive logging in _build and _create for audit trail and debugging.

import factory
from datetime import datetime
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills

# Clean database
JobPost.objects.all().delete()

# Audit log storage (in real app, would be database table or file)
audit_log = []

class AuditedJobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=300)
    salary_min = factory.Faker('random_int', min=50000, max=90000)
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.salary_min + 30000)
    author = factory.SubFactory(TransactionalAuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(GeocodedLocationFactory)
    
    @classmethod
    def _build(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Log build operations for debugging"""
        timestamp = datetime.now().isoformat()
        title = kwargs.get('title', 'Unknown')
        
        log_entry = {
            'timestamp': timestamp,
            'action': 'BUILD',
            'model': model_class.__name__,
            'title': title,
            'saved_to_db': False
        }
        audit_log.append(log_entry)
        
        print(f"  [_build] [{timestamp}] Building {title} (not saving)")
        return model_class(*args, **kwargs)
    
    @classmethod
    def _create(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs):
        """Log create operations for audit trail"""
        timestamp = datetime.now().isoformat()
        title = kwargs.get('title', 'Unknown')
        salary_range = f"${kwargs.get('salary_min', 0):,}-${kwargs.get('salary_max', 0):,}"
        
        print(f"  [_create] [{timestamp}] Creating {title}")
        print(f"  [_create] Salary range: {salary_range}")
        
        manager = cls._get_manager(model_class)
        instance = manager.create(*args, **kwargs)
        
        log_entry = {
            'timestamp': timestamp,
            'action': 'CREATE',
            'model': model_class.__name__,
            'title': title,
            'id': instance.id,
            'salary_min': instance.salary_min,
            'salary_max': instance.salary_max,
            'author_username': instance.author.user.username,
            'location_city': instance.location.city,
            'saved_to_db': True
        }
        audit_log.append(log_entry)
        
        print(f"  [_create] Job created with ID: {instance.id}")
        return instance

print("=== Example 5: Audited JobPost Creation ===\n")

# Clear audit log
audit_log.clear()

# Create job posts
job1 = AuditedJobPostFactory.create(title="Senior Python Developer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title} (ID: {job1.id})")
print(f"  Author: {job1.author.user.username}")
print(f"  Location: {job1.location.city}\n")

# Build job (not saved)
job2_built = AuditedJobPostFactory.build(title="Junior Developer")
print(f"Job 2 (built): {job2_built.title}")
print(f"  Has ID: {job2_built.id}")
print(f"  In DB: {JobPost.objects.filter(title='Junior Developer').exists()}\n")

# Create more jobs
job3 = AuditedJobPostFactory.create(title="DevOps Engineer")
print(f"Job 3: {job3.title} (ID: {job3.id})\n")

# Print audit log
print("=" * 70)
print("AUDIT LOG:")
print("=" * 70)
for i, entry in enumerate(audit_log, 1):
    print(f"\nEntry {i}:")
    print(f"  Timestamp: {entry['timestamp']}")
    print(f"  Action: {entry['action']}")
    print(f"  Title: {entry['title']}")
    if entry['saved_to_db']:
        print(f"  ID: {entry['id']}")
        print(f"  Salary: ${entry['salary_min']:,} - ${entry['salary_max']:,}")
        print(f"  Author: {entry['author_username']}")
        print(f"  Location: {entry['location_city']}")
    print(f"  Saved to DB: {entry['saved_to_db']}")

# Summary
print(f"\n{'=' * 70}")
print("📊 Audit Summary:")
print(f"  Total audit entries: {len(audit_log)}")
print(f"  CREATE actions: {sum(1 for e in audit_log if e['action'] == 'CREATE')}")
print(f"  BUILD actions: {sum(1 for e in audit_log if e['action'] == 'BUILD')}")
print(f"  Objects in DB: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Built but not saved: {sum(1 for e in audit_log if not e['saved_to_db'])}")

_after_postgeneration - Final Cleanup Hook

Hook called after all post-generation hooks have executed.

📖 Full Description

The _after_postgeneration class method is the absolute final hook in the factory object creation lifecycle, called after all post-generation hooks (PostGeneration, RelatedFactory, post_generation decorators) have completed their execution. This hook provides a perfect opportunity for final validation, applying calculated values based on post-generation data, saving objects that were modified by post-generation hooks, cleaning up temporary state or attributes, implementing cascading updates to related objects, or generating summary data. The method receives the fully constructed object, a boolean indicating whether it was created or just built, and optionally a dictionary containing the results from all post-generation hooks. This is particularly powerful when you have complex object graphs where the final state of an object depends on relationships that were created during post-generation. For example, you might want to update a counter field, recalculate aggregates, normalize data across relationships, or perform validation that requires the complete object graph to be in place. The key distinction is that this hook runs AFTER post-generation, giving you access to the object in its nearly-final state with all relationships established.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import ModelName

class ModelFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = ModelName
    
    # Field declarations
    field_name = factory.Faker('provider')
    
    # Post-generation declarations (RelatedFactory, PostGeneration, etc.)
    related = factory.RelatedFactory('OtherFactory', 'parent_field')
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """
        Final hook after all post-generation hooks complete.
        
        Args:
            obj: The created/built object instance
            create: True if CREATE strategy, False if BUILD strategy
            results: Dict mapping post-generation hook names to return values
        
        Returns:
            None (modifies obj in-place if needed)
        """
        # Perform final operations
        if create:
            # Update calculated fields based on relationships
            obj.some_computed_field = calculate_value(obj)
            obj.save()  # Must explicitly save if modified

📋 Parameters Description

cls - The factory class itself (classmethod decorator). Provides access to factory configuration and Meta settings.

obj (Model instance) - The created or built object instance. This is the fully constructed object with all fields set and all post-generation hooks already executed. Any RelatedFactory objects have been created and linked. You can modify this object, but must explicitly save it if create=True and you want changes persisted.

create (bool) - Boolean flag indicating the creation strategy. True means CREATE strategy was used (object is saved to database). False means BUILD strategy was used (object is not saved). Use this to determine if you need to call obj.save() after modifications.

results (dict or None) - Optional dictionary mapping post-generation hook names (strings) to their return values. The dictionary keys are the attribute names of post-generation declarations. The values are whatever those hooks returned. Can be None if no results were collected or if using an older FactoryBoy version.

🏷️ Attributes

obj - The object being finalized. Has all attributes, relationships, and post-generation effects applied.

obj._meta - Django model metadata, useful for introspecting the model structure.

⚙️ Methods

This method typically calls:

obj.save() - Saves the object to database if modifications were made and create=True.

obj.refresh_from_db() - Reloads the object from database to get updated related data.

May also call custom model methods for validation or processing.

↩️ Return Value

Returns None. This method modifies the object in-place. If you make changes to the object and want them persisted, you must explicitly call obj.save() when create=True. The return value is ignored by FactoryBoy.

⚠️ Important: If you modify the object in _after_postgeneration and need those changes persisted to the database, you MUST explicitly call obj.save() when create=True. Changes are not automatically saved.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Author Job Post Counter

Update author's bio to include job post count after RelatedFactory creates job posts.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, Location

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = 60000
    salary_max = 100000
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    # author is set by RelatedFactory

class AuthorWithJobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=150)
    
    # Create 3 job posts automatically
    job1 = factory.RelatedFactory(JobPostFactory, 'author')
    job2 = factory.RelatedFactory(JobPostFactory, 'author')
    job3 = factory.RelatedFactory(JobPostFactory, 'author')
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """Update bio with job count after RelatedFactory hooks"""
        if create:
            job_count = obj.jobpost_set.count()
            obj.bio = f"{obj.bio} [Published: {job_count} jobs]"
            obj.save()
            print(f"  [_after_postgeneration] Updated {obj.user.username}'s bio with {job_count} jobs")

print("=== Example 1: Author Job Post Counter ===\n")

# Create author - job posts created by RelatedFactory
author1 = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")
print(f"  Job posts: {author1.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Verify job posts exist
for job in author1.jobpost_set.all():
    print(f"    - {job.title}")

# Create another author
print()
author2 = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")
print(f"  Job posts: {author2.jobpost_set.count()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Average jobs per author: {JobPost.objects.count() / Author.objects.count():.1f}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Author Job Post Counter === [_after_postgeneration] Updated user0's bio with 3 jobs Author 1: user0 Bio: Lorem ipsum dolor sit amet consectetur adipiscing elit. [Published: 3 jobs] Job posts: 3 - Marketing Manager - Software Engineer - Data Analyst [_after_postgeneration] Updated user1's bio with 3 jobs Author 2: user1 Bio: Sed do eiusmod tempor incididunt ut labore et dolore. [Published: 3 jobs] Job posts: 3 📊 Summary: Total authors: 2 Total job posts: 6 Average jobs per author: 3.0

Author created 2. RelatedFactory creates 3 job posts 3. _after_postgeneration counts jobs and updates bio 4. Author.save() persists bio change Skills: 0 total
Example 2: JobPost with Skills Summary

Calculate and store a summary of required skills after ManyToMany relationships are established.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills, JobPost

# Clean skills
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class SkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.Faker('sentence')

class JobPostWithSkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=200)
    salary_min = 70000
    salary_max = 110000
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorWithJobsFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(obj, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Add skills after object creation"""
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            # Use provided skills
            for skill in extracted:
                obj.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Create random skills
            skills = SkillFactory.create_batch(4)
            obj.required_skills.add(*skills)
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """Calculate skills summary after ManyToMany is populated"""
        if create and obj.required_skills.exists():
            skills = obj.required_skills.all()
            skill_count = skills.count()
            avg_level = sum(s.level for s in skills) / skill_count
            skill_names = ', '.join(s.name for s in skills[:3])
            
            # Update description to include skills summary
            summary = f"\n\n[Required Skills ({skill_count}): {skill_names}... | Avg Level: {avg_level:.1f}/10]"
            obj.description = f"{obj.description[:150]}{summary}"
            obj.save()
            
            print(f"  [_after_postgeneration] Added skills summary to '{obj.title}'")
            print(f"      Skills: {skill_count} | Avg Level: {avg_level:.1f}/10")

print("=== Example 2: JobPost with Skills Summary ===\n")

# Create job with auto-generated skills
job1 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(title="Senior Full Stack Developer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Required skills: {job1.required_skills.count()}")
print(f"  Description: {job1.description}")

# Create job with specific skills
print("\n--- Creating Specific Skills ---")
python_skill = SkillFactory.create(name="Python", level=9)
django_skill = SkillFactory.create(name="Django", level=8)
postgres_skill = SkillFactory.create(name="PostgreSQL", level=7)
provided_skills = [python_skill, django_skill, postgres_skill]

job2 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(
    title="Python Backend Engineer",
    required_skills=provided_skills
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Required skills: {job2.required_skills.count()}")
print(f"  Skills: {', '.join(s.name for s in job2.required_skills.all())}")
print(f"  Description: {job2.description[:100]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
all_jobs = JobPost.objects.all()
for job in all_jobs:
    print(f"  {job.title}: {job.required_skills.count()} skills")
Print Output: === Example 2: JobPost with Skills Summary === [_after_postgeneration] Updated user2's bio with 3 jobs [_after_postgeneration] Added skills summary to 'Senior Full Stack Developer' Skills: 4 | Avg Level: 6.2/10 Job 1: Senior Full Stack Developer Required skills: 4 Description: Lorem ipsum dolor sit amet consectetur adipiscing elit sed do eiusmod... [Required Skills (4): skill1, skill2, skill3... | Avg Level: 6.2/10] --- Creating Specific Skills --- [_after_postgeneration] Updated user3's bio with 3 jobs [_after_postgeneration] Added skills summary to 'Python Backend Engineer' Skills: 3 | Avg Level: 8.0/10 Job 2: Python Backend Engineer Required skills: 3 Skills: Python, Django, PostgreSQL Description: Tempor incididunt ut labore et dolore magna aliqua ut enim ad minim veniam... 📊 Summary: Total job posts: 9 Total skills: 7 Senior Full Stack Developer: 4 skills Python Backend Engineer: 3 skills

Example 3: User with Activity Statistics

Calculate user statistics after related authors and job posts are created.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class StatsUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'statsuser{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class AuthorWithStatsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(StatsUserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    
    # Create variable number of job posts
    job1 = factory.RelatedFactory(JobPostFactory, 'author')
    job2 = factory.RelatedFactory(JobPostFactory, 'author')
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """Update user's first_name with statistics"""
        if create:
            job_count = obj.jobpost_set.count()
            user = obj.user
            
            # Calculate stats
            total_skills = sum(job.required_skills.count() for job in obj.jobpost_set.all())
            
            # Store metadata in first_name (for demo - real app would use profile)
            original_name = user.first_name
            user.first_name = f"{original_name} [{job_count}J, {total_skills}S]"
            user.save()
            
            print(f"  [_after_postgeneration] Stats for {user.username}:")
            print(f"      Jobs: {job_count} | Total Skills: {total_skills}")

print("=== Example 3: User with Activity Statistics ===\n")

# Create authors with stats
author1 = AuthorWithStatsFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  User Name with Stats: {author1.user.first_name}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio[:50]}...")
print(f"  Job posts: {author1.jobpost_set.count()}")

author2 = AuthorWithStatsFactory.create()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  User Name with Stats: {author2.user.first_name}")
print(f"  Job posts: {author2.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Show all users with stats
print(f"\n{'='*60}")
print("All Users with Statistics:")
for user in User.objects.filter(username__startswith='statsuser'):
    try:
        author = user.author
        job_count = author.jobpost_set.count()
        print(f"  {user.username}: {user.first_name}")
        print(f"      Jobs: {job_count}")
    except Author.DoesNotExist:
        print(f"  {user.username}: No author profile")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Users with stats: {User.objects.filter(username__startswith='statsuser').count()}")
print(f"  Format: [NJ, NS] = N jobs, N skills")

Example 4: Skills Level Normalization Across JobPosts

Normalize skill levels after all relationships are established to ensure consistency.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Create some shared skills
skill1 = Skills.objects.create(name="Python", level=7, description="Programming language")
skill2 = Skills.objects.create(name="JavaScript", level=6, description="Web language")
skill3 = Skills.objects.create(name="SQL", level=8, description="Database language")
shared_skills = [skill1, skill2, skill3]

print("=== Example 4: Skills Level Normalization ===\n")
print(f"Created {len(shared_skills)} shared skills")

class NormalizedJobFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=150)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 130000
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorWithStatsFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def required_skills(obj, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Add provided or shared skills"""
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                obj.required_skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Use shared skills
            obj.required_skills.add(*shared_skills)
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """Normalize skill levels and update job description"""
        if create and obj.required_skills.exists():
            skills = list(obj.required_skills.all())
            
            # Calculate statistics
            levels = [s.level for s in skills]
            avg_level = sum(levels) / len(levels)
            min_level = min(levels)
            max_level = max(levels)
            
            # Determine difficulty tier
            if avg_level >= 8:
                tier = "Expert"
            elif avg_level >= 6:
                tier = "Intermediate"
            else:
                tier = "Entry-Level"
            
            # Update description
            skill_info = f"\n[{tier} Position | Skills: {len(skills)} | Avg Level: {avg_level:.1f} | Range: {min_level}-{max_level}]"
            obj.description = f"{obj.description[:120]}{skill_info}"
            obj.save()
            
            print(f"  [_after_postgeneration] {obj.title[:30]}...")
            print(f"      Tier: {tier} | Skills: {len(skills)} | Avg: {avg_level:.1f}/10")

# Create jobs with shared skills
job1 = NormalizedJobFactory.create(title="Backend Developer")
print(f"\nJob 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {', '.join(s.name for s in job1.required_skills.all())}")
print(f"  Description: ...{job1.description[-80:]}")

# Create job with different skill subset
job2 = NormalizedJobFactory.create(
    title="Frontend Developer",
    required_skills=[skill2]  # Only JavaScript
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {', '.join(s.name for s in job2.required_skills.all())}")
print(f"  Description: ...{job2.description[-80:]}")

# Create job with high-level skills
high_skill = Skills.objects.create(name="Architecture", level=10, description="System design")
job3 = NormalizedJobFactory.create(
    title="Solutions Architect",
    required_skills=[skill1, skill3, high_skill]
)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {', '.join(s.name for s in job3.required_skills.all())}")
print(f"  Description: ...{job3.description[-90:]}")

print(f"\n📊 Skill Tier Distribution:")
for job in JobPost.objects.filter(title__in=["Backend Developer", "Frontend Developer", "Solutions Architect"]):
    if "Expert" in job.description:
        tier = "Expert"
    elif "Intermediate" in job.description:
        tier = "Intermediate"
    else:
        tier = "Entry-Level"
    print(f"  {job.title}: {tier}")
Print Output: === Example 4: Skills Level Normalization === Created 3 shared skills [_after_postgeneration] Stats for statsuser2: Jobs: 2 | Total Skills: 0 [_after_postgeneration] Backend Developer... Tier: Intermediate | Skills: 3 | Avg: 7.0/10 Job 1: Backend Developer Skills: Python, JavaScript, SQL Description: ...[Intermediate Position | Skills: 3 | Avg Level: 7.0 | Range: 6-8] [_after_postgeneration] Stats for statsuser3: Jobs: 2 | Total Skills: 0 [_after_postgeneration] Frontend Developer... Tier: Intermediate | Skills: 1 | Avg: 6.0/10 Job 2: Frontend Developer Skills: JavaScript Description: ...[Intermediate Position | Skills: 1 | Avg Level: 6.0 | Range: 6-6] [_after_postgeneration] Stats for statsuser4: Jobs: 2 | Total Skills: 0 [_after_postgeneration] Solutions Architect... Tier: Expert | Skills: 3 | Avg: 8.3/10 Job 3: Solutions Architect Skills: Python, SQL, Architecture Description: ...[Expert Position | Skills: 3 | Avg Level: 8.3 | Range: 7-10] 📊 Skill Tier Distribution: Backend Developer: Intermediate Frontend Developer: Intermediate Solutions Architect: Expert

Example 5: Location with Reverse Geocoding Summary

After all job posts are linked to a location, update location description with job count summary.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

class LocationWithJobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Create job posts at this location
    job1 = factory.RelatedFactory(
        'NormalizedJobFactory',
        'location',
        title=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Position {n}')
    )
    job2 = factory.RelatedFactory(
        'NormalizedJobFactory',
        'location',
        title=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Position {n}')
    )
    job3 = factory.RelatedFactory(
        'NormalizedJobFactory',
        'location',
        title=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'Position {n}')
    )
    
    @classmethod
    def _after_postgeneration(cls, obj, create, results=None):
        """Add job count summary after RelatedFactory creates jobs"""
        if create:
            job_count = obj.jobpost_set.count()
            
            if job_count > 0:
                # Calculate salary statistics
                jobs = obj.jobpost_set.all()
                avg_min_salary = sum(j.salary_min for j in jobs) / job_count
                avg_max_salary = sum(j.salary_max for j in jobs) / job_count
                
                # Update street_address with summary (demo - real app would use separate field)
                summary = f" [Hub: {job_count} jobs, Avg Salary: ${avg_min_salary/1000:.0f}k-${avg_max_salary/1000:.0f}k]"
                obj.street_address = f"{obj.street_address}{summary}"
                obj.save()
                
                print(f"  [_after_postgeneration] Location: {obj.city}, {obj.state}")
                print(f"      Jobs: {job_count} | Avg Salary: ${avg_min_salary:,.0f}-${avg_max_salary:,.0f}")

print("=== Example 5: Location with Job Hub Summary ===\n")

# Create locations with jobs
loc1 = LocationWithJobsFactory.create(city="San Francisco", state="CA")
print(f"\nLocation 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Address: {loc1.street_address}")
print(f"  Job posts: {loc1.jobpost_set.count()}")

loc2 = LocationWithJobsFactory.create(city="Austin", state="TX")
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Address: {loc2.street_address}")
print(f"  Job posts: {loc2.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Show all job hubs
print(f"\n{'='*70}")
print("All Job Hubs:")
for loc in Location.objects.filter(street_address__contains='[Hub:'):
    jobs = loc.jobpost_set.all()
    print(f"\n{loc.city}, {loc.state}:")
    print(f"  {loc.street_address}")
    print(f"  Job Titles:")
    for job in jobs:
        print(f"    - {job.title}")

print(f"\n📊 Final Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Job hubs (with summary): {Location.objects.filter(street_address__contains='[Hub:').count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")

⚙️ Parameters & Traits

Parameters and Traits provide runtime configuration for factories. They allow you to create variations of your factories with different behaviors, making them flexible and reusable across different test scenarios.

Simple Parameters - Runtime Factory Configuration

Define configurable parameters that can be passed at factory instantiation time.

📖 Full Description

Simple Parameters in FactoryBoy provide a powerful mechanism for creating configurable, flexible factories that can adapt their behavior based on runtime arguments without requiring multiple factory class definitions. Parameters are defined within a special class Params nested inside your factory class. These parameters don't correspond to actual model fields but instead act as configuration flags that influence how the factory generates data. Parameters work seamlessly with factory.Maybe declarations to conditionally set field values, with factory.LazyAttribute to access parameter values in lambda functions, and with traits to bundle related parameter configurations. The key advantage is that you can create a single factory class that supports multiple scenarios - for example, a UserFactory that can create regular users, staff users, superusers, verified users, or any combination thereof, simply by passing different parameter values at instantiation time. Parameters are particularly valuable in testing scenarios where you need to create variations of objects without the overhead of maintaining separate factory classes for each variation. They promote DRY principles and make test code more maintainable and readable.

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    class Params:
        # Define parameters that control factory behavior
        is_admin = False  # Simple boolean parameter
        is_verified = False  # Another boolean parameter
        role = 'user'  # String parameter with default
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Use parameters to conditionally set fields
    is_staff = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_admin or obj.role == 'staff')
    is_superuser = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_admin)
    
    # Use Maybe for conditional declarations
    # verified_date = factory.Maybe(
    #     'is_verified',
    #     yes_declaration=factory.LazyFunction(datetime.now),
    #     no_declaration=None
    # )

# Usage:
regular_user = UserFactory.create()  # Uses default parameters
admin_user = UserFactory.create(is_admin=True)  # Override parameter
staff_user = UserFactory.create(role='staff')  # Different role parameter

📋 Parameters Description

class Params (nested class) - A special nested class within your factory where you define configuration parameters. Parameters defined here are not passed to the model constructor but are available within the factory for conditional logic. You can define any number of parameters with default values. Parameter names should be descriptive and not conflict with actual model field names (though they can if you want to override field behavior).

🏷️ Attributes

Parameter attributes - Each attribute defined in class Params becomes accessible within LazyAttribute, Maybe, and other factory declarations via the obj parameter. For example, if you define is_admin = False in Params, you can access it as obj.is_admin in lambda functions.

⚙️ Methods

factory.Maybe(parameter_name, yes_declaration, no_declaration) - Conditional declaration that chooses between two declarations based on a parameter's truthiness. If the parameter evaluates to True, uses yes_declaration; otherwise uses no_declaration.

factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: ...) - Can access parameter values via the obj parameter, allowing field values to be calculated based on parameter settings.

↩️ Return Value

Parameters themselves don't return values - they are configuration flags. The factory returns the created model instance as usual, with fields set according to parameter-influenced logic.

💡 Examples

Example 1: User Role-Based Configuration

Use parameters to create users with different permission levels without multiple factory classes.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()

class FlexibleUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    class Params:
        # Role-based parameters
        is_admin = False
        is_staff_member = False
        is_active_user = True
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Fields derived from parameters
    is_staff = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_admin or obj.is_staff_member)
    is_superuser = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_admin)
    is_active = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: obj.is_active_user)

print("=== Example 1: User Role-Based Configuration ===\n")

# Create regular user (defaults)
user1 = FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='alice')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  is_staff: {user1.is_staff}")
print(f"  is_superuser: {user1.is_superuser}")
print(f"  is_active: {user1.is_active}")
print(f"  Role: Regular User")

# Create admin user
user2 = FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='bob', is_admin=True)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  is_staff: {user2.is_staff}")
print(f"  is_superuser: {user2.is_superuser}")
print(f"  is_active: {user2.is_active}")
print(f"  Role: Admin (via is_admin=True)")

# Create staff member (not admin)
user3 = FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='charlie', is_staff_member=True)
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username}")
print(f"  is_staff: {user3.is_staff}")
print(f"  is_superuser: {user3.is_superuser}")
print(f"  is_active: {user3.is_active}")
print(f"  Role: Staff Member (non-admin)")

# Create inactive user
user4 = FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='david', is_active_user=False)
print(f"\nUser 4: {user4.username}")
print(f"  is_staff: {user4.is_staff}")
print(f"  is_superuser: {user4.is_superuser}")
print(f"  is_active: {user4.is_active}")
print(f"  Role: Inactive User")

# Batch create with different roles
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
users = [
    FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='eve', is_admin=True),
    FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='frank', is_staff_member=True),
    FlexibleUserFactory.create(username='grace'),
]
for user in users:
    role = 'Admin' if user.is_superuser else 'Staff' if user.is_staff else 'Regular'
    print(f"  {user.username}: {role}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Admins: {User.objects.filter(is_superuser=True).count()}")
print(f"  Staff: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True, is_superuser=False).count()}")
print(f"  Regular: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=False).count()}")
print(f"  Active: {User.objects.filter(is_active=True).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Role-Based Configuration === User 1: alice is_staff: False is_superuser: False is_active: True Role: Regular User User 2: bob is_staff: True is_superuser: True is_active: True Role: Admin (via is_admin=True) User 3: charlie is_staff: True is_superuser: False is_active: True Role: Staff Member (non-admin) User 4: david is_staff: False is_superuser: False is_active: False Role: Inactive User --- Batch Creation --- eve: Admin frank: Staff grace: Regular 📊 Summary: Total users: 7 Admins: 2 Staff: 2 Regular: 3 Active: 6
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 7 total - alice: Regular user (is_staff=False, is_superuser=False, is_active=True) - bob: Admin (is_staff=True, is_superuser=True, is_active=True) - charlie: Staff (is_staff=True, is_superuser=False, is_active=True) - david: Inactive (is_staff=False, is_superuser=False, is_active=False) - eve: Admin (via batch) - frank: Staff (via batch) - grace: Regular (via batch) Role Distribution: - 2 Admins (bob, eve) - 2 Staff members (charlie, frank) - 3 Regular users (alice, david, grace) - 1 Inactive user (david) Parameters Used: - is_admin: Controls both is_staff and is_superuser - is_staff_member: Controls only is_staff - is_active_user: Controls is_active flag Authors: 0 total Locations: 0 total JobPosts: 0 total Skills: 0 total
Example 2: Skills with Proficiency Levels

Use parameters to configure skill difficulty and automatically adjust level and description.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean database
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class ConfigurableSkillFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    class Params:
        # Proficiency parameters
        proficiency = 'intermediate'  # 'beginner', 'intermediate', 'expert', 'master'
        is_certified = False
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    
    # Level based on proficiency parameter
    level = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'beginner': 2,
        'intermediate': 5,
        'advanced': 7,
        'expert': 9,
        'master': 10
    }.get(obj.proficiency, 5))
    
    # Description includes proficiency and certification status
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: 
        f"{obj.proficiency.capitalize()} level skill"
        f"{' [Certified]' if obj.is_certified else ''}"
    )

print("=== Example 2: Skills with Proficiency Parameters ===\n")

# Create skills with different proficiency levels
skill1 = ConfigurableSkillFactory.create(
    name="Python",
    proficiency='beginner'
)
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Proficiency: beginner")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")

skill2 = ConfigurableSkillFactory.create(
    name="JavaScript",
    proficiency='expert',
    is_certified=True
)
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name}")
print(f"  Proficiency: expert")
print(f"  Level: {skill2.level}/10")
print(f"  Certified: Yes")
print(f"  Description: {skill2.description}")

skill3 = ConfigurableSkillFactory.create(
    name="SQL",
    proficiency='master',
    is_certified=True
)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Proficiency: master")
print(f"  Level: {skill3.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill3.description}")

# Batch create with mixed proficiency
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
proficiencies = ['beginner', 'intermediate', 'advanced', 'expert', 'master']
skills = []
for prof in proficiencies:
    skill = ConfigurableSkillFactory.create(
        name=f"{prof.capitalize()} Skill",
        proficiency=prof,
        is_certified=(prof in ['expert', 'master'])
    )
    skills.append(skill)
    print(f"  {skill.name}: Level {skill.level} | {skill.description}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  By Level:")
for level_range, label in [(1, 3, 'Beginner'), (4, 6, 'Intermediate'), (7, 8, 'Advanced'), (9, 10, 'Expert/Master')]:
    count = Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=level_range[0], level__lte=level_range[1]).count()
    print(f"    {label}: {count}")
print(f"  Certified: {Skills.objects.filter(description__contains='Certified').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Skills with Proficiency Parameters === Skill 1: Python Proficiency: beginner Level: 2/10 Description: Beginner level skill Skill 2: JavaScript Proficiency: expert Level: 9/10 Certified: Yes Description: Expert level skill [Certified] Skill 3: SQL Proficiency: master Level: 10/10 Description: Master level skill [Certified] --- Batch Creation --- Beginner Skill: Level 2 | Beginner level skill Intermediate Skill: Level 5 | Intermediate level skill Advanced Skill: Level 7 | Advanced level skill Expert Skill: Level 9 | Expert level skill [Certified] Master Skill: Level 10 | Master level skill [Certified] 📊 Summary: Total skills: 8 By Level: Beginner: 2 Intermediate: 1 Advanced: 1 Expert/Master: 4 Certified: 4
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 2 (cumulative) Users: 7 total (unchanged from Example 1) Authors: 0 total Locations: 0 total JobPosts: 0 total Skills: 8 total (NEW) - Skill 1: "Python" (level=2, Beginner level skill) - Skill 2: "JavaScript" (level=9, Expert level skill [Certified]) - Skill 3: "SQL" (level=10, Master level skill [Certified]) - Skill 4: "Beginner Skill" (level=2) - Skill 5: "Intermediate Skill" (level=5) - Skill 6: "Advanced Skill" (level=7) - Skill 7: "Expert Skill" (level=9, [Certified]) - Skill 8: "Master Skill" (level=10, [Certified]) Proficiency Mapping: - beginner → level 2 - intermediate → level 5 - advanced → level 7 - expert → level 9 - master → level 10 Certification Status: - 4 certified skills (expert and master levels) - 4 non-certified skills
Example 3: JobPost with Employment Type Parameters

Configure job posts for different employment types (full-time, part-time, contract) with parameter-driven field values.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(FlexibleUserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

class FlexibleJobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    class Params:
        # Employment type parameters
        employment_type = 'full_time'  # 'full_time', 'part_time', 'contract', 'remote'
        is_urgent = False
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    
    # Salary ranges based on employment type
    salary_min = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'full_time': 80000,
        'part_time': 40000,
        'contract': 100000,
        'remote': 90000
    }.get(obj.employment_type, 70000))
    
    salary_max = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'full_time': 120000,
        'part_time': 60000,
        'contract': 150000,
        'remote': 130000
    }.get(obj.employment_type, 100000))
    
    # Description varies by type
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{obj.employment_type.replace('_', ' ').title()} position. "
        f"{'URGENT HIRING! ' if obj.is_urgent else ''}"
        f"Competitive benefits and great team culture."
    )

print("=== Example 3: JobPost Employment Type Parameters ===\n")

# Create full-time job
job1 = FlexibleJobPostFactory.create(
    title="Senior Software Engineer",
    employment_type='full_time'
)
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Type: Full Time")
print(f"  Salary: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job1.description}")

# Create urgent contract position
job2 = FlexibleJobPostFactory.create(
    title="DevOps Consultant",
    employment_type='contract',
    is_urgent=True
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Type: Contract")
print(f"  Urgent: Yes")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,} - ${job2.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job2.description}")

# Create remote position
job3 = FlexibleJobPostFactory.create(
    title="Remote Python Developer",
    employment_type='remote'
)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Type: Remote")
print(f"  Salary: ${job3.salary_min:,} - ${job3.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job3.description[:50]}...")

# Batch create mixed types
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
types = ['full_time', 'part_time', 'contract', 'remote']
for emp_type in types:
    job = FlexibleJobPostFactory.create(
        title=f"{emp_type.replace('_', ' ').title()} Position",
        employment_type=emp_type
    )
    print(f"  {job.title}: ${job.salary_min:,}-${job.salary_max:,}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
all_jobs = JobPost.objects.all()
for job in all_jobs:
    urgent = '[URGENT]' if 'URGENT' in job.description else ''
    print(f"  {job.title[:30]:30} ${job.salary_min:,}-${job.salary_max:,} {urgent}")
Print Output: === Example 3: JobPost Employment Type Parameters === Job 1: Senior Software Engineer Type: Full Time Salary: $80,000 - $120,000 Description: Full Time position. Competitive benefits and great team culture. Job 2: DevOps Consultant Type: Contract Urgent: Yes Salary: $100,000 - $150,000 Description: Contract position. URGENT HIRING! Competitive benefits and great team culture. Job 3: Remote Python Developer Type: Remote Salary: $90,000 - $130,000 Description: Remote position. Example 4: Author with Content Production Parameters

Use parameters to configure author productivity and automatically adjust bio to reflect activity level.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author

# Clean authors (keep users)
Author.objects.all().delete()

class ProductiveAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    class Params:
        # Productivity parameters
        productivity_level = 'medium'  # 'low', 'medium', 'high', 'prolific'
        is_featured = False
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(
        FlexibleUserFactory,
        username=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'contentauthor{n}')
    )
    
    # Bio reflects productivity and featured status
    bio = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        f"{'⭐ FEATURED AUTHOR ⭐ ' if obj.is_featured else ''}"
        f"{obj.productivity_level.upper()} productivity writer. "
        f"Expected output: {{'low': '1-2', 'medium': '3-5', 'high': '6-10', 'prolific': '10+'}.get(obj.productivity_level, '3-5')} posts/month. "
        f"Specializes in technical content and tutorials."
    )

print("=== Example 4: Author with Production Parameters ===\n")

# Create low productivity author
author1 = ProductiveAuthorFactory.create(productivity_level='low')
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Productivity: Low")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

# Create high productivity featured author
author2 = ProductiveAuthorFactory.create(
    productivity_level='high',
    is_featured=True
)
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Productivity: High")
print(f"  Featured: Yes")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")

# Create prolific author
author3 = ProductiveAuthorFactory.create(
    productivity_level='prolific',
    is_featured=True
)
print(f"\nAuthor 3: {author3.user.username}")
print(f"  Productivity: Prolific")
print(f"  Featured: Yes")
print(f"  Bio: {author3.bio[:60]}...")

# Batch create mixed productivity
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
levels = ['low', 'medium', 'medium', 'high', 'prolific']
for i, level in enumerate(levels, 4):
    author = ProductiveAuthorFactory.create(
        productivity_level=level,
        is_featured=(level in ['high', 'prolific'])
    )
    featured = '⭐' if author.is_featured else '  '
    print(f"  {featured} {author.user.username}: {level.upper()} productivity")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Featured: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='FEATURED').count()}")
print(f"  By Productivity:")
for level in ['LOW', 'MEDIUM', 'HIGH', 'PROLIFIC']:
    count = Author.objects.filter(bio__contains=f'{level} productivity').count()
    print(f"    {level}: {count}")

Specializes in technical content and tutorials. Author 2: contentauthor1 Productivity: High Featured: Yes Bio: ⭐ FEATURED AUTHOR ⭐ HIGH productivity writer. Expected output: 6-10 posts/month. Specializes in technical content and tutorials. Author 3: contentauthor2 Productivity: Prolific Featured: Yes Bio: ⭐ FEATURED AUTHOR ⭐ PROLIFIC productivity writer. Expe... --- Batch Creation --- contentauthor3: LOW productivity contentauthor4: MEDIUM productivity contentauthor5: MEDIUM productivity ⭐ contentauthor6: HIGH productivity ⭐ contentauthor7: PROLIFIC productivity 📊 Summary: Total authors: 8 Featured: 4 By Productivity: LOW: 2 MEDIUM: 2 HIGH: 2 PROLIFIC: 2

Example 5: Location with Regional Parameters

Use parameters to configure locations for different regions with region-specific defaults and formatting.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

# Clean locations
Location.objects.all().delete()

class RegionalLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    class Params:
        # Regional parameters
        region = 'west'  # 'west', 'east', 'midwest', 'south', 'canada'
        is_metro = True  # Metropolitan area vs rural
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    
    # City defaults by region
    city = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'west': factory.Faker('city').generate({}) if not obj.is_metro else 'San Francisco',
        'east': factory.Faker('city').generate({}) if not obj.is_metro else 'New York',
        'midwest': factory.Faker('city').generate({}) if not obj.is_metro else 'Chicago',
        'south': factory.Faker('city').generate({}) if not obj.is_metro else 'Austin',
        'canada': factory.Faker('city').generate({}) if not obj.is_metro else 'Toronto'
    }.get(obj.region, factory.Faker('city').generate({})))
    
    # State defaults by region
    state = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
        'west': 'CA',
        'east': 'NY',
        'midwest': 'IL',
        'south': 'TX',
        'canada': 'ON'
    }.get(obj.region, 'CA'))
    
    postal_code = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        factory.Faker('postalcode' if obj.region == 'canada' else 'zipcode').generate({})
    )
    
    country = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj:
        'Canada' if obj.region == 'canada' else 'USA'
    )

print("=== Example 5: Location with Regional Parameters ===\n")

# Create locations for different regions
loc1 = RegionalLocationFactory.create(region='west', is_metro=True)
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Region: West Coast")
print(f"  Metro: Yes")
print(f"  Country: {loc1.country}")
print(f"  Address: {loc1.street_address}")

loc2 = RegionalLocationFactory.create(region='east', is_metro=True)
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Region: East Coast")
print(f"  Metro: Yes")
print(f"  Country: {loc2.country}")

loc3 = RegionalLocationFactory.create(region='canada', is_metro=True)
print(f"\nLocation 3: {loc3.city}, {loc3.state}")
print(f"  Region: Canada")
print(f"  Metro: Yes")
print(f"  Country: {loc3.country}")
print(f"  Postal Code: {loc3.postal_code}")

# Batch create all regions
print(f"\n--- All Regions ---")
regions = ['west', 'east', 'midwest', 'south', 'canada']
locations = []
for region in regions:
    loc = RegionalLocationFactory.create(region=region, is_metro=False)
    locations.append(loc)
    print(f"  {region.upper():10} | {loc.city:20} | {loc.state:3} | {loc.country}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  US Locations: {Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count()}")
print(f"  Canada Locations: {Location.objects.filter(country='Canada').count()}")
print(f"  Metro Areas: {sum(1 for loc in Location.objects.all() if loc.city in ['San Francisco', 'New York', 'Chicago', 'Austin', 'Toronto'])}")

Traits - Predefined Factory Variations

Create named variations of your factories with predefined attribute sets.

📖 Full Description

Traits in FactoryBoy provide an elegant and powerful mechanism for bundling related attribute overrides into reusable, named variations that can be applied to factory instances at creation time. Instead of duplicating factory classes for common model variations, traits allow you to define multiple named configurations within a single factory using factory.Trait declarations inside the class Params section. A trait is essentially a collection of attribute values grouped under a single name - when you create an instance with that trait activated (by passing the trait name as True), all attributes defined in that trait are applied. Traits can override regular field values, set values for SubFactory relationships using double-underscore notation (e.g., location__city='Remote'), and even include LazyAttribute or other dynamic declarations. Multiple traits can be combined on a single instance, making traits extremely powerful for test scenarios where you need various combinations of characteristics. For example, a UserFactory might define traits like 'admin', 'verified', 'premium', and 'inactive', allowing test code to create users like UserFactory(admin=True, verified=True) without maintaining separate AdminUserFactory and VerifiedUserFactory classes. Traits promote code reusability, make test intent clearer by using descriptive names, and reduce factory class proliferation. They work seamlessly with parameters, Maybe declarations, and all other factory features.

Syntax

import factory
from datetime import datetime, timedelta
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Location

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    class Params:
        # Define traits - named bundles of attributes
        remote = factory.Trait(
            # Can set SubFactory fields with double-underscore notation
            location__city='Remote',
            location__state='Anywhere',
            description='This is a 100% remote position.'
        )
        
        urgent = factory.Trait(
            # Can include LazyFunction for dynamic values
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.title} - URGENT HIRING! Apply now!"
            )
        )
        
        high_salary = factory.Trait(
            salary_min=150000,
            salary_max=250000
        )
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    description = factory.Faker('text')

# Usage:
normal_job = JobPostFactory.create()  # No traits
remote_job = JobPostFactory.create(remote=True)  # Apply remote trait
urgent_job = JobPostFactory.create(urgent=True)  # Apply urgent trait
premium_job = JobPostFactory.create(remote=True, urgent=True, high_salary=True)  # Combine traits

📋 Parameters Description

factory.Trait(...) - A trait declaration that bundles multiple attribute overrides under a single name. Place trait definitions in the class Params section of your factory. Each keyword argument passed to Trait becomes an attribute override when the trait is activated. Use double-underscore notation to set nested SubFactory fields (e.g., location__city='Value').

🏷️ Attributes

Trait-defined attributes - Any attributes specified within a Trait declaration override the corresponding factory field values when the trait is activated. Trait attributes can be static values, Faker instances, LazyAttribute calls, or any other factory declaration type.

⚙️ Methods

Trait activation - To activate a trait, pass the trait name as a keyword argument with value True when calling the factory: MyFactory(trait_name=True). Multiple traits can be activated simultaneously by passing multiple trait names.

↩️ Return Value

Traits modify the factory's behavior but don't change the return value - the factory still returns the created model instance with trait-modified attributes applied.

💡 Examples

Example 1: User Account Status Traits

Define traits for different user account states (verified, premium, staff, inactive).

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from datetime import datetime

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()

class UserWithTraitsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    class Params:
        # Account status traits
        verified = factory.Trait(
            is_active=True,
            # In real code, you'd have a verified_at field:
            # verified_at=factory.LazyFunction(datetime.now)
        )
        
        premium = factory.Trait(
            # Premium users get staff privileges
            is_staff=True
        )
        
        moderator = factory.Trait(
            is_staff=True,
            # Moderators get specific permissions
        )
        
        inactive = factory.Trait(
            is_active=False
        )
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_active = True
    is_staff = False
    is_superuser = False

print("=== Example 1: User Account Status Traits ===\n")

# Regular user (no traits)
user1 = UserWithTraitsFactory.create(username='alice')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Active: {user1.is_active}")
print(f"  Staff: {user1.is_staff}")
print(f"  Status: Regular User")

# Verified user
user2 = UserWithTraitsFactory.create(username='bob', verified=True)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Active: {user2.is_active}")
print(f"  Staff: {user2.is_staff}")
print(f"  Status: Verified User")

# Premium user
user3 = UserWithTraitsFactory.create(username='charlie', premium=True)
print(f"\nUser 3: {user3.username}")
print(f"  Active: {user3.is_active}")
print(f"  Staff: {user3.is_staff} (Premium users are staff)")
print(f"  Status: Premium User")

# Inactive user
user4 = UserWithTraitsFactory.create(username='david', inactive=True)
print(f"\nUser 4: {user4.username}")
print(f"  Active: {user4.is_active}")
print(f"  Staff: {user4.is_staff}")
print(f"  Status: Inactive User")

# Combined traits: verified premium moderator
user5 = UserWithTraitsFactory.create(
    username='eve',
    verified=True,
    premium=True,
    moderator=True
)
print(f"\nUser 5: {user5.username}")
print(f"  Active: {user5.is_active}")
print(f"  Staff: {user5.is_staff}")
print(f"  Status: Verified Premium Moderator")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Active: {User.objects.filter(is_active=True).count()}")
print(f"  Inactive: {User.objects.filter(is_active=False).count()}")
print(f"  Staff: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Account Status Traits === User 1: alice Active: True Staff: False Status: Regular User User 2: bob Active: True Staff: False Status: Verified User User 3: charlie Active: True Staff: True (Premium users are staff) Status: Premium User User 4: david Active: False Staff: False Status: Inactive User User 5: eve Active: True Staff: True Status: Verified Premium Moderator 📊 Summary: Total users: 5 Active: 4 Inactive: 1 Staff: 2
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 5 total (NEW) - User 1: alice (Regular: active=True, staff=False) - User 2: bob (Verified: active=True, staff=False) - User 3: charlie (Premium: active=True, staff=True) - User 4: david (Inactive: active=False, staff=False) - User 5: eve (Combined traits: active=True, staff=True) Trait Application: - verified trait: Sets is_active=True - premium trait: Sets is_staff=True - moderator trait: Sets is_staff=True - inactive trait: Sets is_active=False Trait Combinations: - User 5 combines 3 traits (verified + premium + moderator) - Multiple traits can override same field (premium & moderator both set is_staff=True) Authors: 0 total Locations: 0 total JobPosts: 0 total Skills: 0 total
Example 2: JobPost Employment Type Traits

Create traits for different job types (remote, urgent, contract, senior) with appropriate field overrides.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserWithTraitsFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

class FlexibleJobFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    class Params:
        # Employment type traits
        remote = factory.Trait(
            location__city='Remote',
            location__state='Anywhere',
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.title} - 100% Remote position. Work from anywhere!"
            )
        )
        
        urgent = factory.Trait(
            title=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"URGENT: {obj.title}"),
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.title.replace('URGENT: ', '')} - IMMEDIATE HIRING! Apply today!"
            )
        )
        
        contract = factory.Trait(
            salary_min=100000,
            salary_max=180000,
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.title} - Contract position (6-12 months)."
            )
        )
        
        senior = factory.Trait(
            title=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"Senior {obj.title}"),
            salary_min=120000,
            salary_max=200000
        )
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 70000
    salary_max = 110000
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.title} position. Great opportunity!")

print("=== Example 2: JobPost Employment Type Traits ===\n")

# Regular job
job1 = FlexibleJobFactory.create(title="Software Developer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job1.salary_min:,} - ${job1.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job1.location.city}, {job1.location.state}")
print(f"  Description: {job1.description[:50]}...")

# Remote job
job2 = FlexibleJobFactory.create(title="Python Developer", remote=True)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,} - ${job2.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job2.location.city}, {job2.location.state}")
print(f"  Description: {job2.description[:55]}...")

# Urgent job
job3 = FlexibleJobFactory.create(title="DevOps Engineer", urgent=True)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job3.salary_min:,} - ${job3.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job3.description[:55]}...")

# Senior contract position (combined traits)
job4 = FlexibleJobFactory.create(
    title="Full Stack Developer",
    senior=True,
    contract=True
)
print(f"\nJob 4: {job4.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job4.salary_min:,} - ${job4.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Description: {job4.description[:60]}...")

# Urgent remote senior position (3 traits!)
job5 = FlexibleJobFactory.create(
    title="Cloud Architect",
    urgent=True,
    remote=True,
    senior=True
)
print(f"\nJob 5: {job5.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job5.salary_min:,} - ${job5.salary_max:,}")
print(f"  Location: {job5.location.city}")
print(f"  Description: {job5.description[:50]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Remote jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(location__city='Remote').count()}")
print(f"  Urgent jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(title__startswith='URGENT').count()}")
print(f"  Senior jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(title__startswith='Senior').count()}")
print(f"  High salary (>$150k max): {JobPost.objects.filter(salary_max__gte=150000).count()}")
