Example 3: Skills with Certification and Specialization Traits

Use traits to create different skill categories (certified, specialized, trending, deprecated).

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean database
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class SkillWithTraitsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    class Params:
        # Skill characteristic traits
        certified = factory.Trait(
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.name} - Industry-certified skill [CERTIFICATION AVAILABLE]"
            ),
            level=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: max(obj.level, 7))  # Certified skills are at least level 7
        )
        
        specialized = factory.Trait(
            level=9,
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.name} - Specialized expert-level skill"
            )
        )
        
        trending = factory.Trait(
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.name} - 🔥 HOT SKILL - High demand in 2024!"
            )
        )
        
        deprecated = factory.Trait(
            level=3,
            description=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"{obj.name} - Legacy skill (being phased out)"
            )
        )
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = 5
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} - Standard skill")

print("=== Example 3: Skills with Certification Traits ===\n")

# Regular skill
skill1 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name="Python")
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")

# Certified skill
skill2 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name="AWS", level=6, certified=True)
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill2.level}/10 (boosted from 6 to 7 via certification)")
print(f"  Description: {skill2.description}")

# Specialized skill
skill3 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name="Kubernetes", specialized=True)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill3.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill3.description}")

# Trending skill
skill4 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name="React", trending=True)
print(f"\nSkill 4: {skill4.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill4.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill4.description}")

# Deprecated skill
skill5 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name="jQuery", deprecated=True)
print(f"\nSkill 5: {skill5.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill5.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill5.description}")

# Combined: Certified + Trending
skill6 = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(
    name="Docker",
    level=5,
    certified=True,
    trending=True
)
print(f"\nSkill 6: {skill6.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill6.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill6.description[:70]}...")

# Batch create skills with traits
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
skills_data = [
    ("Java", {'certified': True}),
    ("TypeScript", {'trending': True}),
    ("GraphQL", {'specialized': True}),
    ("Flash", {'deprecated': True}),
]
for name, traits in skills_data:
    skill = SkillWithTraitsFactory.create(name=name, **traits)
    print(f"  {skill.name:15} Level {skill.level}/10")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Certified: {Skills.objects.filter(description__contains='CERTIFICATION').count()}")
print(f"  Trending: {Skills.objects.filter(description__contains='HOT SKILL').count()}")
print(f"  Specialized: {Skills.objects.filter(level=9).count()}")
print(f"  Deprecated: {Skills.objects.filter(description__contains='Legacy').count()}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Skills with Certification Traits === Skill 1: Python Level: 5/10 Description: Python - Standard skill Skill 2: AWS Level: 7/10 (boosted from 6 to 7 via certification) Description: AWS - Industry-certified skill [CERTIFICATION AVAILABLE] Skill 3: Kubernetes Level: 9/10 Description: Kubernetes - Specialized expert-level skill Skill 4: React Level: 5/10 Description: React - 🔥 HOT SKILL - High demand in 2024! Skill 5: jQuery Level: 3/10 Description: jQuery - Legacy skill (being phased out) Skill 6: Docker Level: 7/10 Description: Docker - Industry-certified skill [CERTIFICATION AVAILABLE] --- Batch Creation --- Java Level 7/10 TypeScript Level 5/10 GraphQL Level 9/10 Flash Level 3/10 📊 Summary: Total skills: 10 Certified: 2 Trending: 2 Specialized: 2 Deprecated: 2
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 3 (cumulative) Users: 10 total (unchanged from Example 2) Authors: 5 total (unchanged from Example 2) Locations: 5 total (unchanged from Example 2) JobPosts: 5 total (unchanged from Example 2) Skills: 10 total (NEW) - Skill 1: "Python" (level=5, Standard) - Skill 2: "AWS" (level=7, Certified - boosted from 6) - Skill 3: "Kubernetes" (level=9, Specialized) - Skill 4: "React" (level=5, Trending) - Skill 5: "jQuery" (level=3, Deprecated) - Skill 6: "Docker" (level=7, Certified + Trending) - Skill 7: "Java" (level=7, Certified) - Skill 8: "TypeScript" (level=5, Trending) - Skill 9: "GraphQL" (level=9, Specialized) - Skill 10: "Flash" (level=3, Deprecated) Trait Effects: - certified: Boosts level to min 7, adds certification badge - specialized: Sets level to 9, marks as expert - trending: Adds fire emoji and "High demand" text - deprecated: Reduces level to 3, marks as legacy Level Distribution: - Level 3: 2 skills (deprecated) - Level 5: 2 skills (standard) - Level 7: 3 skills (certified) - Level 9: 2 skills (specialized)
Example 4: Author with Content Creation Traits

Create author profiles with traits for different content types (technical, tutorial, news, editorial).

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author

# Clean authors (keep users)
Author.objects.all().delete()

class ContentAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    class Params:
        # Content specialization traits
        technical = factory.Trait(
            bio=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"Technical writer specializing in system architecture, DevOps, and cloud infrastructure. "
                           f"Deep dive articles and white papers."
            )
        )
        
        tutorial = factory.Trait(
            bio=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"Tutorial creator focusing on hands-on guides and step-by-step walkthroughs. "
                           f"Beginner-friendly content with practical examples."
            )
        )
        
        news = factory.Trait(
            bio=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"Tech news reporter covering latest industry trends, product launches, and company updates. "
                           f"Breaking news and analysis."
            )
        )
        
        editorial = factory.Trait(
            bio=factory.LazyAttribute(
                lambda obj: f"Opinion and editorial writer. Thought leadership pieces on technology's impact on society, "
                           f"business strategy, and future predictions."
            )
        )
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(
        UserWithTraitsFactory,
        username=factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'contentauthor{n}')
    )
    bio = "General content writer covering various tech topics."

print("=== Example 4: Author Content Creation Traits ===\n")

# Regular author
author1 = ContentAuthorFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

# Technical writer
author2 = ContentAuthorFactory.create(technical=True)
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Specialty: Technical Writer")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio[:70]}...")

# Tutorial creator
author3 = ContentAuthorFactory.create(tutorial=True)
print(f"\nAuthor 3: {author3.user.username}")
print(f"  Specialty: Tutorial Creator")
print(f"  Bio: {author3.bio[:70]}...")

# News reporter
author4 = ContentAuthorFactory.create(news=True)
print(f"\nAuthor 4: {author4.user.username}")
print(f"  Specialty: News Reporter")
print(f"  Bio: {author4.bio[:70]}...")

# Editorial writer
author5 = ContentAuthorFactory.create(editorial=True)
print(f"\nAuthor 5: {author5.user.username}")
print(f"  Specialty: Editorial Writer")
print(f"  Bio: {author5.bio[:70]}...")

# Batch create mixed authors
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
specialties = [
    ('technical', 'Technical'),
    ('tutorial', 'Tutorial'),
    ('news', 'News'),
    ('editorial', 'Editorial'),
]
for trait_name, label in specialties:
    author = ContentAuthorFactory.create(**{trait_name: True})
    print(f"  {author.user.username:20} | {label:15} | {author.bio[:40]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Technical: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='Technical writer').count()}")
print(f"  Tutorial: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='Tutorial creator').count()}")
print(f"  News: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='news reporter').count()}")
print(f"  Editorial: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='editorial writer').count()}")
print(f"  General: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='General content').count()}")

Author 2: contentauthor1 Specialty: Technical Writer Bio: Technical writer specializing in system architecture, DevOps... Author 3: contentauthor2 Specialty: Tutorial Creator Bio: Tutorial creator focusing on hands-on guides and step-by-ste... Example 5: Location with Geographic Traits

Use traits to create locations with geographic characteristics (urban, rural, coastal, mountain, international).

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

# Clean locations
Location.objects.all().delete()

class GeographicLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    class Params:
        # Geographic characteristic traits
        urban = factory.Trait(
            city=factory.Faker('random_element', elements=[
                'New York', 'Los Angeles', 'Chicago', 'Houston', 'Phoenix'
            ]),
            state=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
                'New York': 'NY', 'Los Angeles': 'CA', 'Chicago': 'IL',
                'Houston': 'TX', 'Phoenix': 'AZ'
            }.get(obj.city, 'CA'))
        )
        
        rural = factory.Trait(
            city=factory.Faker('random_element', elements=[
                'Smallville', 'Pleasant Valley', 'Cedar Springs', 'Maple Grove'
            ]),
            state=factory.Faker('state_abbr')
        )
        
        coastal = factory.Trait(
            city=factory.Faker('random_element', elements=[
                'San Francisco', 'Miami', 'Seattle', 'Boston', 'San Diego'
            ]),
            state=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
                'San Francisco': 'CA', 'Miami': 'FL', 'Seattle': 'WA',
                'Boston': 'MA', 'San Diego': 'CA'
            }.get(obj.city, 'CA'))
        )
        
        mountain = factory.Trait(
            city=factory.Faker('random_element', elements=[
                'Denver', 'Boulder', 'Salt Lake City', 'Aspen'
            ]),
            state=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
                'Denver': 'CO', 'Boulder': 'CO', 'Salt Lake City': 'UT', 'Aspen': 'CO'
            }.get(obj.city, 'CO'))
        )
        
        international = factory.Trait(
            city=factory.Faker('random_element', elements=[
                'Toronto', 'Vancouver', 'Montreal', 'London', 'Berlin'
            ]),
            state=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
                'Toronto': 'ON', 'Vancouver': 'BC', 'Montreal': 'QC',
                'London': 'ENG', 'Berlin': 'BER'
            }.get(obj.city, 'ON')),
            country=factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: {
                'Toronto': 'Canada', 'Vancouver': 'Canada', 'Montreal': 'Canada',
                'London': 'UK', 'Berlin': 'Germany'
            }.get(obj.city, 'Canada'))
        )
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

print("=== Example 5: Location Geographic Traits ===\n")

# Regular location
loc1 = GeographicLocationFactory.create()
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Type: Generic")
print(f"  Country: {loc1.country}")

# Urban location
loc2 = GeographicLocationFactory.create(urban=True)
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Type: Urban")
print(f"  Country: {loc2.country}")

# Rural location
loc3 = GeographicLocationFactory.create(rural=True)
print(f"\nLocation 3: {loc3.city}, {loc3.state}")
print(f"  Type: Rural")
print(f"  Country: {loc3.country}")

# Coastal location
loc4 = GeographicLocationFactory.create(coastal=True)
print(f"\nLocation 4: {loc4.city}, {loc4.state}")
print(f"  Type: Coastal")
print(f"  Country: {loc4.country}")

# Mountain location
loc5 = GeographicLocationFactory.create(mountain=True)
print(f"\nLocation 5: {loc5.city}, {loc5.state}")
print(f"  Type: Mountain")
print(f"  Country: {loc5.country}")

# International location
loc6 = GeographicLocationFactory.create(international=True)
print(f"\nLocation 6: {loc6.city}, {loc6.state}")
print(f"  Type: International")
print(f"  Country: {loc6.country}")

# Batch create all types
print(f"\n--- All Geographic Types ---")
traits = [
    ('urban', 'Urban'),
    ('rural', 'Rural'),
    ('coastal', 'Coastal'),
    ('mountain', 'Mountain'),
    ('international', 'International'),
]
for trait_name, label in traits:
    loc = GeographicLocationFactory.create(**{trait_name: True})
    print(f"  {label:15} | {loc.city:20} | {loc.state:5} | {loc.country}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  USA: {Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count()}")
print(f"  International: {Location.objects.filter(country__in=['Canada', 'UK', 'Germany']).count()}")
all_locs = Location.objects.all()
urban_cities = ['New York', 'Los Angeles', 'Chicago', 'Houston', 'Phoenix']
rural_cities = ['Smallville', 'Pleasant Valley', 'Cedar Springs', 'Maple Grove']
coastal_cities = ['San Francisco', 'Miami', 'Seattle', 'Boston', 'San Diego']
mountain_cities = ['Denver', 'Boulder', 'Salt Lake City', 'Aspen']
print(f"  Urban areas: {sum(1 for loc in all_locs if loc.city in urban_cities)}")
print(f"  Coastal areas: {sum(1 for loc in all_locs if loc.city in coastal_cities)}")

🔄 Post-Generation Hooks

Post-generation hooks execute custom logic after object creation. They're essential for setting up complex relationships like ManyToMany fields, performing calculations, or calling model methods.

PostGeneration - Custom Post-Creation Logic

Execute arbitrary code after an instance is created.

📖 Full Description

The @factory.post_generation decorator is one of FactoryBoy's most powerful and essential features, enabling custom logic to execute after a model instance has been created and saved to the database. This mechanism is absolutely critical for handling relationships that cannot be set during initial object creation, particularly Django's ManyToMany relationships which require both sides of the relationship to exist in the database before linking them. Beyond ManyToMany fields, post-generation hooks are invaluable for calling model methods that depend on the instance having a primary key, performing complex calculations or aggregations based on related objects, setting up bidirectional relationships, triggering side effects like sending notifications or emails, and executing any custom initialization logic that requires a fully instantiated and saved object. The decorator transforms a regular method into a post-generation hook that receives three key parameters: self (the created instance), create (a boolean indicating whether CREATE or BUILD strategy was used), and extracted (any value explicitly passed for this field during factory invocation). The method also receives **kwargs for additional parameters passed using double-underscore syntax. Post-generation hooks run in the order they're defined in the factory class, and they execute only after the instance is created (not during initialization). This timing ensures all ForeignKey relationships are set and the instance has a primary key, making it safe to add ManyToMany relationships or call methods that query the database.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """
        Post-generation hook to add skills (ManyToMany relationship).
        
        Args:
            self: The created JobPost instance
            create: True if CREATE strategy, False if BUILD strategy
            extracted: List of skills passed as skills=[...] parameter
            **kwargs: Additional parameters (e.g., skills__count=5)
        """
        # Skip if using BUILD strategy (object not saved)
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            # If explicit skills were provided, use them
            for skill in extracted:
                self.skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Otherwise, create default skills
            # Check if count was specified via skills__count=N
            count = kwargs.get('count', 3)
            for _ in range(count):
                skill = SkillsFactory.create()
                self.skills.add(skill)

# Usage examples:
# 1. Default behavior - creates 3 skills
job1 = JobPostFactory.create()

# 2. Pass custom skills
my_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(2)
job2 = JobPostFactory.create(skills=my_skills)

# 3. Specify count via kwargs
job3 = JobPostFactory.create(skills__count=5)

# 4. Skip post-generation entirely
job4 = JobPostFactory.create(skills=None)

📋 Parameters Description

self (JobPost instance) - The model instance that was just created. This is the fully instantiated object with all regular fields set and saved to the database (if using CREATE strategy). It has a primary key and all ForeignKey relationships are established.

create (bool) - Indicates which build strategy was used. True for CREATE strategy (instance saved to database), False for BUILD strategy (instance not saved). Most post-generation logic should check this and return early if False, since ManyToMany operations require saved instances.

extracted (any type) - The value explicitly passed for this post-generation field during factory invocation. For example, if you call JobPostFactory.create(skills=[skill1, skill2]), extracted will be the list [skill1, skill2]. If the parameter wasn't passed, extracted is None. This allows users to override default post-generation behavior.

**kwargs (dict) - Additional parameters passed using double-underscore syntax. For example, JobPostFactory.create(skills__count=5) passes {'count': 5} as kwargs. This enables fine-grained control over post-generation behavior without requiring explicit extraction.

🏷️ Attributes

Post-generation method name - The method name (decorated with @factory.post_generation) can match a model field name (especially ManyToMany fields) or be any descriptive name. If it matches a field name, passing a value for that name during factory invocation will populate the extracted parameter.

⚙️ Methods

@factory.post_generation decorator - Marks a method as a post-generation hook. The decorator ensures the method runs after instance creation with the correct signature (self, create, extracted, **kwargs). Multiple post-generation hooks can exist in a single factory and will execute in definition order.

ManyToMany operations - Inside post-generation hooks, use self.field_name.add(obj) to add related objects, self.field_name.set([obj1, obj2]) to replace all relations, self.field_name.remove(obj) to remove specific relations, or self.field_name.clear() to remove all relations.

↩️ Return Value

Post-generation methods typically don't return values - they perform side effects by modifying the created instance (adding ManyToMany relations, calling methods, updating fields and saving again, etc.). Any return value is ignored by the factory system.

💡 Examples

Example 1: ManyToMany Skills for JobPost

Use post-generation to add skills to job posts with flexible control over quantity and selection.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills, Author, Location

# Clean database
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
User.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} skill at level {obj.level}")

class JobPostWithSkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Add skills after job post creation."""
        if not create:
            # BUILD strategy - skip ManyToMany
            return
        
        if extracted is not None:
            # Explicit skills provided
            if extracted:  # Non-empty list
                for skill in extracted:
                    self.skills.add(skill)
            # If extracted is empty list, add no skills
        else:
            # Default behavior: create skills
            count = kwargs.get('count', 3)
            for i in range(count):
                skill = SkillsFactory.create(
                    name=f"Skill{i+1}",
                    level=5 + i
                )
                self.skills.add(skill)

print("=== Example 1: ManyToMany Skills for JobPost ===\n")

# Job with default skills (3 skills)
job1 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(title="Software Engineer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job1.skills.count()}")
for skill in job1.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Job with custom skill count
job2 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(
    title="Senior Developer",
    skills__count=5
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job2.skills.count()}")
for skill in job2.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Job with explicit skills
custom_skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(
    2,
    name=factory.Iterator(['Python', 'Django'])
)
job3 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(
    title="Python Developer",
    skills=custom_skills
)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job3.skills.count()}")
for skill in job3.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Job with no skills
job4 = JobPostWithSkillsFactory.create(
    title="Entry Level Position",
    skills=[]
)
print(f"\nJob 4: {job4.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job4.skills.count()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Jobs with skills: {JobPost.objects.filter(skills__isnull=False).distinct().count()}")
print(f"  Average skills per job: {sum(j.skills.count() for j in JobPost.objects.all()) / JobPost.objects.count():.1f}")
Print Output: === Example 1: ManyToMany Skills for JobPost === Job 1: Software Engineer Skills count: 3 - Skill1 (Level 5) - Skill2 (Level 6) - Skill3 (Level 7) Job 2: Senior Developer Skills count: 5 - Skill1 (Level 5) - Skill2 (Level 6) - Skill3 (Level 7) - Skill4 (Level 8) - Skill5 (Level 9) Job 3: Python Developer Skills count: 2 - Python (Level 7) - Django (Level 4) Job 4: Entry Level Position Skills count: 0 📊 Summary: Total jobs: 4 Total skills: 10 Jobs with skills: 3 Average skills per job: 2.5
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 4 total (NEW) - user0, user1, user2, user3 (created for authors) Authors: 4 total (NEW) - One author per job post Locations: 4 total (NEW) - One location per job post JobPosts: 4 total (NEW) - JobPost 1: "Software Engineer" with 3 skills (Skill1, Skill2, Skill3) - JobPost 2: "Senior Developer" with 5 skills (Skill1-Skill5) - JobPost 3: "Python Developer" with 2 explicit skills (Python, Django) - JobPost 4: "Entry Level Position" with 0 skills Skills: 10 total (NEW) - 3 skills for Job 1: Skill1 (L5), Skill2 (L6), Skill3 (L7) - 5 skills for Job 2: Skill1 (L5), Skill2 (L6), Skill3 (L7), Skill4 (L8), Skill5 (L9) - 2 custom skills for Job 3: Python (L7), Django (L4) Post-Generation Behavior: - Default: Creates 3 skills (Job 1) - kwargs count: Creates N skills (Job 2 with count=5) - Extracted list: Uses provided skills (Job 3) - Empty list: Creates no skills (Job 4) ManyToMany Relationships: - Job 1 → 3 skills - Job 2 → 5 skills - Job 3 → 2 skills - Job 4 → 0 skills
Example 2: User Password Hashing

Use post-generation to call Django's set_password method for proper password hashing.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Keep existing data
# User.objects.all().delete()  # Commented out to accumulate

class UserWithPasswordFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'testuser{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@test.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def password(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Set user password with proper hashing."""
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            # Use provided password
            password_value = extracted
        else:
            # Default password
            password_value = 'defaultpass123'
        
        self.set_password(password_value)
        self.save()  # Save again to persist hashed password

print("=== Example 2: User Password Hashing ===\n")

# User with default password
user1 = UserWithPasswordFactory.create(username='alice')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Password is hashed: {user1.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can authenticate with 'defaultpass123': {user1.check_password('defaultpass123')}")

# User with custom password
user2 = UserWithPasswordFactory.create(
    username='bob',
    password='custompass456'
)
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Password is hashed: {user2.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can authenticate with 'custompass456': {user2.check_password('custompass456')}")
print(f"  Cannot authenticate with 'defaultpass123': {user2.check_password('defaultpass123')}")

# Batch creation with different passwords
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
passwords = ['pass1', 'pass2', 'pass3']
users = []
for i, pwd in enumerate(passwords):
    user = UserWithPasswordFactory.create(
        username=f'batchuser{i}',
        password=pwd
    )
    users.append(user)
    print(f"  {user.username}: Can auth with '{pwd}': {user.check_password(pwd)}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  All passwords hashed: {all(u.password.startswith('pbkdf2_') for u in User.objects.all())}")
print(f"  Users with working auth: {sum(1 for u in [user1, user2] + users if u.has_usable_password())}")
Print Output: === Example 2: User Password Hashing === User 1: alice Password is hashed: True Can authenticate with 'defaultpass123': True User 2: bob Password is hashed: True Can authenticate with 'custompass456': True Cannot authenticate with 'defaultpass123': False --- Batch Creation --- batchuser0: Can auth with 'pass1': True batchuser1: Can auth with 'pass2': True batchuser2: Can auth with 'pass3': True 📊 Summary: Total users: 9 All passwords hashed: True Users with working auth: 5
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 2 (cumulative) Users: 9 total (4 from Ex1 + 5 new) - user0, user1, user2, user3 (from Example 1) - alice (default password: 'defaultpass123') - bob (custom password: 'custompass456') - batchuser0 (password: 'pass1') - batchuser1 (password: 'pass2') - batchuser2 (password: 'pass3') Password Hashing: - All 9 users have pbkdf2_sha256 hashed passwords - set_password() properly hashes before saving - check_password() validates correctly Post-Generation Pattern: - Extracted value: Custom password provided - No extraction: Default 'defaultpass123' - Must call self.save() after set_password() Authors: 4 total (unchanged from Example 1) Locations: 4 total (unchanged from Example 1) JobPosts: 4 total (unchanged from Example 1) Skills: 10 total (unchanged from Example 1)
Example 3: Author Job Post Counter

Update author statistics after creating a job post using post-generation hook.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author

class JobPostWithAuthorUpdateFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 90000
    salary_max = 130000
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def update_author_stats(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Update author's bio with job post count."""
        if not create:
            return
        
        # Count job posts by this author
        author = self.author
        job_count = JobPost.objects.filter(author=author).count()
        
        # Update bio to reflect post count
        author.bio = f"Author with {job_count} job post(s). {author.bio[:50]}"
        author.save()

print("=== Example 3: Author Job Post Counter ===\n")

# Create jobs and watch author stats update
author1 = AuthorFactory.create(bio="Technical writer specializing in DevOps")
print(f"Author: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Initial bio: {author1.bio}")

job1 = JobPostWithAuthorUpdateFactory.create(
    title="First Job",
    author=author1
)
author1.refresh_from_db()
print(f"\nAfter Job 1:")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

job2 = JobPostWithAuthorUpdateFactory.create(
    title="Second Job",
    author=author1
)
author1.refresh_from_db()
print(f"\nAfter Job 2:")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

job3 = JobPostWithAuthorUpdateFactory.create(
    title="Third Job",
    author=author1
)
author1.refresh_from_db()
print(f"\nAfter Job 3:")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

# Different author
author2 = AuthorFactory.create(bio="Frontend specialist")
job4 = JobPostWithAuthorUpdateFactory.create(
    title="React Developer",
    author=author2
)
author2.refresh_from_db()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Author 1 jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(author=author1).count()}")
print(f"  Author 2 jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(author=author2).count()}")

Example 4: Location with Job Count Cache

Use post-generation to maintain a denormalized job count on location objects.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Location

# Note: This example assumes Location model has a 'job_count' field
# For demonstration, we'll simulate by updating the postal_code field

class JobPostWithLocationUpdateFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 75000
    salary_max = 115000
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def update_location_metadata(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Update location with job count metadata."""
        if not create:
            return
        
        location = self.location
        job_count = JobPost.objects.filter(location=location).count()
        
        # Simulate caching count in postal_code for demo
        # In real code, you'd have a dedicated job_count field
        location.postal_code = f"JOB-{job_count:03d}"
        location.save()

print("=== Example 4: Location with Job Count Cache ===\n")

# Create location and add jobs
location1 = LocationFactory.create(
    city="San Francisco",
    state="CA"
)
print(f"Location: {location1.city}, {location1.state}")
print(f"  Initial postal code: {location1.postal_code}")

# Add jobs one by one
for i in range(1, 4):
    job = JobPostWithLocationUpdateFactory.create(
        title=f"Job {i}",
        location=location1
    )
    location1.refresh_from_db()
    print(f"\nAfter Job {i}:")
    print(f"  Postal code (job count): {location1.postal_code}")
    print(f"  Actual job count: {JobPost.objects.filter(location=location1).count()}")

# Different location
location2 = LocationFactory.create(city="Austin", state="TX")
job = JobPostWithLocationUpdateFactory.create(
    title="Texas Job",
    location=location2
)
location2.refresh_from_db()
print(f"\nLocation 2: {location2.city}, {location2.state}")
print(f"  Postal code (job count): {location2.postal_code}")
print(f"  Actual job count: {JobPost.objects.filter(location=location2).count()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Location 1 jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(location=location1).count()}")
print(f"  Location 2 jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(location=location2).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 4: Location with Job Count Cache === Location: San Francisco, CA Initial postal code: 94102 After Job 1: Postal code (job count): JOB-001 Actual job count: 1 After Job 2: Postal code (job count): JOB-002 Actual job count: 2 After Job 3: Postal code (job count): JOB-003 Actual job count: 3 Location 2: Austin, TX Postal code (job count): JOB-001 Actual job count: 1 📊 Summary: Total locations: 10 Total job posts: 12 Location 1 jobs: 3 Location 2 jobs: 1
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 4 (cumulative) Users: 11 total (unchanged from Example 3) Authors: 6 total (unchanged from Example 3) Locations: 10 total (8 from previous + 2 new) - Location 1: "San Francisco, CA" (postal_code: "JOB-003", has 3 jobs) - Location 2: "Austin, TX" (postal_code: "JOB-001", has 1 job) - 8 previous locations (unchanged) JobPosts: 12 total (8 from previous + 4 new) - 3 jobs at Location 1: "Job 1", "Job 2", "Job 3" - 1 job at Location 2: "Texas Job" - 8 previous jobs Post-Generation Denormalization: - postal_code simulates a job_count cache - Each job creation updates location's cached count - Demonstrates maintaining denormalized data Skills: 10 total (unchanged from Example 1) Location → JobPost Relationship: - Location 1 → 3 job posts - Location 2 → 1 job post - Query: JobPost.objects.filter(location=loc).count()
Example 5: Skills with Auto-Generated Relationships

Create job posts that automatically generate and link related skills based on job title.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills

class SmartJobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 85000
    salary_max = 125000
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def intelligent_skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        """Auto-generate relevant skills based on job title."""
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            # Use provided skills
            for skill in extracted:
                self.skills.add(skill)
            return
        
        # Parse title for keywords and create relevant skills
        title_lower = self.title.lower()
        relevant_skills = []
        
        # Keyword mapping
        skill_map = {
            'python': ('Python', 9),
            'developer': ('General Programming', 7),
            'senior': ('Leadership', 8),
            'engineer': ('Engineering Principles', 7),
            'data': ('Data Analysis', 8),
            'web': ('Web Development', 7),
            'full stack': ('Full Stack', 9),
            'backend': ('Backend Development', 8),
            'frontend': ('Frontend Development', 8),
        }
        
        # Find matching skills
        for keyword, (skill_name, level) in skill_map.items():
            if keyword in title_lower:
                skill = SkillsFactory.create(
                    name=skill_name,
                    level=level,
                    description=f"Auto-generated skill for {self.title}"
                )
                relevant_skills.append(skill)
        
        # Add default skill if none matched
        if not relevant_skills:
            skill = SkillsFactory.create(
                name="Generic Skill",
                level=5,
                description="Default skill for job"
            )
            relevant_skills.append(skill)
        
        # Add all skills
        for skill in relevant_skills:
            self.skills.add(skill)

print("=== Example 5: Intelligent Skill Generation ===\n")

# Python job - should create Python skill
job1 = SmartJobPostFactory.create(title="Python Developer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {job1.skills.count()}")
for skill in job1.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Senior full stack - multiple keyword matches
job2 = SmartJobPostFactory.create(title="Senior Full Stack Engineer")
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {job2.skills.count()}")
for skill in job2.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Generic job - should get default skill
job3 = SmartJobPostFactory.create(title="Office Manager")
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {job3.skills.count()}")
for skill in job3.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

# Override with explicit skills
custom_skill = SkillsFactory.create(name="Custom Skill", level=10)
job4 = SmartJobPostFactory.create(
    title="Data Scientist",
    intelligent_skills=[custom_skill]
)
print(f"\nJob 4: {job4.title}")
print(f"  Skills: {job4.skills.count()}")
for skill in job4.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name} (Level {skill.level})")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total jobs: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Jobs with auto-generated skills: 3")
print(f"  Jobs with explicit skills: 1")
all_jobs = JobPost.objects.all()
for job in all_jobs[12:]:  # Show latest 4 jobs
    print(f"  {job.title}: {job.skills.count()} skill(s)")
Print Output: === Example 5: Intelligent Skill Generation === Job 1: Python Developer Skills: 2 - Python (Level 9) - General Programming (Level 7) Job 2: Senior Full Stack Engineer Skills: 4 - Leadership (Level 8) - Full Stack (Level 9) - Engineering Principles (Level 7) - General Programming (Level 7) Job 3: Office Manager Skills: 1 - Generic Skill (Level 5) Job 4: Data Scientist Skills: 1 - Custom Skill (Level 10) 📊 Summary: Total jobs: 16 Total skills: 19 Jobs with auto-generated skills: 3 Jobs with explicit skills: 1 Python Developer: 2 skill(s) Senior Full Stack Engineer: 4 skill(s) Office Manager: 1 skill(s) Data Scientist: 1 skill(s)
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 5 (cumulative - FINAL) Users: 15 total (11 from previous + 4 new for job authors) Authors: 10 total (6 from previous + 4 new) Locations: 14 total (10 from previous + 4 new) JobPosts: 16 total (12 from previous + 4 new) - JobPost 13: "Python Developer" with 2 skills (Python, General Programming) - JobPost 14: "Senior Full Stack Engineer" with 4 skills (Leadership, Full Stack, Engineering Principles, General Programming) - JobPost 15: "Office Manager" with 1 skill (Generic Skill) - JobPost 16: "Data Scientist" with 1 explicit skill (Custom Skill) Skills: 19 total (10 from previous + 9 new) - 8 auto-generated skills from keyword matching - 1 custom skill for Job 4 Post-Generation Intelligence: - Parses job title for keywords - Creates relevant skills automatically - Skill levels vary by skill type (Leadership=8, Python=9, etc.) - Falls back to generic skill if no keywords match - Respects explicit skills if provided Keyword Mapping Results: - "python" → Python (L9) + developer → General Programming (L7) - "senior" → Leadership (L8) + "full stack" → Full Stack (L9) + "engineer" → Engineering Principles (L7) + developer → General Programming (L7) - No keywords → Generic Skill (L5) - Extracted skills override auto-generation TOTAL OBJECTS ACROSS ALL EXAMPLES: - 15 Users - 10 Authors - 14 Locations - 16 JobPosts - 19 Skills Post-Generation Patterns Demonstrated: 1. ManyToMany relationship management (skills) 2. Method calls (set_password) 3. Reverse relationship queries (job counts) 4. Denormalized data updates (location cache) 5. Intelligent data generation (keyword-based skills)

PostGenerationMethodCall - Call Model Methods After Creation

Automatically call a method on the generated object with specified arguments.

📖 Full Description

The PostGenerationMethodCall declaration provides a clean, declarative way to automatically invoke a specific method on your model instance immediately after it's been created by the factory. This feature eliminates the need for explicit post-generation decorators when all you need to do is call a single method with predefined arguments. It's particularly valuable for Django's User model and its set_password method, which must be called to properly hash passwords rather than storing them in plain text. The pattern is also useful for any model methods that perform initialization, validation, state setup, or side effects that depend on the instance existing with a primary key. PostGenerationMethodCall accepts the method name as a string, optional positional arguments (limited to at most one for API simplicity), and optional keyword arguments - all of which get passed to the method when it's invoked. The declaration integrates seamlessly with factory parameters, allowing you to override the default method arguments at factory invocation time. For example, you can define a default password but override it when creating specific test users. Unlike the @factory.post_generation decorator, PostGenerationMethodCall doesn't give you access to the create/extracted/kwargs pattern - it simply calls the method. This makes it perfect for straightforward method calls but means you should use the decorator approach for more complex post-creation logic. The method call happens after all regular fields are set and after the instance is saved (for CREATE strategy), ensuring the instance is in a consistent state before the method executes.

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from django.contrib.auth.hashers import make_password

# Example: User model needs set_password called
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Call set_password method automatically with default password
    password = factory.PostGenerationMethodCall('set_password', 'default_password')

# Usage:
user1 = UserFactory.create()
# Automatically calls: user1.set_password('default_password')
# Password is properly hashed

user2 = UserFactory.create(password='custom_pass123')
# Calls: user2.set_password('custom_pass123')
# Overrides the default password

# Custom model with initialization method
class JobPost(models.Model):
    title = models.CharField(max_length=200)
    status = models.CharField(max_length=50, default='draft')
    
    def activate(self, notify=False):
        \"\"\"Activate the job post.\"\"\"
        self.status = 'active'
        if notify:
            # Send notification logic
            pass
        self.save()

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    
    # Call activate method with default notify=False
    activate = factory.PostGenerationMethodCall('activate', notify=False)

# Usage:
job1 = JobPostFactory.create()  # Calls job1.activate(notify=False)
job2 = JobPostFactory.create(activate__notify=True)  # Calls job2.activate(notify=True)

📋 Parameters Description

method_name (str, required) - The string name of the method to call on the generated instance. This must be a valid method name that exists on your model. The method is looked up at runtime using getattr, so typos will cause AttributeError. Examples: 'set_password', 'activate', 'initialize', 'validate'.

*args (positional argument, optional) - A single positional argument to pass to the method. PostGenerationMethodCall limits you to at most one positional argument for API simplicity. If you need multiple positional arguments, use the @factory.post_generation decorator instead. Example: factory.PostGenerationMethodCall('set_password', 'defaultpass') passes 'defaultpass' as the first argument.

**kwargs (keyword arguments, optional) - Any keyword arguments to pass to the method. These are forwarded directly to the method call. You can override these at factory invocation time using double-underscore syntax. Examples: factory.PostGenerationMethodCall('activate', notify=True, priority='high').

🏷️ Attributes

Attribute name - The name you assign to the PostGenerationMethodCall in your factory becomes the parameter name you can use to override the method call at invocation time. For example, if you define password = factory.PostGenerationMethodCall(...), you can override it with UserFactory(password='newpass'). If you define activate = factory.PostGenerationMethodCall(...), you can skip it with JobFactory(activate=None).

⚙️ Methods

Method execution - The specified method is called immediately after instance creation (and after save for CREATE strategy). The method receives the positional argument (if provided) and any keyword arguments. The method's return value is ignored by the factory system. If the method modifies the instance and you want those changes persisted, the method must call self.save() itself.

Override syntax - Override the default method call at factory invocation using: MyFactory(method_name='new_arg') to change the positional argument, MyFactory(method_name__kwarg='value') to change keyword arguments, or MyFactory(method_name=None) to skip the method call entirely.

↩️ Return Value

PostGenerationMethodCall doesn't return anything directly - it's a declaration, not a function call. The factory returns the created model instance as usual. Any return value from the called method is ignored.

💡 Examples

Example 1: User Password Hashing with PostGenerationMethodCall

Use PostGenerationMethodCall to automatically call set_password for proper password hashing.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'testuser{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # PostGenerationMethodCall for password hashing
    password = factory.PostGenerationMethodCall('set_password', 'default123')

print("=== Example 1: User Password Hashing ===\n")

# User with default password
user1 = UserFactory.create(username='alice')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Password hashed: {user1.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can auth with 'default123': {user1.check_password('default123')}")

# User with custom password
user2 = UserFactory.create(username='bob', password='secure_pass456')
print(f"\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  Password hashed: {user2.password.startswith('pbkdf2_')}")
print(f"  Can auth with 'secure_pass456': {user2.check_password('secure_pass456')}")
print(f"  Cannot auth with 'default123': {user2.check_password('default123')}")

# Batch creation with varied passwords
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
passwords = ['pass1', 'pass2', 'pass3']
users = []
for i, pwd in enumerate(passwords):
    user = UserFactory.create(username=f'batch{i}', password=pwd)
    users.append(user)
    print(f"  {user.username}: Auth with '{pwd}': {user.check_password(pwd)}")

# Skip password setting (not recommended, but possible)
user_nopass = UserFactory.create(username='nopass', password=None)
print(f"\n{user_nopass.username}: Has usable password: {user_nopass.has_usable_password()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Users with hashed passwords: {sum(1 for u in User.objects.all() if u.password.startswith('pbkdf2_'))}")
print(f"  Users with usable passwords: {sum(1 for u in User.objects.all() if u.has_usable_password())}")
Print Output: === Example 1: User Password Hashing === User 1: alice Password hashed: True Can auth with 'default123': True User 2: bob Password hashed: True Can auth with 'secure_pass456': True Cannot auth with 'default123': False --- Batch Creation --- batch0: Auth with 'pass1': True batch1: Auth with 'pass2': True batch2: Auth with 'pass3': True nopass: Has usable password: False 📊 Summary: Total users: 6 Users with hashed passwords: 5 Users with usable passwords: 5
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 6 total (NEW) - alice (password hashed via set_password('default123')) - bob (password hashed via set_password('secure_pass456')) - batch0 (password hashed via set_password('pass1')) - batch1 (password hashed via set_password('pass2')) - batch2 (password hashed via set_password('pass3')) - nopass (no password set, unusable password) PostGenerationMethodCall Effects: - Automatically calls set_password() after user creation - Default argument: 'default123' - Override via: UserFactory(password='custom') - Skip via: UserFactory(password=None) Password Security: - All passwords properly hashed with pbkdf2_sha256 - check_password() validates correctly - Default password can be overridden per instance Authors: 0 total Locations: 0 total JobPosts: 0 total Skills: 0 total
Example 2: Author Bio Generation Method

Call a custom model method to generate bio content after author creation.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Keep existing data
# Author.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'author_user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

# Extend Author model hypothetically with a generate_bio method
# For demo, we'll simulate by updating bio in post-generation
class AuthorFactoryWithMethod(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = "Temporary bio"
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def generate_bio(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        \"\"\"Simulate a model method that generates bio.\"\"\"
        if not create:
            return
        
        # Simulate calling self.generate_bio(style='professional')
        style = kwargs.get('style', 'professional')
        
        if style == 'professional':
            self.bio = f"{self.user.username} - Professional technical writer with years of experience."
        elif style == 'casual':
            self.bio = f"Hey! I'm {self.user.username}, love writing about tech!"
        elif style == 'formal':
            self.bio = f"{self.user.username.upper()} - Senior Technical Documentation Specialist."
        else:
            self.bio = f"{self.user.username} - Content creator."
        
        self.save()

print("=== Example 2: Author Bio Generation ===\n")

# Author with default professional style
author1 = AuthorFactoryWithMethod.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio}")

# Author with casual style
author2 = AuthorFactoryWithMethod.create(generate_bio__style='casual')
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author2.bio}")

# Author with formal style
author3 = AuthorFactoryWithMethod.create(generate_bio__style='formal')
print(f"\nAuthor 3: {author3.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author3.bio}")

# Batch with mixed styles
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
styles = ['professional', 'casual', 'formal', 'custom']
for style in styles:
    author = AuthorFactoryWithMethod.create(generate_bio__style=style)
    print(f"  {author.user.username}: {author.bio[:50]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Professional bios: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='Professional').count()}")
print(f"  Casual bios: {Author.objects.filter(bio__startswith='Hey').count()}")
print(f"  Formal bios: {Author.objects.filter(bio__contains='Senior').count()}")

Example 3: Location Geocoding Simulation

Simulate calling a geocode method to set latitude/longitude after location creation.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Location

# Clean locations
Location.objects.all().delete()

class LocationWithGeocodeFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def geocode(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        \"\"\"Simulate geocoding - set postal_code to encode lat/long.\"\"\"
        if not create:
            return
        
        # Simulate geocoding based on city (fake coordinates)
        # In real code: lat, long = geocoding_service.geocode(self.address)
        city_coords = {
            'San Francisco': (37.77, -122.41),
            'New York': (40.71, -74.00),
            'Chicago': (41.88, -87.62),
            'Austin': (30.27, -97.74),
        }
        
        coords = city_coords.get(self.city, (0.0, 0.0))
        
        # Store in postal_code for demo (normally you'd have lat/long fields)
        self.postal_code = f"LAT:{coords[0]:.2f}_LON:{coords[1]:.2f}"
        self.save()

print("=== Example 3: Location Geocoding Simulation ===\n")

# Location that will be geocoded
loc1 = LocationWithGeocodeFactory.create(city="San Francisco", state="CA")
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Postal code (coordinates): {loc1.postal_code}")

loc2 = LocationWithGeocodeFactory.create(city="New York", state="NY")
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Postal code (coordinates): {loc2.postal_code}")

loc3 = LocationWithGeocodeFactory.create(city="Chicago", state="IL")
print(f"\nLocation 3: {loc3.city}, {loc3.state}")
print(f"  Postal code (coordinates): {loc3.postal_code}")

# Unknown city gets default coordinates
loc4 = LocationWithGeocodeFactory.create(city="Unknown City", state="XX")
print(f"\nLocation 4: {loc4.city}, {loc4.state}")
print(f"  Postal code (coordinates): {loc4.postal_code}")

# Batch creation
print(f"\n--- Batch Geocoding ---")
cities = ['San Francisco', 'Austin', 'Chicago']
for city in cities:
    loc = LocationWithGeocodeFactory.create(city=city)
    print(f"  {loc.city:20} | {loc.postal_code}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Geocoded locations: {Location.objects.filter(postal_code__startswith='LAT:').count()}")
all_locs = Location.objects.all()
for loc in all_locs:
    print(f"  {loc.city:20} | {loc.postal_code}")

Example 4: Skills Validation and Normalization

Call a validation method after skill creation to normalize data.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean skills
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class SkillWithValidationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = "Temporary description"
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def normalize_skill(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        \"\"\"Normalize skill name and generate proper description.\"\"\"
        if not create:
            return
        
        # Normalize name: title case
        self.name = self.name.title()
        
        # Generate description based on level
        if self.level >= 9:
            proficiency = "Expert"
        elif self.level >= 7:
            proficiency = "Advanced"
        elif self.level >= 5:
            proficiency = "Intermediate"
        elif self.level >= 3:
            proficiency = "Beginner"
        else:
            proficiency = "Novice"
        
        self.description = f"{self.name} - {proficiency} level ({self.level}/10)"
        self.save()

print("=== Example 4: Skills Validation ===\n")

# Skill with low level
skill1 = SkillWithValidationFactory.create(name="python", level=2)
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")

# Skill with medium level
skill2 = SkillWithValidationFactory.create(name="javascript", level=6)
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill2.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill2.description}")

# Skill with expert level
skill3 = SkillWithValidationFactory.create(name="docker", level=10)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill3.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill3.description}")

# Batch with various levels
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
skills_data = [
    ("kubernetes", 8),
    ("react", 5),
    ("aws", 9),
    ("html", 3),
]
for name, level in skills_data:
    skill = SkillWithValidationFactory.create(name=name, level=level)
    print(f"  {skill.name:15} Level {skill.level:2}/10 | {skill.description}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Expert skills (9-10): {Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=9).count()}")
print(f"  Advanced skills (7-8): {Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=7, level__lt=9).count()}")
print(f"  Intermediate skills (5-6): {Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=5, level__lt=7).count()}")
print(f"  Beginner skills (3-4): {Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=3, level__lt=5).count()}")
print(f"  Novice skills (1-2): {Skills.objects.filter(level__lt=3).count()}")

Example 5: JobPost with Status Activation

Simulate calling an activate() method on job posts after creation.

import factory
from jobpost.models import JobPost, Author, Location
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

# Keep existing data
# JobPost.objects.all().delete()

class SimpleUserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'jobuser{n}')

class SimpleAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(SimpleUserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=50)

class SimpleLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostWithActivationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(SimpleAuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(SimpleLocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    description = "Draft job post"
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def activate_job(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        \"\"\"Simulate activating job post.\"\"\"
        if not create:
            return
        
        # Simulate calling self.activate(priority='normal')
        priority = kwargs.get('priority', 'normal')
        
        # Update description to reflect activation
        status = f"[ACTIVE-{priority.upper()}]"
        self.description = f"{status} {self.title} - Now accepting applications!"
        self.save()

print("=== Example 5: JobPost Activation ===\n")

# Job with normal priority
job1 = JobPostWithActivationFactory.create(title="Software Engineer")
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Description: {job1.description}")

# Job with high priority
job2 = JobPostWithActivationFactory.create(
    title="Senior Developer",
    activate_job__priority='high'
)
print(f"\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Description: {job2.description}")

# Job with urgent priority
job3 = JobPostWithActivationFactory.create(
    title="DevOps Lead",
    activate_job__priority='urgent'
)
print(f"\nJob 3: {job3.title}")
print(f"  Description: {job3.description}")

# Batch with mixed priorities
print(f"\n--- Batch Activation ---")
priorities = ['low', 'normal', 'high', 'urgent']
for priority in priorities:
    job = JobPostWithActivationFactory.create(
        title=f"{priority.capitalize()} Position",
        activate_job__priority=priority
    )
    print(f"  {priority:10} | {job.description[:50]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Active jobs: {JobPost.objects.filter(description__contains='ACTIVE').count()}")
print(f"  High priority: {JobPost.objects.filter(description__contains='HIGH').count()}")
print(f"  Urgent priority: {JobPost.objects.filter(description__contains='URGENT').count()}")

⚠️ Note: PostGenerationMethodCall allows at most one positional argument for API simplicity. For complex method calls requiring multiple positional arguments, use the @factory.post_generation decorator instead. Use keyword arguments (via double-underscore syntax) for fine-grained control over method behavior.

RelatedFactory - Reverse Relationship Creation

Automatically create related objects that point back to the current instance.

📖 Full Description

RelatedFactory is a specialized FactoryBoy declaration that inverts the normal parent-child relationship pattern, enabling you to automatically create child objects that reference a parent when the parent is created. While SubFactory creates a parent object for a child (following the ForeignKey dependency chain upward), RelatedFactory works in reverse - it creates child objects that point back to the just-created parent via their ForeignKey field. This is particularly valuable when you want to ensure test data includes realistic one-to-many relationships without manually creating child objects after the parent. For example, when testing an Author model, you might want every author to automatically have at least one associated JobPost, which you can achieve by declaring a RelatedFactory in AuthorFactory. The RelatedFactory declaration specifies which factory to use for creating the related objects and which field in the related model points back to the parent (the factory_related_name parameter). When you create a parent instance, FactoryBoy automatically creates the related child object(s) and sets the child's ForeignKey to reference the parent. This happens after the parent is fully created and saved, ensuring the parent has a primary key that can be referenced. RelatedFactory also supports passing additional keyword arguments that get forwarded to the child factory, allowing you to customize the created children. The key benefit is that your test setup becomes more declarative and realistic - creating an author automatically gives you an author with job posts, without requiring explicit post-creation steps in your test code.

Syntax

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, User, Location

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

# Child factory (must be defined first or use forward reference)
class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    # Note: author will be set by RelatedFactory
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    description = factory.Faker('text')

# Parent factory with RelatedFactory
class AuthorWithJobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)
    
    # Automatically create one job post for this author
    job_post = factory.RelatedFactory(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',  # Field in JobPost that references Author
        title='Default Job Post',  # Default values passed to JobPostFactory
        salary_min=90000
    )

# Usage:
author = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create()
# Author is created first
# Then JobPost is created with author=
print(author.jobpost_set.count())  # Returns 1

# Override related factory parameters
author2 = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create(
    job_post__title='Custom Job Title',
    job_post__salary_min=100000
)
print(author2.jobpost_set.first().title)  # 'Custom Job Title'

📋 Parameters Description

factory (factory class, required) - The factory class to use for creating the related child objects. This should be a complete factory class (typically a DjangoModelFactory subclass) that knows how to create instances of the child model. The factory can be specified as a class directly or as a string reference if there are circular dependency issues.

factory_related_name (str, required) - The name of the ForeignKey field in the child model that points back to the parent model. This tells RelatedFactory which field to set when creating the child. For example, if JobPost has author = ForeignKey(Author), then factory_related_name='author'. This is one of the most critical parameters - getting it wrong will cause relationship setup to fail.

**kwargs (optional) - Default values to pass to the child factory. These become the default field values for created child objects. You can override these at parent factory invocation time using double-underscore syntax: ParentFactory(related_field__child_field='value').

🏷️ Attributes

Attribute name - The name you assign to the RelatedFactory declaration in your parent factory becomes the prefix for overriding child factory parameters. For example, if you define job_post = factory.RelatedFactory(...), you can customize child creation with ParentFactory(job_post__title='Custom').

⚙️ Methods

Automatic creation - RelatedFactory automatically creates one child object for each parent created (to create multiple children, use RelatedFactoryList instead). The child is created after the parent is saved, ensuring the parent has a primary key. The child factory is invoked with the parent instance passed to the specified ForeignKey field.

Parameter override - Override child factory parameters using double-underscore syntax: ParentFactory(related_attr__child_field='value'). This passes child_field='value' to the child factory.

↩️ Return Value

RelatedFactory doesn't change the return value of the parent factory - you still get the parent instance. The child objects are created as a side effect and can be accessed via Django's reverse relationship (e.g., parent.child_set.all() or using related_name).

💡 Examples

Example 1: Author with Automatic JobPost Creation

Create authors that automatically have one associated job post.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, Location

# Clean database
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
User.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 75000
    salary_max = 110000
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

class AuthorWithJobFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)
    
    # RelatedFactory creates one job post per author
    job_post = factory.RelatedFactory(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author'
    )

print("=== Example 1: Author with Automatic JobPost ===\n")

# Create author - automatically creates job post
author1 = AuthorWithJobFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio[:50]}...")
print(f"  Job posts: {author1.jobpost_set.count()}")
job1 = author1.jobpost_set.first()
print(f"  Job title: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Job author matches: {job1.author == author1}")

# Create another author
author2 = AuthorWithJobFactory.create()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts: {author2.jobpost_set.count()}")
job2 = author2.jobpost_set.first()
print(f"  Job title: {job2.title}")

# Batch create authors
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
authors = AuthorWithJobFactory.create_batch(3)
for i, author in enumerate(authors, 1):
    job_count = author.jobpost_set.count()
    job_title = author.jobpost_set.first().title if job_count > 0 else 'N/A'
    print(f"  Author {i}: {author.user.username} | Jobs: {job_count} | Title: {job_title}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Authors with jobs: {Author.objects.filter(jobpost__isnull=False).distinct().count()}")
print(f"  Average jobs per author: {JobPost.objects.count() / Author.objects.count():.1f}")

Job posts: 1 Job title: Software Developer Job author matches: True Author 2: user1 Job posts: 1 Job title: Data Analyst --- Batch Creation --- Author 1: user2 | Jobs: 1 | Title: Network Engineer Author 2: user3 | Jobs: 1 | Title: Project Manager Author 3: user4 | Jobs: 1 | Title: UX Designer 📊 Summary: Total authors: 5 Total job posts: 5 Authors with jobs: 5 Average jobs per author: 1.0

Example 2: Customizing Related Objects

Override related factory parameters to customize automatically created job posts.

import factory

print("=== Example 2: Customizing Related Objects ===\n")

Example 3: Location with Multiple JobPosts (via RelatedFactory)

Create locations that automatically have associated job posts.

import factory

# Define factory with RelatedFactory for location → jobpost
class JobPostForLocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorWithJobFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 115000
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)

class LocationWithJobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'
    
    # Automatically create one job post at this location
    job_post = factory.RelatedFactory(
        JobPostForLocationFactory,
        factory_related_name='location'
    )

print("=== Example 3: Location with JobPosts ===\n")

# Create location with automatic job
loc1 = LocationWithJobsFactory.create(city="San Francisco", state="CA")
print(f"Location 1: {loc1.city}, {loc1.state}")
print(f"  Job posts at this location: {loc1.jobpost_set.count()}")
job = loc1.jobpost_set.first()
print(f"  Job title: {job.title}")
print(f"  Job location matches: {job.location == loc1}")

# Create location with custom job
loc2 = LocationWithJobsFactory.create(
    city="Austin",
    state="TX",
    job_post__title='Texas-Based Developer',
    job_post__salary_min=95000
)
print(f"\nLocation 2: {loc2.city}, {loc2.state}")
print(f"  Job posts: {loc2.jobpost_set.count()}")
job2 = loc2.jobpost_set.first()
print(f"  Job title: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Salary: ${job2.salary_min:,}")

# Batch create locations
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
cities = ['Seattle', 'Portland', 'Denver']
for city in cities:
    loc = LocationWithJobsFactory.create(city=city)
    job_count = loc.jobpost_set.count()
    print(f"  {city:15} | Jobs: {job_count}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Locations with jobs: {Location.objects.filter(jobpost__isnull=False).distinct().count()}")

# Show locations with their job counts
all_locs = Location.objects.all()[9:]  # Show only new locations from this example
for loc in all_locs:
    job_count = loc.jobpost_set.count()
    print(f"  {loc.city:20} | {job_count} job(s)")
Print Output: === Example 3: Location with JobPosts === Location 1: San Francisco, CA Job posts at this location: 1 Job title: Marketing Specialist Job location matches: True Location 2: Austin, TX Job posts: 1 Job title: Texas-Based Developer Salary: $95,000 --- Batch Creation --- Seattle | Jobs: 1 Portland | Jobs: 1 Denver | Jobs: 1 📊 Summary: Total locations: 14 Total job posts: 14 Locations with jobs: 14 San Francisco | 1 job(s) Austin | 1 job(s) Seattle | 1 job(s) Portland | 1 job(s) Denver | 1 job(s)

Example 4: Skills with Related JobPosts

Demonstrate that RelatedFactory works with ManyToMany relationships (though not recommended - use post_generation instead).

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean skills
Skills.objects.all().delete()

# Note: For ManyToMany, post_generation is typically better
# This example shows RelatedFactory capability

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} skill at level {obj.level}")

print("=== Example 4: Skills with Metadata ===\n")

# Create skills independently
skill1 = SkillsFactory.create(name="Python", level=9)
print(f"Skill 1: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")

skill2 = SkillsFactory.create(name="Django", level=8)
print(f"\nSkill 2: {skill2.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill2.level}/10")

skill3 = SkillsFactory.create(name="PostgreSQL", level=7)
print(f"\nSkill 3: {skill3.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill3.level}/10")

# Manually associate with job posts (RelatedFactory doesn't work well for M2M)
job = JobPost.objects.first()
if job:
    job.skills.add(skill1, skill2, skill3)
    print(f"\n  Added skills to job: {job.title}")
    print(f"  Job skills count: {job.skills.count()}")

# Batch create
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(
    5,
    name=factory.Iterator(['JavaScript', 'React', 'Node.js', 'TypeScript', 'Git'])
)
for skill in skills:
    print(f"  {skill.name:15} | Level {skill.level}/10")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  High-level skills (8+): {Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=8).count()}")
print(f"  Average skill level: {sum(s.level for s in Skills.objects.all()) / Skills.objects.count():.1f}")
Print Output: === Example 4: Skills with Metadata === Skill 1: Python Level: 9/10 Description: Python skill at level 9 Skill 2: Django Level: 8/10 Skill 3: PostgreSQL Level: 7/10 Added skills to job: Software Developer Job skills count: 3 --- Batch Creation --- JavaScript | Level 6/10 React | Level 5/10 Node.js | Level 7/10 TypeScript | Level 9/10 Git | Level 4/10 📊 Summary: Total skills: 8 High-level skills (8+): 3 Average skill level: 6.9
