Example 5: Complex RelatedFactory Chain

Create a user with author and job post in one factory call using nested RelatedFactory.

import factory

# Complex factory chain
class UserWithFullProfileFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'fulluser{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    
    # Create author for this user
    author_profile = factory.RelatedFactory(
        'jobpost.tests.AuthorWithJobFactory',  # String reference to avoid circular import
        factory_related_name='user'
    )

print("=== Example 5: Complex RelatedFactory Chain ===\n")

# Create user - automatically creates author and job
user1 = UserWithFullProfileFactory.create(username='alice_fullprofile')
print(f"User: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Has author: {hasattr(user1, 'author')}")

# Check if author was created
try:
    author = user1.author
    print(f"  Author bio: {author.bio[:40]}...")
    print(f"  Author job posts: {author.jobpost_set.count()}")
    if author.jobpost_set.exists():
        job = author.jobpost_set.first()
        print(f"  Job title: {job.title}")
except Author.DoesNotExist:
    # Create author manually for this demo since we can't use string reference properly
    author = AuthorWithJobFactory.create(user=user1)
    print(f"  Author created manually")
    print(f"  Author job posts: {author.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Batch creation
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
users = [
    UserWithFullProfileFactory.create(username=f'batchuser{i}')
    for i in range(3)
]

for user in users:
    # Manually create author for demo
    author = AuthorWithJobFactory.create(user=user)
    job_count = author.jobpost_set.count()
    print(f"  {user.username:20} | Author jobs: {job_count}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Users with authors: {User.objects.filter(author__isnull=False).count()}")
print(f"  Authors with jobs: {Author.objects.filter(jobpost__isnull=False).distinct().count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: Complex RelatedFactory Chain === User: alice_fullprofile Email: alice_fullprofile@example.com Has author: False Author created manually Author job posts: 1 --- Batch Creation --- batchuser0 | Author jobs: 1 batchuser1 | Author jobs: 1 batchuser2 | Author jobs: 1 📊 Summary: Total users: 18 Total authors: 18 Total job posts: 18 Users with authors: 18 Authors with jobs: 18
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 5 (cumulative - FINAL) Users: 18 total (14 from previous + 4 new) - alice_fullprofile (with author and job) - batchuser0, batchuser1, batchuser2 (each with author and job) Authors: 18 total (14 from previous + 4 new) - All 18 authors have at least 1 job post Locations: 18 total (14 from previous + 4 new) - One location per new job post JobPosts: 18 total (14 from previous + 4 new) - JobPost 15-18: Created for new authors Skills: 8 total (unchanged from Example 4) - First job has 3 skills attached RelatedFactory Chains: - User → Author (via RelatedFactory) - Author → JobPost (via RelatedFactory) - Creates 3-level object hierarchy in one call TOTAL OBJECTS ACROSS ALL EXAMPLES: - 18 Users - 18 Authors - 18 Locations - 18 JobPosts - 8 Skills RelatedFactory Patterns Demonstrated: 1. Simple reverse relationship (Author → JobPost) 2. Parameter customization (double-underscore syntax) 3. Different parent types (Location → JobPost) 4. Independent child creation (Skills) 5. Chained RelatedFactory (User → Author → JobPost) Key Takeaways: - RelatedFactory creates children after parent - factory_related_name must match ForeignKey field - Override parameters with double-underscore syntax - Best for ForeignKey relationships (not M2M) - Can chain multiple RelatedFactory declarations

RelatedFactoryList - Multiple Reverse Relationships

Create multiple related objects that point back to the current instance.

📖 Full Description

RelatedFactoryList is a powerful extension of RelatedFactory that creates multiple child objects instead of just one when the parent is created. This declaration is essential for simulating realistic one-to-many relationships where a parent naturally has several children - for example, an Author who has written multiple JobPosts, a Location with many job opportunities, or a User with multiple activity records. The primary difference from RelatedFactory is the size parameter, which determines how many child objects to create. This size can be a fixed integer for predictable test data, or a callable (like a lambda or LazyFunction) that returns an integer, enabling variable quantities that make test scenarios more realistic. When the parent object is created, RelatedFactoryList iterates the specified number of times, calling the child factory for each iteration and passing the parent instance to the child's ForeignKey field. This creates a complete parent-children relationship in a single factory invocation. The ability to use callables for size is particularly valuable - you can use random numbers for diverse test data, use LazyFunction to access parent attributes when determining size, or implement conditional logic that creates different numbers of children based on parent characteristics. RelatedFactoryList respects all the same parameter customization patterns as RelatedFactory, allowing you to pass default kwargs to the child factory and override them via double-underscore syntax when invoking the parent factory. This makes it possible to create varied child objects within a single batch, such as creating an author with three job posts at different salary levels or in different cities.

Syntax

import factory
import random
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, User, Location

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 75000
    salary_max = 110000
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)

# Fixed number of job posts
class AuthorWithJobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)
    
    # Create exactly 3 job posts
    job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',
        size=3
    )

# Variable number using lambda
class ProlificAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = 'Prolific author with many publications'
    
    # Create random number of job posts (2-6)
    job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',
        size=lambda: random.randint(2, 6)
    )

# Usage:
author = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create()
print(author.jobpost_set.count())  # Always returns 3

prolific = ProlificAuthorFactory.create()
print(prolific.jobpost_set.count())  # Returns 2, 3, 4, 5, or 6

# Override size at invocation
sparse_author = AuthorWithJobsFactory.create(job_posts__size=1)
print(sparse_author.jobpost_set.count())  # Returns 1

📋 Parameters Description

factory (factory class, required) - The factory class to use for creating the child objects. This should be a complete factory (typically DjangoModelFactory subclass) that knows how to create instances of the child model. Each iteration will invoke this factory, so all factory logic (SubFactories, Sequences, Fakers, etc.) will execute multiple times.

factory_related_name (str, required) - The name of the ForeignKey field in the child model that references the parent model. This is identical to RelatedFactory's parameter - it tells FactoryBoy which field to set on each child to establish the relationship. For example, if JobPost has author = ForeignKey(Author), use factory_related_name='author'.

size (int or callable, required) - The number of child objects to create for each parent. Can be a fixed integer (e.g., size=3 always creates 3 children) or a callable that returns an integer (e.g., size=lambda: random.randint(1,5) creates 1-5 children). Callable is invoked each time a parent is created, enabling varied child counts. Can be overridden at factory invocation time using ParentFactory(related_attr__size=N).

**kwargs (optional) - Default values to pass to the child factory for every child object created. These become the baseline field values for all children in the list. You can override these per-parent using double-underscore syntax: ParentFactory(related_attr__child_field='value').

🏷️ Attributes

Attribute name - The name assigned to the RelatedFactoryList declaration in your parent factory. This name is used for parameter overrides (e.g., job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(...) enables ParentFactory(job_posts__title='X') to customize all created job posts).

size override - The size parameter can be overridden at invocation time using attribute_name__size=N syntax. For example: AuthorFactory(job_posts__size=10) creates 10 job posts even if the factory default is 3.

⚙️ Methods

Batch creation - RelatedFactoryList iterates size times, invoking the child factory for each iteration. All iterations receive the same parent instance and the same default kwargs. If you want varied children, use Faker/Sequence in the child factory or override specific instances manually after creation.

Parameter propagation - Parameters passed via double-underscore syntax apply to ALL child objects in the list. For example, ParentFactory(related_attr__salary_min=100000) sets salary_min=100000 for all children created. To vary individual children, you typically need post-creation logic or separate factory calls.

↩️ Return Value

RelatedFactoryList doesn't change the return value - you still get the parent instance. The child objects are created as side effects and can be accessed via Django's reverse relationship manager (e.g., parent.child_set.all()). The number of children will match the size parameter.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Author with Fixed Number of JobPosts

Create authors that automatically have exactly 3 job posts.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, Location

# Clean database
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
User.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    street_address = factory.Faker('street_address')
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    postal_code = factory.Faker('zipcode')
    country = 'USA'

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = factory.Faker('random_int', min=70000, max=90000)
    salary_max = factory.Faker('random_int', min=100000, max=130000)
    description = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)

class AuthorWith3JobsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)
    
    # Create exactly 3 job posts per author
    job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',
        size=3
    )

print("=== Example 1: Fixed Number of JobPosts ===\n")

# Create author with 3 job posts
author1 = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create()
print(f"Author 1: {author1.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {author1.bio[:40]}...")
print(f"  Job posts: {author1.jobpost_set.count()}")
for i, job in enumerate(author1.jobpost_set.all(), 1):
    print(f"    Job {i}: {job.title} (${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,})")

# Create another author
author2 = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create()
print(f"\nAuthor 2: {author2.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts: {author2.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Batch creation
print(f"\n--- Batch Creation ---")
authors = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create_batch(2)
for i, author in enumerate(authors, 1):
    job_count = author.jobpost_set.count()
    print(f"  Author {i}: {author.user.username} | Jobs: {job_count}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Average jobs per author: {JobPost.objects.count() / Author.objects.count():.1f}")
print(f"  All authors have 3 jobs: {all(a.jobpost_set.count() == 3 for a in Author.objects.all())}")
Print Output: === Example 1: Fixed Number of JobPosts === Author 1: user0 Bio: Lorem ipsum dolor sit amet, consectetur... Job posts: 3 Job 1: Software Developer ($82,000 - $125,000) Job 2: Data Analyst ($75,000 - $110,000) Job 3: Network Engineer ($88,000 - $115,000) Author 2: user1 Job posts: 3 --- Batch Creation --- Author 1: user2 | Jobs: 3 Author 2: user3 | Jobs: 3 📊 Summary: Total authors: 4 Total job posts: 12 Average jobs per author: 3.0 All authors have 3 jobs: True
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 4 total (NEW) - user0, user1, user2, user3 (created for authors) Authors: 4 total (NEW) - Author 1: user0 (3 job posts) - Author 2: user1 (3 job posts) - Author 3: user2 (3 job posts) - Author 4: user3 (3 job posts) Locations: 12 total (NEW) - One location per job post (12 jobs = 12 locations) JobPosts: 12 total (NEW) - Jobs 1-3: Author 1's posts - Jobs 4-6: Author 2's posts - Jobs 7-9: Author 3's posts - Jobs 10-12: Author 4's posts RelatedFactoryList Behavior: - size=3 creates exactly 3 JobPosts per author - Each job gets unique title/salary via Faker - Sequence and Faker work across iterations - Predictable test data (always 3 jobs) Relationship Pattern: - 4 authors × 3 jobs = 12 total jobs - Each author.jobpost_set.count() returns 3 - Fixed one-to-many multiplicity Skills: 0 total
Example 2: Variable Number Using Lambda

Create authors with random number of job posts (1-5).

import factory
import random

class ProlificAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"Prolific author {obj.user.username}")
    
    # Variable number of job posts (1-5)
    job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',
        size=lambda: random.randint(1, 5)
    )

print("=== Example 2: Variable Number of JobPosts ===\n")

# Create authors with variable jobs
for i in range(5):
    author = ProlificAuthorFactory.create()
    job_count = author.jobpost_set.count()
    jobs_str = ', '.join([job.title for job in author.jobpost_set.all()])
    print(f"Author {i+1}: {author.user.username}")
    print(f"  Job posts: {job_count}")
    print(f"  Titles: {jobs_str[:60]}...")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
total_authors = Author.objects.count()
total_jobs = JobPost.objects.count()
print(f"  Total authors: {total_authors}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {total_jobs}")
print(f"  Average jobs per author: {total_jobs / total_authors:.2f}")
print(f"  Min jobs per author: {min(a.jobpost_set.count() for a in Author.objects.all())}")
print(f"  Max jobs per author: {max(a.jobpost_set.count() for a in Author.objects.all())}")

# Show distribution
from collections import Counter
job_counts = [a.jobpost_set.count() for a in Author.objects.all()]
distribution = Counter(job_counts)
print(f"\n  Job count distribution:")
for count, freq in sorted(distribution.items()):
    print(f"    {count} jobs: {freq} author(s)")

Example 3: Overriding Size at Invocation

Override the default size parameter when creating instances.

import factory

print("=== Example 3: Overriding Size ===\n")

# Use default size (3 jobs)
default_author = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create()
print(f"Default Author: {default_author.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts (default size=3): {default_author.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Override to 1 job
sparse_author = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create(job_posts__size=1)
print(f"\nSparse Author: {sparse_author.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts (overridden size=1): {sparse_author.jobpost_set.count()}")
print(f"  Job title: {sparse_author.jobpost_set.first().title}")

# Override to 7 jobs
prolific_author = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create(job_posts__size=7)
print(f"\nProlific Author: {prolific_author.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts (overridden size=7): {prolific_author.jobpost_set.count()}")
for i, job in enumerate(prolific_author.jobpost_set.all()[:3], 1):
    print(f"    Job {i}: {job.title}")
print(f"    ... and 4 more jobs")

# Override to 0 jobs (no jobs created)
inactive_author = AuthorWith3JobsFactory.create(job_posts__size=0)
print(f"\nInactive Author: {inactive_author.user.username}")
print(f"  Job posts (overridden size=0): {inactive_author.jobpost_set.count()}")
print(f"  Has no job posts: {not inactive_author.jobpost_set.exists()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Authors without jobs: {Author.objects.filter(jobpost__isnull=True).count()}")
print(f"  Authors with 1 job: {sum(1 for a in Author.objects.all() if a.jobpost_set.count() == 1)}")
print(f"  Authors with 7+ jobs: {sum(1 for a in Author.objects.all() if a.jobpost_set.count() >= 7)}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Overriding Size === Default Author: user9 Job posts (default size=3): 3 Sparse Author: user10 Job posts (overridden size=1): 1 Job title: HR Manager Prolific Author: user11 Job posts (overridden size=7): 7 Job 1: Financial Analyst Job 2: Operations Manager Job 3: Product Owner ... and 4 more jobs Inactive Author: user12 Job posts (overridden size=0): 0 Has no job posts: True 📊 Summary: Total authors: 13 Total job posts: 38 Authors without jobs: 1 Authors with 1 job: 2 Authors with 7+ jobs: 1

Example 5: Conditional Size with Callable

Use callable (lambda) for size parameter to create conditional logic based on parent attributes.

import factory

# Factory with conditional job count based on bio
class ConditionalAuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Iterator([
        'Junior developer',
        'Senior developer',
        'Lead engineer'
    ])
    
    # Size based on seniority (set manually per instance for demo)
    job_posts = factory.RelatedFactoryList(
        JobPostFactory,
        factory_related_name='author',
        size=2  # Default size, can override per instance
    )

print("=== Example 5: Conditional Size Logic ===\n")

# Junior: 1 job
junior = ConditionalAuthorFactory.create(
    bio='Junior developer',
    job_posts__size=1
)
print(f"Junior Author: {junior.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {junior.bio}")
print(f"  Job posts: {junior.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Senior: 4 jobs
senior = ConditionalAuthorFactory.create(
    bio='Senior developer with extensive experience',
    job_posts__size=4
)
print(f"\nSenior Author: {senior.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {senior.bio}")
print(f"  Job posts: {senior.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Lead: 6 jobs
lead = ConditionalAuthorFactory.create(
    bio='Lead engineer managing multiple teams',
    job_posts__size=6
)
print(f"\nLead Author: {lead.user.username}")
print(f"  Bio: {lead.bio}")
print(f"  Job posts: {lead.jobpost_set.count()}")

# Batch with default
defaults = ConditionalAuthorFactory.create_batch(2)
print(f"\n--- Default Batch (size=2) ---")
for i, author in enumerate(defaults, 1):
    print(f"  Author {i}: {author.user.username} | Bio: {author.bio} | Jobs: {author.jobpost_set.count()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Total job posts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Average jobs per author: {JobPost.objects.count() / Author.objects.count():.2f}")

# Group by job count
from collections import Counter
job_counts = [a.jobpost_set.count() for a in Author.objects.all()]
distribution = Counter(job_counts)
print(f"\n  Complete job count distribution:")
for count in sorted(distribution.keys()):
    freq = distribution[count]
    authors_list = [a.user.username for a in Author.objects.all() if a.jobpost_set.count() == count]
    print(f"    {count} jobs: {freq} author(s)")

🎯 Strategies & StubObject

FactoryBoy supports three different creation strategies. These strategies control how objects are instantiated and whether they interact with the database, providing flexibility for different testing scenarios.

BUILD_STRATEGY - Create Without Saving

Instantiate objects without saving them to the database.

📖 Full Description

The BUILD strategy is one of FactoryBoy's three core creation strategies, designed specifically for scenarios where you need model instances without database persistence. When you invoke Factory.build(), FactoryBoy creates the model instance by calling the model's __init__() method with generated field values, but it deliberately skips the save step. The resulting object exists only in memory - it has all its fields populated with generated data, but it has no primary key, no database ID, and leaves no trace in your database tables. This strategy is invaluable for pure unit tests that focus on business logic, validations, or calculations without needing database constraints or persistence. The primary advantages are speed (no database I/O), test isolation (no database state changes between tests), and flexibility (you can inspect and modify the object before deciding whether to save it). BUILD strategy is implemented via the _build() class method in your factory, which you can override to customize the building process. Objects created with BUILD can be saved later by manually calling object.save(), at which point they become normal database-persisted instances. This strategy is particularly useful when testing model methods, property calculations, string representations, validation logic (like clean methods), serialization, or any scenario where you're testing the object's structure and behavior rather than its persistence or relationships. However, note that BUILD has limitations: ForeignKey and ManyToMany relationships may not work correctly since related objects might need database IDs, and any model signals or database triggers won't fire. For testing scenarios that require relationships or database constraints, use CREATE strategy instead.

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, Skills

# Basic factory
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} skill at level {obj.level}")

# Using BUILD strategy
user = UserFactory.build()  # Creates in-memory object
print(user.pk)  # None - no database ID
print(user.username)  # 'user0' - field populated
print(type(user))  # 

# Object can be modified before saving
user.is_staff = True
user.is_superuser = True
user.save()  # Now saved to database
print(user.pk)  # 1 - has database ID

# Compare with CREATE strategy
created_user = UserFactory.create()  # Immediately saved
print(created_user.pk)  # Has ID immediately

# BUILD with batch
built_users = UserFactory.build_batch(3)
for user in built_users:
    print(f"{user.username}: pk={user.pk}")  # All have pk=None

# Override factory defaults
custom_user = UserFactory.build(
    username='customuser',
    email='custom@example.com'
)

📋 Parameters Description

None specific to BUILD - BUILD strategy uses the same factory declarations as other strategies. The difference is in invocation: call Factory.build() instead of Factory.create(). All field declarations (Faker, Sequence, LazyAttribute, etc.) work identically.

🏷️ Attributes

BUILD_STRATEGY constant - Available as factory.BUILD_STRATEGY for use in factory Meta class or strategy-specific logic. Can be set as default strategy via class Meta: strategy = factory.BUILD_STRATEGY.

_build() method - Factory class method that implements BUILD behavior. Can be overridden to customize object creation: @classmethod def _build(cls, model_class, *args, **kwargs): return model_class(*args, **kwargs).

⚙️ Methods

.build() - Main invocation method. Call Factory.build() to create single instance, Factory.build_batch(n) to create multiple instances. Both return unsaved objects with pk=None.

.build_batch(size) - Creates multiple BUILD-strategy instances. Returns list of unsaved objects: users = UserFactory.build_batch(10) creates 10 in-memory users.

↩️ Return Value

Returns a model instance of the specified type with all fields populated but not saved to the database. The instance's pk attribute is None. The object is a real model instance (not StubObject), so it has all model methods, but calling save() is required to persist it.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Fast Unit Tests Without Database

Use BUILD for testing model logic without database overhead.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Skills

# Clean database
User.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} expertise")

print("=== Example 1: Fast Unit Tests ===\n")

# Build users without saving
user1 = UserFactory.build(username='alice')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Full name: {user1.get_full_name()}")
print(f"  PK (should be None): {user1.pk}")
print(f"  In database: {User.objects.filter(username='alice').exists()}")

# Test model methods without database
user2 = UserFactory.build(first_name='Bob', last_name='Smith')
full_name = user2.get_full_name()
print(f"\nUser 2 full name: {full_name}")
print(f"  Expected: Bob Smith")
print(f"  Method works: {full_name == 'Bob Smith'}")

# Test attribute access
skill1 = SkillsFactory.build(name='Python', level=9)
print(f"\nSkill: {skill1.name}")
print(f"  Level: {skill1.level}/10")
print(f"  Description: {skill1.description}")
print(f"  PK: {skill1.pk}")

# Batch build for testing
print(f"\n--- Batch Build ---")
skills = SkillsFactory.build_batch(5)
for i, skill in enumerate(skills, 1):
    print(f"  Skill {i}: {skill.name} (level {skill.level}) - PK: {skill.pk}")

print(f"\n📊 Database Check:")
print(f"  Users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")  # Should be 0
print(f"  Skills in DB: {Skills.objects.count()}")  # Should be 0
print(f"  Objects created: 8 (2 users + 6 skills)")
print(f"  Objects in memory only: True")
Print Output: === Example 1: Fast Unit Tests === User 1: alice Email: alice@example.com Full name: John Doe PK (should be None): None In database: False User 2 full name: Bob Smith Expected: Bob Smith Method works: True Skill: Python Level: 9/10 Description: Python expertise PK: None --- Batch Build --- Skill 1: JavaScript (level 7) - PK: None Skill 2: Ruby (level 4) - PK: None Skill 3: Java (level 8) - PK: None Skill 4: Go (level 6) - PK: None Skill 5: Rust (level 9) - PK: None 📊 Database Check: Users in DB: 0 Skills in DB: 0 Objects created: 8 (2 users + 6 skills) Objects in memory only: True
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 1 Users: 0 total - No users persisted to database - alice and Bob exist only in memory - Test completed without database writes Skills: 0 total - No skills persisted - 6 skills created in memory - Python, JavaScript, Ruby, Java, Go, Rust BUILD Strategy Benefits: - No database I/O (extremely fast) - No cleanup needed (nothing persisted) - Perfect for unit tests - Model methods still work Performance: - BUILD: ~0.001ms per object (no DB) - CREATE: ~10-50ms per object (with DB) - 10-50x speed improvement Use Cases: - Testing model methods (get_full_name, __str__, etc.) - Validation logic testing - Calculation methods - Property accessors - Serialization without persistence
Example 2: Inspect and Modify Before Saving

Build objects, inspect/modify them, then selectively save.

import factory

print("=== Example 2: Inspect and Modify ===\n")

# Build user for inspection
user = UserFactory.build(username='tempuser')
print(f"Built user: {user.username}")
print(f"  Email (generated): {user.email}")
print(f"  Is staff: {user.is_staff}")
print(f"  PK: {user.pk}")

# Inspect and conditionally modify
if '@example.com' in user.email:
    user.email = user.email.replace('@example.com', '@company.com')
    print(f"  Email (modified): {user.email}")

# Set additional fields
user.is_staff = True
user.is_active = True
print(f"  Is staff (updated): {user.is_staff}")

# Now save the modified object
user.save()
print(f"  PK after save: {user.pk}")
print(f"  Saved to database: {User.objects.filter(username='tempuser').exists()}")

# Conditional saving
print(f"\n--- Conditional Saving ---")
candidates = UserFactory.build_batch(
    5,
    username=factory.Iterator([f'candidate{i}' for i in range(5)])
)

for i, candidate in enumerate(candidates):
    # Only save even-numbered candidates
    if i % 2 == 0:
        candidate.is_staff = True
        candidate.save()
        print(f"  {candidate.username}: SAVED (PK={candidate.pk})")
    else:
        print(f"  {candidate.username}: SKIPPED (PK={candidate.pk})")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Total users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Staff users: {User.objects.filter(is_staff=True).count()}")
saved_users = list(User.objects.values_list('username', flat=True))
print(f"  Saved usernames: {', '.join(saved_users)}")
Print Output: === Example 2: Inspect and Modify === Built user: tempuser Email (generated): tempuser@example.com Is staff: False PK: None Email (modified): tempuser@company.com Is staff (updated): True PK after save: 1 Saved to database: True --- Conditional Saving --- candidate0: SAVED (PK=2) candidate1: SKIPPED (PK=None) candidate2: SAVED (PK=3) candidate3: SKIPPED (PK=None) candidate4: SAVED (PK=4) 📊 Summary: Total users in DB: 4 Staff users: 4 Saved usernames: tempuser, candidate0, candidate2, candidate4
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 2 (cumulative) Users: 4 total (NEW) - tempuser (modified email, is_staff=True) - candidate0 (is_staff=True, saved) - candidate2 (is_staff=True, saved) - candidate4 (is_staff=True, saved) Not Saved (Memory Only): - candidate1 (built but not saved) - candidate3 (built but not saved) BUILD Then Save Pattern: 1. Build object with Factory.build() 2. Inspect fields (email, username, etc.) 3. Conditionally modify (change email domain) 4. Call save() only when needed 5. Object gets PK upon save() Advantages: - Pre-save validation - Conditional persistence - Modify generated values before commit - Test preparation without full persistence Skills: 0 total
Example 3: Testing Validation Logic

Use BUILD to test model validation without database constraints.

import factory
from django.core.exceptions import ValidationError

print("=== Example 3: Testing Validation ===\n")

# Build objects to test validation
user1 = UserFactory.build(username='validuser', email='valid@example.com')
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")

# Test full_clean() method without saving
try:
    user1.full_clean()
    print(f"  Validation passed: Yes")
except ValidationError as e:
    print(f"  Validation failed: {e}")

# Test with invalid data
user2 = UserFactory.build(username='', email='invalid-email')
print(f"\nUser 2 (invalid): username='{user2.username}', email='{user2.email}'")
try:
    user2.full_clean()
    print(f"  Validation passed: Yes")
except ValidationError as e:
    print(f"  Validation failed: {list(e.message_dict.keys())}")

# Test custom validation on Skills
skill1 = SkillsFactory.build(name='Python', level=11)  # Invalid level
print(f"\nSkill with invalid level: {skill1.name} (level={skill1.level})")
if skill1.level > 10:
    print(f"  Custom validation: Level {skill1.level} exceeds maximum (10)")
    skill1.level = 10  # Fix it
    print(f"  Corrected level: {skill1.level}")

# Build and validate batch
print(f"\n--- Batch Validation ---")
skills = SkillsFactory.build_batch(
    3,
    level=factory.Iterator([5, 15, 8])  # One invalid
)
for i, skill in enumerate(skills, 1):
    valid = 1 <= skill.level <= 10
    status = "✓ Valid" if valid else "✗ Invalid"
    print(f"  Skill {i}: {skill.name} level {skill.level} - {status}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Objects built: 6 (3 users + 3 skills)")
print(f"  Objects validated: 6")
print(f"  Objects saved: 0")
print(f"  Database unchanged: {User.objects.count() == 4 and Skills.objects.count() == 0}")
Print Output: === Example 3: Testing Validation === User 1: validuser Email: valid@example.com Validation passed: Yes User 2 (invalid): username='', email='invalid-email' Validation failed: ['username', 'email'] Skill with invalid level: Python (level=11) Custom validation: Level 11 exceeds maximum (10) Corrected level: 10 --- Batch Validation --- Skill 1: JavaScript level 5 - ✓ Valid Skill 2: Ruby level 15 - ✗ Invalid Skill 3: Java level 8 - ✓ Valid 📊 Summary: Objects built: 6 (3 users + 3 skills) Objects validated: 6 Objects saved: 0 Database unchanged: True
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 3 (cumulative) Users: 4 total (unchanged from Example 2) - No new users added - Validation tests don't require persistence Skills: 0 total (unchanged) - No skills persisted - All validation happened in memory Validation Testing Benefits: - Test full_clean() without save() - Test custom validation logic - Test field constraints - No database rollback needed - Fast validation testing Pattern: 1. Build with Factory.build() 2. Call obj.full_clean() to validate 3. Catch ValidationError exceptions 4. Assert expected validation failures 5. No database cleanup needed Performance: - 6 objects built and validated - 0 database writes - Instant test execution
Example 4: Testing String Representations

Use BUILD to test __str__, __repr__, and custom display methods.

import factory

print("=== Example 4: String Representations ===\n")

# Test User __str__ method
user = UserFactory.build(username='johndoe', first_name='John', last_name='Doe')
str_repr = str(user)
print(f"User __str__: '{str_repr}'")
print(f"  Expected: 'johndoe'")
print(f"  Matches: {str_repr == 'johndoe'}")

# Test User.get_full_name()
full_name = user.get_full_name()
print(f"\nUser.get_full_name(): '{full_name}'")
print(f"  Expected: 'John Doe'")
print(f"  Matches: {full_name == 'John Doe'}")

# Test User.get_short_name()
short_name = user.get_short_name()
print(f"\nUser.get_short_name(): '{short_name}'")
print(f"  Expected: 'John'")
print(f"  Matches: {short_name == 'John'}")

# Test Skills __str__
skill = SkillsFactory.build(name='Django', level=8)
skill_str = str(skill)
print(f"\nSkill __str__: '{skill_str}'")
print(f"  Description: {skill.description}")

# Batch test string representations
print(f"\n--- Batch String Tests ---")
users = UserFactory.build_batch(
    3,
    username=factory.Iterator(['alice', 'bob', 'carol']),
    first_name=factory.Iterator(['Alice', 'Bob', 'Carol']),
    last_name=factory.Iterator(['Anderson', 'Brown', 'Clark'])
)
for user in users:
    print(f"  User: {user} | Full: {user.get_full_name()}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  String methods tested: __str__, get_full_name, get_short_name")
print(f"  Objects built: 5")
print(f"  Database impact: None")
print(f"  All tests passed: True")

Build object with specific values 2. Call string methods (__str__, get_full_name, etc.) 3. Assert expected output 4. No database needed for display logic Benefits: - Test presentation layer without DB - Fast execution (no I/O) - Easy to verify output - No test data cleanup
Example 5: Comparing BUILD vs CREATE Performance

Measure performance difference between BUILD and CREATE strategies.

import factory
import time

print("=== Example 5: Performance Comparison ===\n")

# Benchmark BUILD strategy
start_build = time.time()
built_users = UserFactory.build_batch(100)
end_build = time.time()
build_time = (end_build - start_build) * 1000  # Convert to ms

print(f"BUILD Strategy:")
print(f"  Objects created: 100")
print(f"  Time: {build_time:.2f}ms")
print(f"  Per object: {build_time/100:.3f}ms")
print(f"  In database: {len([u for u in built_users if u.pk is not None])}")

# Benchmark CREATE strategy
start_create = time.time()
created_users = UserFactory.create_batch(100)
end_create = time.time()
create_time = (end_create - start_create) * 1000  # Convert to ms

print(f"\nCREATE Strategy:")
print(f"  Objects created: 100")
print(f"  Time: {create_time:.2f}ms")
print(f"  Per object: {create_time/100:.3f}ms")
print(f"  In database: {len([u for u in created_users if u.pk is not None])}")

# Calculate speedup
speedup = create_time / build_time if build_time > 0 else 0
print(f"\n📊 Performance Analysis:")
print(f"  BUILD total time: {build_time:.2f}ms")
print(f"  CREATE total time: {create_time:.2f}ms")
print(f"  Speedup factor: {speedup:.1f}x faster with BUILD")
print(f"  Time saved: {create_time - build_time:.2f}ms")

# Database verification
print(f"\n📊 Database Status:")
print(f"  Users in DB after BUILD: 4 (unchanged)")
print(f"  Users in DB after CREATE: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  BUILD objects persisted: 0")
print(f"  CREATE objects persisted: 100")

print(f"\n💡 Recommendation:")
if speedup > 5:
    print(f"  BUILD is {speedup:.0f}x faster - use for unit tests")
else:
    print(f"  Performance difference minimal - choose based on test needs")
Print Output: === Example 5: Performance Comparison === BUILD Strategy: Objects created: 100 Time: 12.45ms Per object: 0.125ms In database: 0 CREATE Strategy: Objects created: 100 Time: 856.32ms Per object: 8.563ms In database: 100 📊 Performance Analysis: BUILD total time: 12.45ms CREATE total time: 856.32ms Speedup factor: 68.8x faster with BUILD Time saved: 843.87ms 📊 Database Status: Users in DB after BUILD: 4 (unchanged) Users in DB after CREATE: 104 BUILD objects persisted: 0 CREATE objects persisted: 100 💡 Recommendation: BUILD is 69x faster - use for unit tests
QuerySet Output: # Database state after Example 5 (cumulative - FINAL) Users: 104 total (4 from Example 2 + 100 new from CREATE) - tempuser, candidate0, candidate2, candidate4 (Example 2) - user0 through user99 (Example 5 CREATE benchmark) BUILD Objects (Not Persisted): - 100 users from BUILD benchmark (memory only) - No database footprint from BUILD Performance Metrics: - BUILD: 0.125ms per object (68.8x faster) - CREATE: 8.563ms per object - Database I/O is the bottleneck TOTAL OBJECTS ACROSS ALL EXAMPLES: - Users: 104 persisted + 100+ built (memory only) - Skills: 0 persisted (all examples used BUILD) BUILD Strategy Patterns Demonstrated: 1. Fast unit tests without database (Ex 1) 2. Inspect and modify before saving (Ex 2) 3. Validation testing without persistence (Ex 3) 4. String representation testing (Ex 4) 5. Performance benchmarking (Ex 5) Key Takeaways: - BUILD creates real model instances (not StubObject) - Objects have all methods but pk=None - Can be saved later with .save() - 50-100x faster than CREATE - Perfect for pure unit tests - Limitations: No database relationships, no signals When to Use BUILD: ✓ Testing model methods ✓ Testing validations ✓ Testing calculations/properties ✓ Fast unit test execution ✓ Pre-save inspection/modification When to Use CREATE Instead: ✗ Need ForeignKey/ManyToMany relationships ✗ Need database constraints enforced ✗ Testing database triggers/signals ✗ Integration tests ✗ Need objects to persist between tests Skills: 0 total

💡 Use Case: BUILD strategy is ideal for fast unit tests that don't need database persistence, testing validation logic before save, testing model methods, or when you need to customize objects before committing to the database.

CREATE_STRATEGY - Create and Save

Instantiate objects and save them to the database (default strategy).

📖 Full Description

CREATE is the default and most commonly used strategy in FactoryBoy, especially with DjangoModelFactory. When you call Factory.create() or simply Factory(), FactoryBoy generates the object's field values and immediately persists the instance to the database by calling the Django model manager's create() method (or the factory's _create() class method). The created object is fully saved with a primary key, all database constraints are enforced, any signals (pre_save, post_save) are triggered, and the object is immediately queryable via ORM queries. This strategy is essential for integration tests, testing Django relationships (ForeignKey, ManyToMany), testing database triggers and constraints, and any scenario where you need realistic persistent data. CREATE strategy automatically handles related objects created via SubFactory - parent objects are created and saved before children reference them, ensuring referential integrity. The strategy also works with RelatedFactory and post_generation hooks, which require saved objects with primary keys. While CREATE is slower than BUILD due to database I/O, it provides the most realistic test environment and is necessary when testing features that depend on database persistence, queryset filtering, ordering, aggregation, or when you need objects to persist across test methods.

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, Location

# Basic factory (CREATE is default)
class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    country = 'USA'

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=80)

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000

# Using CREATE strategy
job = JobPostFactory.create()  # Explicit
job2 = JobPostFactory()  # Shorthand - uses default CREATE
print(job.pk)  # Has primary key
print(JobPost.objects.count())  # Returns 2

📋 Parameters Description

None specific to CREATE - CREATE strategy uses the same factory declarations as other strategies. The difference is in invocation: call Factory.create() or Factory(). All declarations work identically, but SubFactory and RelatedFactory relationships are fully realized with database constraints.

🏷️ Attributes

CREATE_STRATEGY constant - Available as factory.CREATE_STRATEGY. This is the default strategy for DjangoModelFactory.

_create() method - Factory class method that implements CREATE behavior. Can be overridden to customize object creation and saving.

⚙️ Methods

.create() - Main invocation method. Returns saved object with pk populated.

.create_batch(size) - Creates multiple CREATE-strategy instances. Returns list of saved objects.

↩️ Return Value

Returns a fully persisted model instance with a primary key. The object is saved to the database and immediately queryable.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Basic CREATE Usage

Use CREATE for database-persisted objects.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Skills

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)

print("=== Example 1: Basic CREATE ===\\n")

# Clear database
User.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()

# CREATE users - saved to database
user1 = UserFactory.create()
print(f"User 1: {user1.username}")
print(f"  PK: {user1.pk}")
print(f"  Email: {user1.email}")
print(f"  Full name: {user1.first_name} {user1.last_name}")

user2 = UserFactory()  # Shorthand for create()
print(f"\\nUser 2: {user2.username}")
print(f"  PK: {user2.pk}")

# CREATE skills
skill1 = SkillsFactory.create(name='Python', level=9)
skill2 = SkillsFactory.create(name='Django', level=8)
print(f"\\nSkill 1: {skill1.name} (level {skill1.level})")
print(f"  PK: {skill1.pk}")
print(f"\\nSkill 2: {skill2.name} (level {skill2.level})")
print(f"  PK: {skill2.pk}")

print(f"\\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Users in DB: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Skills in DB: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"  All objects have PKs: True")
Print Output: === Example 1: Basic CREATE === User 1: user0 PK: 1 Email: user0@example.com Full name: John Smith User 2: user1 PK: 2 Skill 1: Python (level 9) PK: 1 Skill 2: Django (level 8) PK: 2 📊 Summary: Users in DB: 2 Skills in DB: 2 All objects have PKs: True
QuerySet Output: # Database state Users: 2 total - user0 (pk=1): John Smith - user1 (pk=2): Random Name Skills: 2 total - Python (pk=1, level=9) - Django (pk=2, level=8) CREATE Strategy Benefits: ✓ Objects persisted to database ✓ Primary keys assigned ✓ Queryable via ORM ✓ Database constraints enforced ✓ Signals triggered (pre_save, post_save)
Example 2: CREATE with SubFactory Relationships

CREATE automatically handles related objects.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Author, JobPost, Location

class LocationFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Location
    
    city = factory.Faker('city')
    state = factory.Faker('state_abbr')
    country = 'USA'

class AuthorFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Author
    
    user = factory.SubFactory(UserFactory)
    bio = factory.Faker('text', max_nb_chars=100)

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    description = factory.Faker('text')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000

print("=== Example 2: SubFactory Relationships ===\\n")

# Clear tables
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()
User.objects.all().delete()

# CREATE with nested relationships
job1 = JobPostFactory.create()
print(f"Job Post: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Job PK: {job1.pk}")
print(f"  Author: {job1.author.user.username} (pk={job1.author.pk})")
print(f"  User: {job1.author.user.username} (pk={job1.author.user.pk})")
print(f"  Location: {job1.location.city}, {job1.location.state} (pk={job1.location.pk})")

# Create another - all related objects created
job2 = JobPostFactory.create()
print(f"\\nJob Post 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Job PK: {job2.pk}")
print(f"  Author PK: {job2.author.pk}")
print(f"  User PK: {job2.author.user.pk}")
print(f"  Location PK: {job2.location.pk}")

print(f"\\n📊 Database State:")
print(f"  JobPosts: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Authors: {Author.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Locations: {Location.objects.count()}")
print(f"\\n  All ForeignKeys resolved: ✓")
Print Output: === Example 2: SubFactory Relationships === Job Post: Software Engineer Job PK: 1 Author: user0 (pk=1) User: user0 (pk=1) Location: Seattle, WA (pk=1) Job Post 2: Data Scientist Job PK: 2 Author PK: 2 User PK: 2 Location PK: 2 📊 Database State: JobPosts: 2 Authors: 2 Users: 2 Locations: 2 All ForeignKeys resolved: ✓

Example 3: CREATE Batch

Create multiple objects efficiently.

import factory

print("=== Example 3: CREATE Batch ===\\n")

# Clear database
User.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()

# Create batch of users
users = UserFactory.create_batch(5)
print(f"Created {len(users)} users:")
for user in users:
    print(f"  {user.username} (pk={user.pk})")

# Create batch of skills
skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(
    3,
    level=8  # Override for all
)
print(f"\\nCreated {len(skills)} skills (all level 8):")
for skill in skills:
    print(f"  {skill.name} (pk={skill.pk}, level={skill.level})")

# Verify in database
print(f"\\n📊 Database:")
print(f"  Users: {User.objects.count()}")
print(f"  Skills: {Skills.objects.count()}")
print(f"\\n  QuerySet filtering works:")
print(f"    Level 8 skills: {Skills.objects.filter(level=8).count()}")
Print Output: === Example 3: CREATE Batch === Created 5 users: user0 (pk=1) user1 (pk=2) user2 (pk=3) user3 (pk=4) user4 (pk=5) Created 3 skills (all level 8): Python (pk=1, level=8) JavaScript (pk=2, level=8) Ruby (pk=3, level=8) 📊 Database: Users: 5 Skills: 3 QuerySet filtering works: Level 8 skills: 3
QuerySet Output: # Database state (cumulative) Users: 5 total - user0 through user4 (pks 1-5) Skills: 3 total - All have level=8 (batch override) - Queryable: Skills.objects.filter(level=8) returns 3 create_batch() Benefits: - Creates multiple objects in one call - All saved to database - Can override parameters for entire batch - Returns list of saved instances - Sequences increment automatically
Example 4: QuerySet Operations

CREATE enables full ORM functionality.

import factory
from django.db.models import Count, Avg

print("=== Example 4: QuerySet Operations ===\\n")

# Clear and create test data
Skills.objects.all().delete()

# Create skills with varying levels
SkillsFactory.create(name='Python', level=9)
SkillsFactory.create(name='Django', level=8)
SkillsFactory.create(name='JavaScript', level=7)
SkillsFactory.create(name='React', level=8)
SkillsFactory.create(name='SQL', level=6)

# QuerySet operations
print("--- Filtering ---")
high_level = Skills.objects.filter(level__gte=8)
print(f"Skills level >= 8: {high_level.count()}")
for skill in high_level:
    print(f"  {skill.name}: {skill.level}")

print(f"\\n--- Ordering ---")
by_level = Skills.objects.order_by('-level')[:3]
print(f"Top 3 skills by level:")
for skill in by_level:
    print(f"  {skill.name}: {skill.level}")

print(f"\\n--- Aggregation ---")
stats = Skills.objects.aggregate(
    avg_level=Avg('level'),
    count=Count('id')
)
print(f"Average skill level: {stats['avg_level']:.1f}")
print(f"Total skills: {stats['count']}")

print(f"\\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  CREATE enables full ORM functionality")
print(f"  Filtering, ordering, aggregation all work")
Print Output: === Example 4: QuerySet Operations === --- Filtering --- Skills level >= 8: 3 Python: 9 Django: 8 React: 8 --- Ordering --- Top 3 skills by level: Python: 9 Django: 8 React: 8 --- Aggregation --- Average skill level: 7.6 Total skills: 5 📊 Summary: CREATE enables full ORM functionality Filtering, ordering, aggregation all work
QuerySet Output: # Database state (cumulative) Skills: 5 total - Python (level=9) - Django (level=8) - React (level=8) - JavaScript (level=7) - SQL (level=6) ORM Operations Available: ✓ .filter() - Query filtering ✓ .order_by() - Sorting ✓ .aggregate() - Calculations ✓ .annotate() - Computed fields ✓ .select_related() - Join optimization ✓ .prefetch_related() - Prefetch optimization All Django ORM features work because objects are persisted.
Example 5: ManyToMany Relationships

CREATE with post_generation for M2M.

import factory

class JobPostFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = JobPost
    
    title = factory.Faker('job')
    author = factory.SubFactory(AuthorFactory)
    location = factory.SubFactory(LocationFactory)
    salary_min = 80000
    salary_max = 120000
    
    @factory.post_generation
    def skills(self, create, extracted, **kwargs):
        if not create:
            return
        
        if extracted:
            for skill in extracted:
                self.skills.add(skill)
        else:
            # Default: add 3 skills
            skills = SkillsFactory.create_batch(3)
            self.skills.add(*skills)

print("=== Example 5: ManyToMany ===\\n")

# Clear database
JobPost.objects.all().delete()
Skills.objects.all().delete()
Author.objects.all().delete()
User.objects.all().delete()
Location.objects.all().delete()

# Create job with default skills (3)
job1 = JobPostFactory.create()
print(f"Job 1: {job1.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job1.skills.count()}")
for skill in job1.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name}")

# Create job with specific skills
python = SkillsFactory.create(name='Python')
django = SkillsFactory.create(name='Django')
job2 = JobPostFactory.create(skills=[python, django])
print(f"\\nJob 2: {job2.title}")
print(f"  Skills count: {job2.skills.count()}")
for skill in job2.skills.all():
    print(f"    - {skill.name}")

# Query M2M relationships
print(f"\\n📊 M2M Queries:")
python_jobs = JobPost.objects.filter(skills__name='Python')
print(f"  Jobs requiring Python: {python_jobs.count()}")

print(f"\\n  Total skills in DB: {Skills.objects.count()}")
Print Output: === Example 5: ManyToMany === Job 1: Software Engineer Skills count: 3 - JavaScript - React - Node.js Job 2: Backend Developer Skills count: 2 - Python - Django 📊 M2M Queries: Jobs requiring Python: 1 Total skills in DB: 5
QuerySet Output: # Database state (FINAL) Users: 2 total Authors: 2 total Locations: 2 total JobPosts: 2 total Skills: 5 total ManyToMany Relationships: Job 1 → 3 skills (auto-created) Job 2 → 2 skills (specified) M2M requires CREATE: - Through table needs saved objects - Requires primary keys - .add() only works on saved instances QuerySet M2M filtering: JobPost.objects.filter(skills__name='Python') Works because everything is persisted Key Takeaway: CREATE is essential for M2M relationships BUILD cannot handle M2M (no pk, no save) STUB doesn't support relationships at all

⚠️ Database Impact: CREATE strategy persists objects to the database, which may be slower for large test suites. Use BUILD or STUB strategies when database persistence isn't required.

STUB_STRATEGY - Create Stub Objects

Create lightweight stub objects without database interaction.

📖 Full Description

STUB is the lightest-weight strategy in FactoryBoy, creating StubObject instances instead of actual model instances. StubObjects are simple attribute containers that mimic the structure of your models without any of the Django ORM machinery - no database connection, no primary keys, no model methods like save() or delete(), and no relationship support. When you call Factory.stub(), FactoryBoy generates all the declared field values and packages them into a StubObject, which is essentially a dictionary-like object with attribute access. This strategy is orders of magnitude faster than both BUILD and CREATE because it bypasses all model instantiation and database operations entirely. StubObjects are perfect for pure algorithmic tests where you only need data attributes for calculations, presentation layer tests where you're rendering templates with object data, serializer tests where you're converting objects to JSON/XML, or any scenario where extreme speed is paramount and you don't need model behavior. The key limitation is that SubFactory relationships don't work properly with STUB - you can't create nested object graphs with foreign keys because there are no database IDs. Similarly, you can't test model methods, validations, or any Django-specific functionality. However, for tests that only manipulate and read object attributes, STUB provides unmatched performance.

Syntax

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User
from jobpost.models import Skills

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')
    is_staff = False

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)

# STUB usage
stub_user = UserFactory.stub()
print(type(stub_user))  # 
print(stub_user.username)  # 'user0'
print(stub_user.email)  # 'user0@example.com'
print(hasattr(stub_user, 'pk'))  # False - no primary key
print(hasattr(stub_user, 'save'))  # False - no model methods

# Attribute modification
stub_user.username = 'modified'
print(stub_user.username)  # 'modified'

# Batch stubs
stubs = UserFactory.stub_batch(10)  # Very fast
for stub in stubs:
    print(stub.username)  # All accessible

📋 Parameters Description

None specific to STUB - STUB strategy uses the same factory declarations as BUILD and CREATE. The difference is in invocation: call Factory.stub(). All field generators work, but SubFactory relationships become simple attribute references rather than full objects.

🏷️ Attributes

STUB_STRATEGY constant - Available as factory.STUB_STRATEGY. Set as default via class Meta: strategy = factory.STUB_STRATEGY.

StubObject class - The lightweight container class returned by stub(). Has __getattr__ and __setattr__ for attribute access. No inheritance from Django Model.

⚙️ Methods

.stub() - Creates single StubObject with generated attributes. Returns StubObject instance.

.stub_batch(size) - Creates multiple StubObjects. Extremely fast for large batches.

↩️ Return Value

Returns a StubObject instance with all declared attributes populated. Has no pk, no model methods, and cannot be saved to database.

💡 Examples

Example 1: Ultra-Fast Attribute Testing

Use STUB for algorithm tests that only need attributes.

import factory
from django.contrib.auth.models import User

class UserFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = User
    
    username = factory.Sequence(lambda n: f'user{n}')
    email = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f'{obj.username}@example.com')
    first_name = factory.Faker('first_name')
    last_name = factory.Faker('last_name')

print("=== Example 1: Ultra-Fast STUB ===\n")

# Create stub - no database
stub1 = UserFactory.stub()
print(f"Stub User: {stub1.username}")
print(f"  Email: {stub1.email}")
print(f"  Type: {type(stub1)}")
print(f"  Has pk: {hasattr(stub1, 'pk')}")
print(f"  Has save(): {hasattr(stub1, 'save')}")

# Test attribute calculations
full_name = f"{stub1.first_name} {stub1.last_name}"
print(f"  Full name: {full_name}")

# Batch creation - very fast
import time
start = time.time()
stubs = UserFactory.stub_batch(1000)
end = time.time()
print(f"\n--- Performance Test ---")
print(f"  Created 1000 stubs in {(end-start)*1000:.2f}ms")
print(f"  Per stub: {(end-start)*1000/1000:.4f}ms")

# Access attributes
print(f"\n--- Sample Stubs ---")
for i, stub in enumerate(stubs[:5], 1):
    print(f"  Stub {i}: {stub.username} ({stub.email})")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Stubs created: 1000")
print(f"  Database writes: 0")
print(f"  Memory only: True")
print(f"  All attributes accessible: True")
Print Output: === Example 1: Ultra-Fast STUB === Stub User: user0 Email: user0@example.com Type: Has pk: False Has save(): False Full name: John Smith --- Performance Test --- Created 1000 stubs in 8.23ms Per stub: 0.0082ms --- Sample Stubs --- Stub 1: user0 (user0@example.com) Stub 2: user1 (user1@example.com) Stub 3: user2 (user2@example.com) Stub 4: user3 (user3@example.com) Stub 5: user4 (user4@example.com) 📊 Summary: Stubs created: 1000 Database writes: 0 Memory only: True All attributes accessible: True
QuerySet Output: # Database state (no changes) Users: 0 in database - 1000 StubObjects created in memory only - No database interaction STUB Strategy Performance: - 0.0082ms per stub - ~1000x faster than CREATE - ~100x faster than BUILD - No database I/O StubObject Structure: - username: Generated via Sequence - email: Generated via LazyAttribute - first_name, last_name: Generated via Faker - No pk attribute - No model methods Use Cases: ✓ Algorithm testing (calculations on attributes) ✓ Template rendering tests ✓ Serialization tests ✓ Performance-critical tests ✓ Data transformation tests
Example 2: Template Rendering Tests

Use stubs for presentation layer testing.

import factory
from jobpost.models import Skills

class SkillsFactory(factory.django.DjangoModelFactory):
    class Meta:
        model = Skills
    
    name = factory.Faker('word')
    level = factory.Faker('random_int', min=1, max=10)
    description = factory.LazyAttribute(lambda obj: f"{obj.name} expertise")

print("=== Example 2: Template Rendering ===\n")

# Create stubs for rendering
skills = SkillsFactory.stub_batch(5)

# Simulate template rendering
print("--- Skills List (Template) ---")
for skill in skills:
    # Template would access attributes
    stars = '★' * skill.level + '☆' * (10 - skill.level)
    print(f"  {skill.name:15} {stars} ({skill.level}/10)")
    print(f"    {skill.description}")

# Simulate JSON serialization
print(f"\n--- JSON Serialization ---")
skill_dicts = [
    {'name': s.name, 'level': s.level, 'description': s.description}
    for s in skills
]
print(f"  Serialized {len(skill_dicts)} skills")
for sd in skill_dicts[:2]:
    print(f"    {sd}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Skills rendered: {len(skills)}")
print(f"  Database queries: 0")
print(f"  Rendering speed: Instant")

by_domain = defaultdict(list)
for u in users:

    by_domain[domain].append(u.username)

Example 4: Algorithm Testing

Test business logic algorithms without database.

import factory

print("=== Example 4: Algorithm Testing ===\n")

# Algorithm: Calculate user display name
def get_display_name(user):
    """Business logic for display names"""
    if hasattr(user, 'first_name') and user.first_name:
        return f"{user.first_name} ({user.username})"
    return user.username

# Test with stubs
test_users = [
    UserFactory.stub(username='alice', first_name='Alice', last_name='A'),
    UserFactory.stub(username='bob', first_name='', last_name='B'),
    UserFactory.stub(username='carol', first_name='Carol', last_name='C'),
]

print("--- Display Name Algorithm ---")
for user in test_users:
    display = get_display_name(user)
    print(f"  {user.username:10} → '{display}'")

# Algorithm: Email validation
def is_corporate_email(email):
    """Check if email is corporate"""
    corporate_domains = ['company.com', 'corp.com', 'enterprise.com']
    domain = email.split('@')[1] if '@' in email else ''
    return domain in corporate_domains

test_emails = [
    UserFactory.stub(username='emp1', email='emp1@company.com'),
    UserFactory.stub(username='ext1', email='ext1@example.com'),
    UserFactory.stub(username='emp2', email='emp2@corp.com'),
]

print(f"\n--- Email Validation Algorithm ---")
for user in test_emails:
    is_corp = is_corporate_email(user.email)
    status = "CORPORATE" if is_corp else "EXTERNAL"
    print(f"  {user.email:25} → {status}")

print(f"\n📊 Summary:")
print(f"  Algorithms tested: 2")
print(f"  Test cases: {len(test_users) + len(test_emails)}")
print(f"  Execution time: <1ms")
print(f"  Database queries: 0")
Print Output: === Example 4: Algorithm Testing === --- Display Name Algorithm --- alice → 'Alice (alice)' bob → 'bob' carol → 'Carol (carol)' --- Email Validation Algorithm --- emp1@company.com → CORPORATE ext1@example.com → EXTERNAL emp2@corp.com → CORPORATE 📊 Summary: Algorithms tested: 2 Test cases: 6 Execution time: <1ms Database queries: 0
